THE MAN AGAINST THE POST
TO one whose habit of leaning against a
post has become proverbial, the pro-
fession of epigrammatist seems pecu-
liarly appropriate. To lean languidly
against a post and pass remarks upon Aus-
tralia—that weary swagman humping along
the well-worn track of nations his awkward
and tremendous bluqy, a sundowner hope-
fully and paradoxically toiling toward the
sunrise—is a polite occupation that would
especially appeal to the Australian. And the
request for epigrams upon Australia has
stimulated no less than sixty-five post-sup-
porters to hurl remarks at that passing swag-
man. Parenthetically, as “Leonardo”
points out, there is something to be said for
the post-buttressing habit: before the Aus-
tralian could" lean against a post, he had to
put up a post to lean against !
This looks epigrammatic, but obviously is
no epigram. It has the crack of the whip,
but is slovenly handled. “ Leonardo ” takes
nine lines to say what should have been put
in four. He might have got nearer epi-
grammatic terseness if, for instance, he had
put it thus : /
John Bull, in his grandfather’s easy chair,
May laugh at the post that I’m seen against;
But while he was loafing, firm and square
I planted the post that I lean against !
Or, emphasising the contrast, thus :
His toiling fathers built that grey old wall
That John Bull, resting, may be seen against;
’Neath my verandah I care not at all :
I planted first the post I lean against !
J*
On the whole, the trudging swagman has
come well out of that running fire of com-
mentary from the little knot of loungers
against the posts of the pub. verandah.
Occasionally his feelings have been hurt
when someone, as in this epigram by
“ Semicolon,” hurls at the stranger an
unwarrantably heavy brick
Australia is bounded by the Pacific and Indian
Oceans, and unbounded by its own conceit.
But the epigrammatists, while observing
the weaknesses of Australia, do not alto-
gether despair of him. The tolerant point
of view of that group of loungers has been
aptly suvnmed-up by “ Poimena”
I dunno wot’s a Eppy Gram—I dunno that I care ;
It seems no sorter use, as I can see.
I've never left this country, in which I holds a
share.
Orstralian land is goodemiff for me.
Bat as ther Red Rag’s arsked us to do our little
bit ;
And show them other blokes as we’re all rite,
I gives yer my conviction, and I fancy it’ll fit,
Tuat wot. Orstralia wants is— Lots cr Skitc.
Ther Yank's no fool at telling wot ’Murka’s going
to do ;
Ther Britisher can also flute it strong ;
And when them two gets goin’, it makes a feller
blue,
Fer no one squeaks Orstralia's little song.
So Task yer when yer writin’ (and mind yer rub
it in),
Don’t smoodge ’em, if yer are afraid of gore.
Tell all ther tired readers of yer Crimson Bulletin,
Orstralia, is a, land, to barrack fore !
The epigram is evidently a somewhat un-
considered form to Australian critics. Several
• competitors forwarded the qx instead of the
bovril, and handed us a whip that wouldn’t
crack. “Syd. C., : ' “ Erin,” and May Siede
furnish examples of the long-windedness that
is fatal to an epigram. An epigram ought
almost to gasp for breath. May Siede has got
hold of an /idea, but it is not worth eight
lines.
W.H.D. gets nearer the form; but is too
laborious :
But thou, disdaining ancient whims
(The days of miracles are o(er) #
The fetters that once bound thyiimbs
Hast helped to rivet on the Boer.
It takes “Seaweed” eight long lines to
remark that Australians are the owners of a
great white jewel whose setting is insecure ;
while “ Will Fay ” condenses so much that
he forwards a long addenda of elucidation of
his opinion that Asia is the puss to Aus-
tralia’s mouse.
**
And as the sundowner slouches into the
bar he hears a few more comments upon
himself, none of which cause him any con-
cern.
Because Australia is “the devil I know,” for me
its aspect is angelic.
G.S.T.
* * * *
Australia is best appreciated by those who have
been away looking for something better.
G.S.T.
* * * *
Australia, the Land of Promise—when the
Promissory Note period has passed.
Huffin-Guffin.
Australia is a slice of bread, whereof the white
mice have only nibbled at the crust.
Semicolon.
* * # *
Norman Lilley has the epigrammatic habit.
Here is Australia’s pretty apology for wattle-
blossom
Peroxide’s out of date ? . O yes, I know ;
But those poor dear spring poets love it So !
And two others from his batch are clever-.
Summer met a dirt-bin in the backyard of a
suburban hotel; and, after a few days’ stay in an
adjacent room, a traveller returned to London and
wrote poetic essays on “ The Myriad Odors of the
Australian Bush.”
* * * *
“ A poor place, Australia.” says the absentee.
“ I don’t know what it would do without me.”
d*
The swagman has had “his drink with the
men inside, and starts again along the dusty
track that nation after nation has -so hope-
fully taken ; but as he goes he hears behind
him some remarks that maybe lie will ponder
over on his way. For there area few leaners
against posts that have not only something
to sav, but the knowledge how to say it.
“ F.B.” calls after him :—
And, looking at the sundowner from over-
seas, “A.S.” truthfully and smartly ob-
serves : , ■
Australia, the inter alia of British statesman-
ship ! ■
While Thomas Porter, from rain-sodden
Maoriland, unkindly carps :
Australia—kind, sunny, bright nurse for a
young-nation— dry nurse !
And ere he disappears over the dusty edge
of space he hears from “Locust ” this stimu-
lating advice
In the Australian Democracy Caste has had no
place. Let us beware of giving place to the Half-
caste.
«£*
“Yes, yes,” mutter's the old swagman.
“No caste— no half-caste!” and, after a
long pause, “ and no cast-offs ! ” And had
the old man only thought of sending that
"itfcle- remark into The Bulletin office he
might, this day, have been a guinea the
richer. For the man against the post was
too busy polishing the post to polish his
epigram.
And perhaps the swagman did not hear
D. O’Reilly’s jibe : —•
And D. O’Reilly (Sydney Grammar School)
was shouted for by the keeper of the Red
Page Pub.
Yet as the old sundowner goes grimly on
toward the sunrise, he makes one remark to
himself:—
The Australians should think,in continents.
They are the only race with a whole continent
to think in.
Bit nobody but the desert heard him, and
it was only months afterwards, when still
trudging toward the sunrise, lie learned that
he had twice missed that guinea. But the
desert heard him ; for he thought in deserts
and other large things like that. It isn’t
everybody who lias a wiiole desert to think in.
TWO EPIGRAMS ON AUSTRALIA.
The last Cup winner hauls
Her ammunition waggon,
Laden with bats and balls,
Northward —to fight the dragon,
* * * *
Asia's a-swarm !
What then ? — we’ll bump her /
Noble’s in form,
So’s Trumper.
SYDUEY.
D. O’REILLY.
When a critic laughs and points at me,
The “ Cornstalk ” of Australia, he'
Laughs from his grandpa’s easy chair.
He says that all my life I lean
Against a post, and swear.
But let me tell John Bull Gotthelf
That all alone, upon my own,
I dug the hole and cut that pole,
Awl planted it myself.
In history of nations and of men
We find one number enter time again ;
For many Fame has smiled with classic nine ;
But Land of Southern Cross and great Carbine,
Thus far in your short life we fain must draw
Your fame is linked with the number four—
Your heavenly with the four‘stars of the Cross,
Your earthly with the four legs of a “ hoss ” !
In olden days, the cripple cured
Into some chnroh his crutches brought,
And told the pains he had endured
And wha(j a miracle was wrought.
On every side green arms of Ocean toss,
Guarding a land of sunshine and good cheer,
For, though folk speakabouta “ Southern”cross,
We only “ get our wool off” once a year.
E. F. Christie.
Too great to be turned in a small piece of verse,
This land of a great corftradiction ;
One side shows the youngest of lands, the obverse
Dame Nature’s most ancient condition.
Naso.
Australia, beware of the little brown men
Who visit you, courteous and bland :
Though rough in your surface with gully and ridge,
It must never be really Japanned.
A. A. Jones.
The dawning impulse of youth,
Vigorous a’nd bold.
Happy to be young—yet
Eager to be old.
H. E. R.
King Pippin of all Terra’s garden crop,
’Tis seen why she the last man sucks delight from;
Wise fruiterer-like, God placed her first atop—
Man open’d up the barrel at the bottom.
Vulcan.
A feast for gods
Long hidden in a nook,
In Nature’s Banquet Hall,
Uncovered by a Cook.
Drum.
O great Loan Land ! Who would have thought
Your rulers so omniscient ?
For each high post a stranger’s sought,
They deeming for your sons’ support
The lamp-post’s height sufficient.
And Fred Davison’s sarcasm almost makes
him wince
Our habit of a hundred years,
Through which we’ll yet sup sorrow—
“ It should he done, it must be done,
It SHALL be done—to-morrow.”
Asia’s a-swarm !
What then—we’ll bump her !
Noble’s in form,
So’s Trumper.
TWO ENGLISH NOVELS
Cursorily let us glance at two of the “best
.read ” English novels of the day.
England is the only place in the world where
The Far Horizon (Hutchinson and Co., per Angus
and Robertson) could have been written. The
heavy hand of George Eliot is upon the later
English woman novelist: a tradition of sombre
dullness still lingers in the modern “serious”
work, which treats of various brands of religion
and suburban at-homes yvith a portentous serious-
ness that out here, in the sunlight, seems some-
what uncalled for.
Lucas Malet, after writing one good book
(The Wages of Sin), one distressingly clever
book ( Sir Richard Calmpdy), now gives us a
serious and suburban book. The story moves
through solemn, rounded, lethargic periods of
deliberate prose; its hero is an ex-bank clerk,
past fifty, with heart disease—and we have to
wade through fifty pages before we get the
heroine. The story has its delicate woman's
touches, such as the picture of the woman with
the clouded mind, who finds in her endless
making of Irish lace a symbolic meaning, a mystic
significance. Into the intricate designs and end-
less variety of delicate and ingenious stitches,
“she poured forth her soul, as another might
pour it forth in music, finding there an imagina-
tive language far surpassing, in its subtlety of
suggestion, articulate speech. There were deserts
of net, of spider’s-web fineness, to he laboriously
traversed. . . . And there were, to her, vaguely
agitating and even fearful things in this
lace-work also—confusions of outline, broken
purposes, multiplicity of opposing intentions)
Struggle of good and evil powers in the intricacies
of some rich arabesque ;/or monotonous repetitions
of design which distressed her as with the terrors
of imprisonment and of unesuapable fate.”
The Far Horizon is the leisurely novel in which
all the furniture in every room is laboriously and
lovingly described ; perhaps we would not be
wronging Lucas Malet of the later manner if we
described her work in the words used by one of
her suburban characters for her heavy and hybrid
suppers: “ All comfortable and friendly, no cere-
mony and fal-lals, but everything put down on
the table so that you can see it and please your-
self.”
And Poppy, the deliberately indecorous heroine,
whohas a Past, but whose indiscretions in the novel
are confined to frank remarks (wickedly designed
to shock the suburban mind) is, after all, a cha-
racter quite alive. She objects to the fact that
most women that enter into men’s lives have had
so many predecessors. ‘ ‘At best, one comes in a bad
tinrd—more often a bad thrCe-and-twentieth. . . .
They have romantic memories of half-a-dozen
women. . . . One knows all the time that the
whole show’s merely a rechauffe. Visions of
Clara and Gladys, and dear little Emily, and
Riosina, and Beatrice, and the lovely Lucinda—
angels every one of them, if you haven’t seen
them for ten years, and wouldn’t know them again
if you met them in the street—haunt the back-
ground of every man’s mind by the time he’s five-
and-thirty, and cut entrancing capeVs against the
sky-line, so that one finds the actually present
woman, here in the foreground, hasn’t really a
look-in at all.”
Toward the end the novel is thickly larded
over with heavy slabs about religion, and the sus-
pected tendency of the hero towards preaching
breaks out in laborious paragraphs. Still, the
religious novel has always had a wild, unhal-
lowed attraction for the London suburbs. And,
finally, the tale moves with a sombre deliberation
which, after all, is rather like life. But a great
novel ? No. F 2,
Let us turn to something more promising. The
name of the author, William J. Locke, hints at
fantasy and delight. For Locke is always happily
impossible and genially shocking—and he Knows
Woman. It was in The Morals of Marcus
Ordeyne that he made assured his position in the
first ranks of English novelists’; but it is an illus-
tration of the lagging footsteps of liter.iYy recog-
nition that before the appearance of that achieve-
ment Locke had industriously published eight
good novels. The youfig Australian novelist who
wishes to get famous in a hurry might ponder
this depressing fact. In The Morals , Locke drew
a delightful heroine ; as a drama, the story has
been received with enthusiasm. It is one of the
possibilities of the future that here we have
a dramatist who will inject a fresh ichor into the
veins of the moribund English drama. His latest
book, The Beloved Vagabond (John Lane, per
hycnev L wc Ciuh) inis all the happy irresponsi-
bility of Alarcm. It may he classed Fl. it is a
glorification, and sometimes a frank apology, for
vagabondage. His hero is picturesque, sottish,
artistic, dirty, abrucndabrant —in put Quixote,
Cyrano, and Tartarin. It is a comedy of the
artistic temperament. BerzeliusNibbidard Para-
got when he found a resonant name our vaga-
bond made a practice of adopting it—has a mind
that thinks in palaces.
“lam a genius,” said Paragot modestly. “I
used to think in Most men’s palaces
are little buildings written big.--My small build-
ings were palaces reduced. I could have roofed
in the whole of Paris with a dome.”
With this paragon a youth, a dog, and an un-
prepossessing girl wander the world. Paragot
hints to his adoring disciple the cause of his
vagabondage.
“ Well, this young man was clad in purple and
fine linen, and fared sumptuously every day. He
also had valuable furniture. One evening tome-
thing happened to annoy him—a flaw iif what he
had conceived to be the scheme of the universe.
It annoys many people. The young man lieing
annoyed, cast the fruits of his genius into the
tire, tore up his purple and fine linen, and smashed
his furniture with a crusader’s mace which hap-
pened to be hanging by way of an ornament on
the wall. It's made of steel with a knob full of
spikes. I know nothing like it for destroying a
Louis Quinze table, or for knocking the works
out of a clock. If you're good, my son, you shall
have one when you grow up.”
So this “ film full of wind and wonder, fantasy
and folly” is “driven like thistledown about the
world;” and when his reformation comes we
know it cannot last. Paragot is true to himself,
the self that, with a violin, “could rip out the
hearts of a dead Municipal Council.”
“ Paragot drained his tumbler . . . and cleared
his throat, with the contentment of a man whose
thirst has been slaked.
“ ‘ Now one can spit,’ he exclaimed, heartily.
“ ‘That is always a comfort to a man,’ re-
marked the patronne.
“ ‘lt is the potentiality that is the comfort.’”
And with a pleasant memory of the design he
drew for his Palais de Dipsoraanie— in whose
drunken architecture columns stood upside down,
and enormous buttresses supporting nothing
leaned incapable against the building, till the
whole palace looked reeling drunk—we leave
Paragot, a contented father. “Who says that
Berzelius Nibbidard Paragot can’t make things
grow ? 1 was born to it. Nom de Dieu I could
make anything grow. I could plant your
palette and it would dbme up a landscape. And
snere mille cochons , I have done the most
miraculous thing of all. I am the father of a
human being, a real live human being. He is
small as yet,” he added apologetically, “ but still
he is alive. He has teeth, Asticot, It is the
most remarkable thing in this astonishing
universe.” , '
Dr. H. Hensoldt is to he congratulated upon
his pluck. In this materialistic age when Thibet
is no longer a mysterious country, when the
spiritual ruler of that land has paid a visit to
India, when the last refuge of esoteric Budd-
hism has been explored by- military expedi-
tions, and when instead of Mahatmas were found
only ignorance and dirt, Dr. Hensoldt, in his new
magazine, Orient and Occident (Royal Chambers,
Hunter-street, Sydney', 6d.), comes forward with
an astounding interview with the Dalai Lama in
1882. The Dalai Lama, the predecessor of the
one who fled to Mongolia on the approach of the
Younghusband expedition, was then a boy of eight
years of age ; and Dr. Hensoldt naively puts aside
the most obvious explanation why that unfortunate
individual’s career is always suddenly terminated
by death as soon as he shows signs of growing up
—and taking charge. But it is the interview
with this child that excites our admiration of the
editor of Orient and Occident as a writer of sensa-
tional literature. This boy of eight, despite Dr.
Hensoldt’s disguise, promptly addressed him in his
native German, and moreover in a dialect which he
had not heard for many years, and which the child
could not. have acquired by any process known to
ordinary mortals. The explorer’s explanation is
that this miracle was merely a species of mind-
reading, and that thoughts were conveyed with-
out the medium of speech, leaving the language
in which the thoughts were appiirently spoken
entirely to the imagination of the listener. If so,
Dr. Hensoldt is to be congratulated upon his
imagination. But the child god not only read the
explorer’s thoughts but replied to them—before
the doctor could enunciate them—with deep
philosophical learning. We can recommend
Orient and Occident, not only to all believers in
Blavatsky, but to all in search of stimulating
reading.
It seems a pity that, though GhiyasUddin Abul
Fath Omar Ihn Ibrahim al-Khayyami ((letter
known as Omaf Khayyam) died a centenarian, he
did not live long enough to draw his royalties.
For the editions of Fitzgerald’s translation grow
innumerable. The latest is a pleasing reprint
published by T. C. Lothian, Melb., in his artistic
“Nugget ” series, wherein, for ninepence, you get
the wisdom of the old philosopher in the inter-
stices between elaborate color-decorations. Stone’s
Print Shop, Melb., also publishes a neat little
Rubaiyat, appropriately decorated.
“Australia’s Penny Post” is the title of an
article in Feb. World's Work. The author,
“E.J.T.8.,” is somewhat previous. The World s
TForA - is one of the few English magazines that
are worth reading. The most remarkable article
in a varied contents-bill is one upon' “The Con-
quest of the Air.’’-fully illustrated with photo-
graphs of the latest things in air-ships. Ihe
American edition of The World's Work has also
articles of solid interest.
The Bond l of Music (Truslove and Hanson,
Eng.; 2s. 9rf.), daintily printed and bound, is a
little anthology for music-lovers —a collection ol
poems and prose-selections from English litera-
ture dealing with music, well compiled by Duncan
and August Macdougal.

VARIOVS VERSE
[FOR THE BULLETIN.
Eheu Fugaces...Labuntur Anni.
Life’s but a breath
’Twixt Death and Death,
It needs no sooth to say;
The midge that springs
On gauzy wings
Foretells our little day.
Man is a pawn
To be withdrawn
Whenever that God will;
His words and deeds
Are windy reeds
Upon a flame-swept hill.
The grave’s disgrace
Shall mar the face
That glittered here with pride,
The fairest fair
Must soon repair
To be the worm’s white bride..
“ The hanging judge,”
He, too, must trudge
The starless pike to Hell,
To pay the tolls
Of all the souls
He pluckt from prison-cell.
What worth is Fame ?
’Tis but a name
Blown world-wide dustily ;
How slow, how slow
The laurels grow;
How fast the cypress-tree !
N.s.w.
HUGH McCRAE.
Travelling to Latrobe.
As I was travelling to Latrobe, one sunny after-
noon in May,
I met a couple riding
Where the road winds round St. Patrick’s Bay.
A fair-haired girl on a gallant gray
I hat pranced as though proud of his burden gay,
Behind an old farmer who led the way,
A fat bay mare bestriding.
Travelling, travelling to Latrobe,
By the road that runs on the eastern side
Of the Mersey River, where far and wide
It lies like a lake at the full of the tide
As you’re travelling to Latrobe.
As I was travelling to Latrobe, that sunny after-
noon in May,
A wicked mood came o’er me.
I passed the farmer the time o’ day
As he ambled by on his dappled bay,
And smiled and winked in the same old way
At his daughter fair on the gallant gray,
As my father did before me.
As I was travelling to Latrobe, that sunny after-
noon in May,
I could not but adore her.
The farmer passed me the time o’ day
In a gruff and grumpy kind o’ way,
While his daughter fair, on the gallant gray,
Blushed and smiled, as I should say
Her mother did before her.
Travelling, travelling to Latrobe
By the road that runs on the eastern side
Of the Mersey River, where, far and wide,
It lies like a lake at the full of the tide
As you’re travelling to Latrobe.
TASMANIA.
VIVIAN.
My Prayer.
Prayers are not for such as me,
Still I’d mumble one,
Just because it can’t do harm
(All the harm is done).
All I ask from What’s Up There
(Call It what you will—
I don’t want to think it out,
Time’s too short—but still)
All I ask from That Which Is
(Ask —not beg or crave—
I won’t cringe—till afterwards,
’Tother side the grave)—
All I ask is only this :
Let no sunset light
Ling’ring wane when Life's Day ends :
Quick and sharp—the night!
No dulled eye, no sluggard brain,
Palsied, shaking limb,
Fussing friends to flutter round
In a sick-room dim.
While my feet are sure and fleet,
Walking where I like,
Let Fate’s winged arrow fall,
Sharp and silent strike
While my stubborn will’s my own,
While my voice can raise
One mad song to Love and Life
(These alone I’ll praise).
Then—there’s one more thing I want—
One more blessed boon
When it’s done, let it be done—
Whether late or soon.
When my Sun sinks to its rest—
Let it rise no more
On another land, but just
Night for evermore.
QUEENSLAND.
CHAS. E. HOWELL.

Paper
A correspondent asks: “To what other uses besides insulating silos may
GIANT PAPER be put ? ” Although all up-to-date silos are insulated with Giant
Paper that channel is perhaps the smallest in which GIANT PAPER is used. The
walls, ceilings and floors of all the best houses are made air-tight, damp, dust and
insect proof with GIANT PAPER. Refrigerating Chambers, Refrigerator Cars,
Cold Storage Chambers, Cellars, Dairy and Cheese Factories, and Breweries are
usually insulated with GIANT PAPER. In all modern houses and factories
insulation is of vital importance. Perfect insulation can only be secured by an
odorless, thoroughly saturated air-tight, waterproof paper. That paper is
GIANT BUILDING PAPER.

THURSDAY, MARCH 28, 1907.
Pbioe 6d.
This, if you like, is a portrait of
the editor of The Watchman —halo
and all—taken at the moment of
writing ungallant things about the
scarlet young person of Babylon.
And here we have another God-tearing editor—Rev. W. H. Fitchett, of
Life. Mr. Fitchett does not wear his halo during business hours. He thinks
i- conduces to baldness. It will be seen in the upper right-hand corner of
the picture, hanging on a nail.
JOURNALISM IN THE NEVER-NEVER.—Behold here the “brains” of the
Back-Blox Thunderer. His back Is toward us, bat it is easy to see by his
strenuous attitude that he is rubbing up the Kaiser or the Czar the wrong way.
As an interesting companion picture to the last, is a busy day in the sans*
turn of a “ Qreat Daily.” Editor Ward’s whiskers have many admirers.
SOME OF OUR GREAT EDITORS.-A PEEP BEHIND THE SCENES.

to he addressed to William Macleod, Managing Director.
It out to Address Betters.
Correspondents to The Bulletin ivould oblige the paper
(and, to an extent, themselves) by taking some care as to
methods of address.
Business letters (with regard to subscriptions, advertise-
ments, etc.) should in no case be addressed to the Editor,
but to the Manager. To send them to the Editor is to
invite inattention.
It is not a good thing to address editorial letters to
anyone by name. “ The Editor, Bulletin,” is the proper
superscription for “ copy.” Delay, and even loss, is
sometimes caused by addressing to persons instead of
officials. The person may not be there ; the official
always is.
Special Notice.
Insufficiently stamped letters are liable to be refused.
The contents of this paper are copyright by the pub-
lishers. The right to reproduce or translate any part of
the contents of this number is hereby expressly reserved by
the publishers.
The Editor will carefully read and, where practicable,
acknowledge in the “ Correspondence ” column all contri-
butions submitted, whether in the form of Political,
Social, or other Articles, Verse, Short Tales or Sketches
(those dealing with Australian subjects, and not exceeding
two columns in length, or, say, 3000 ivords, are specially
acceptable), Paragraphs, Letters, or Newspaper Clippings.
All communications will be regarded as strictly confi-
dential.
The Bulletin will return all unsuitable MS. when the
address is specified and stamps are enclosed But no
MS. 'will be returned, or preserved, unless these conditions
are observed. When MS. is fonvarded without stamps
for return it will be understood that the copy is to be
destroyed if not acceptable. Personal applications for
return of MS. cannot receive attention. Tire Bulletin
desires, further, those sending MS. to distinctly under-
stand that it is not responsible for the preservation or
transmission of MS.

THURSDAY. MARCH 28, 1907.
HOW PILATE, THE DAGO, WASHED
HIS HANDS.
To-morrow is the anniversary—nearly the 1900th
anniversary—of a day which made more history
than any other in the world’s record. Jerusalem
was Italian territory in those times, and the proud
and insolent Dago legions clanked around in their
hardware, and their pomp, and their Sunday
clothes, and looked down with bloated arrogance
on all and sundry. All Dagodom looked down
then with arrogance on all and sundry—save on the
German occasionally and the Parthian now and
then. Pontius Pilate, the Italian, whom the
quaint Ethiopian church afterwards made into a
saint, was mandarin, or procurator, or chief robber
of Judea, and he seems to have been the true Italian
of the period, with a monstrous pride, and a paw
like Behemoth for backsheesh, and the glassy eye
of contempt for everything except Caesar and
Caesar’s minions— Sejanus and his kind. Close
on nineteen centuries ago this typical representa-
tive of Italy sat up listlessly in his chair
of state to listen to an accusation, and
but for that event his name would probably
have disappeared from history as a heavy thing
of no importance —say a disused anvil—disappears
in deep water. The Christ had innumerable
friends—mostly Jews. He also had active and
virulent enemies, mostly Jews; their enmity
was owing to unutterable disappointment when
they found that the Prince of the House of
David was no warrior after all, and had not
shifted the unspeakable dead weight of Italian
tyranny by so much as a hair. There is no
evidence that He had one friend among the
Italian ruling caste, and the fact that He
had not, apparently, in that same caste one
very active enemy seemed to be owing rather
to the gigantic superciliousness of the Dago of
those days than to any sympathy or kindliness of
heart. The Italian soldiery scourged Him and
plaited His crown of thorns; the Italian authority
passed the death sentence ; the Italian legionaries
did the Crucifixion and stole the victim’s gar-
ment ; the Italian troopers watched, with un-
friendly intent, at the sepulchre where two
kindly Jews had given the body decent burial.
Italy has struggled hard to forget these things
ever since, and has sought comfort in the thought
that Pilate, the perfunctory Italian, washed his
hands even as he passed sentence, and de-
clared that he took none of the responsibility
of his position—only the pomp, and the salary,
and anything he could steal. But there was no
calling out of the clanking legions to suppress the
petty local disturbance which clamored for a
death sentence; only the spectacle of a lazy
oppressor washing his hands, which he would
probably have washed about that time of day in
any case. And the Tiber has been muddy ever
since, and the lagoons of Venice persistently silt
up with what looks like the debris of the biggest
and most futile wash in the world’s history.
* * • *
And ever since then, Jew and Italian have
been busy shifting the responsibility back and
forward, to and fro, this way and that; and
the Italian has been the louder of the pair,
and has gesticulated more furiously than the
other man, and so has got more of an audience.
The converted Jews who adopted the gospel of
the new Teacher have long since vanished from
history. They sank out of sight amid the
adjacent populations, and where they are now
no man can really tell. The unconverted
Jews were scattered in the great cataclysm
which swallowed up Jerusalem, but in that
respect they had no worse luck than Tyre,
Sidon, Carthage, Assyria, or a score of other
obliterated nations. And in the amazing manner
in which they were able to preserve their
nationality, they had better fortune than most
other conquered races in that gory period of
Italian domination ; also, they had better fortune
than their converted and obliterated brethren.
It was a time when small nations, whether they
were supposed to be specially accursed of Heaven
or not, met with much the same fortune; the
only difference lay in the amazing Hebrew courage
and vitality, which kept the Jewish race above
ground when so many others went below. If
the preservation of a race is a matter of good
fortune, then the Jew, with all his disasters,
has been blessed above most men of his time
—above the Greek, who is now represented
by a mongrel interloping brigand who sells dirty
currants and calls himself Greek; or the Piet,
who isn’t visibly represented by anything; or
the aboriginal Briton ; or the original Irishman
who lingers doubtfully in Connaught and there-
abouts. Meanwhile Italy, with its share of the
curse dragging at its heels, went its way to
destruction. It beheaded the Apostles, and
burned the converts. It became a monstrous
mass of corruption without losing one whit of its
arrogance, or diminishing in the least the glassy
eye of its superciliousness. It was more or less
converted in course of time; but conversion, in its
case, seemed to be merely varnish poured on a
corpse ; for its new Christianity, so far as all the
evidence goes, made it not one whit less corrupt
than its old heathenism, and did not stay by one
second its downward progress. From the time of
Pilate hardly a gleam of luck came in the way
of the Italian empire. It became the Mrs.
Sicewton (Dickens’ Mrs. Skewton) among nations
—old, frisky, shrill, painted, and struggling to
impress the threatening young races with tl e
glitter of its false teeth and the hostile look
of its artificial eye. Probably even the
dispersion of the Jews was hardly a cir-
cumstance compared with the dispersion of
the old dominant Italian people. The men went
to the frontiers of the empire to hold back the
barbarians, and didn’t return. The cavity thus
created at the heart of the empire was filled by
niggers, Asiatics, Greeks, Egyptians, miscellaneous
foreigners, eunuchs, hairdressers, and dancing
bears. A time came when almost everybody
could be found in Rome except the Romans. The
empire itself grabbed its crown and bolted to
Byzantium, and large-hoofed northerners came
and trod on Pontius Pilate’s countrymen, and
they became like the dodo and the moa—a people
hard to find, and a ruin of confused bones.
< « *
Christianised Italy always behaved like a
country with something on its mind. It was always
washing its hands, like Pilate, and the Tiber
continued muddy. Its denunciations and perse-
cutions of the Hebrew race in later years consti-
tuted the record bluff of history—the bluff of a
pious nation which was desperately anxious
to change the subject, and which desired
above all things to draw attention away
from the fact that it was an Italian governor
who passed the most infamous death sentence
in all history, and a ribald Italian soldiery that
gambled for the dying Teacher’s clothes on Cal-
vary. The old Italian race might be mostly
dead and gone, but the new race, partly Saracen in
the south, partly German in the north, partly any-
thing in the middle districts, had inherited the
Italian name and the feverish Italian desire to
wash its hands, and it had taken over Pilate’s
tin dish and bit of soap along with the rest of the
inheritance. Yet despite all its anxious wash, Italy
continued singularly scarce of good fortune. Cer-
tainly there was a curious decadent radiance about
it in the Middle Ages. Venice, Genoa, Milan,
Rome, and other States were great in a way.
They produced great tyrants in a small fashion —
men like Gian Visconti, who nearly conquered
all Lombardy, and Cassar Borgia, who raked
all Gehenna to build up an empire that would
about extend from Sydney to Katoomba ; great
painters, great poets, great poisoners, amazing
liars, and men whose perjury amounted to genius.
They also produced great soldiers—great in their
capacity to lick small Italian soldiers ; but except
that once the Italians asserted themselves in the
field against Frederick of Germany, Italy was
everybody’s dog, and the only reason why it
wasn’t permanently conquered was that there
were too many conquerors, and Germans, French,
and Spaniards could never agree on a division of
the spoil. Italy to this day is something of a
jest—the only Great Power in history which never
won a battle single-handed against anybody worth
mentioning, either since it became a Great Power
or for centuries previous to that humorous event.
* * * *
The church, nowadays, is a less ferocious thing
than it was a few hundred years ago. It is less
credulous, and has ceased to wholly pin its faith
to Ahasuerus, the Wandering Jew, although an
hysterical lady identified that person in Sydney,
a few years ago, and solemnly gave him two-and-
sixpence, and asked him for particulars of his
career. It is also less addicted to cursing, and
not nearly so positive that some great, imperish-
able anathema followed on the heels of that
tragedy that lies nineteen centuries back in the
dim wastes of history. But still, it hasn’t got
rid of the impression that the dispersed people of
Jewry, who have so wonderfully preserved their
nationality, are, in some sense, a sign of
Divine displeasure. Yet the homeless Jew
has compensations. He is the world’s financier,
while the Italian sells 12 bananas in a bag for 6d.
The Jew owns gold mines and finances new rail-
roads, and when he wants a tunnel dug out he
hires the Italian laborer to flourish the pick and
shovel on the j ob, and to bring his dinner along in a
handkerchief. The Jew owns great banks, and the
Italian grinds the barrel organ at his gates, and
urges the monkey to dance with renewed fury,
and hopes that the Hebrew may condescend to
look out of the window and throw him a coin. The
Jew is grasping and covetous, and so is the
Italian, but £1 sticks to the capable Hebrew
while the other man fails to get a really firm hold
of 3d. .And if the Italian has a country of his
own it is not his fault; be owes it to the mterfer-
ence of that bogus Bonaparte, the gaudy
Napoleon 111. reputedly the son of a Dutch
Admiral; therefore the fact that the Dago is less
homeless than the Israelite is not so distinctly to
his credit as it might be. And the Italian has
more on his mind than the Jew. He is still
worried about Pontius Pilate. One small remote
offshoot of the Italian Church, it is true, made
Pilate a saint, which was an heroic way of dispos-
ing of him; and celebrated him on the 25th of each
succeeding June. But the Italian race hasn’t yet
got quite rid of the legend that the devil fishes its
long-dead procurator out of a Swiss lake every
Good Friday, and that when he is extracted he
seats himself dripping on the shore and washes his
hands. It may be symbolical of the painful and
complicated case of Pilate, the ancient Dago who
washed his hands, that the modern Dago so often
forgets to wash the other parts of himself. Long
centuries of unconscious concentration of thought
on Pilate’s hands may have tended to make him
oblivious to the condition of his own legs. And
on the other hand, Pilate may have nothing to do
with the downfall of Italy, and that country may
be simply another evidence of how pride, bloated
insolence, vast estates and cheap colored labor
can undermine a great empire and destroy a once
virile people. The case is a complicated one—
altogether too complicated for decision off-hand—
and it is hereby adjourned indefinitely, with Costs,
not that the chance of collecting Costs from the
defendant amounts to much.
THE FEDERAL BUREAUCRACY.
When the Public Service Act— governing the
conditions of employment in the xlustralian Civil
Service —was under discussion by Panicment, the
one danger against which precaution was deemed
necessary was “political influence.” All parties
professed a great dread of the intrusive paw of
the politician, and eagerly assented to every
safeguard suggested against that evil. But —
as Dan O’Connor would say—in avoiding
the dangerous embrace of Scylla, Parliament
rushed to the devouring bosom of Charybdis.
Political influence was banished effectively enough,
but at the same time the foundations were laid, deep
and firm, for a system of Civil Service bureau-
cracy, which, even now in its young growth,
thwarts public administration, fills Ministers with
uneasiness, and has earned scathing denunciation
from the Public Service Commissioner himself.
L nless there is prompt reform —and reform can
■only come from the Parliament —the Federal
Civil Service may become, as it gets to better
know its power, a real danger to Australia.
it ¥ * *
The present unsatisfactory state of the Aus-
tralian Public Service is in a small part due to
the fact that the first selection of big officials
was not the best possible. Some of the men
chosen had given promise of capacity, and have
fulfilled that promise since. Others won to posi-
tions which were far above their capacities. Social
good fellowship, ability to dance attendance on
a great man, to carry his coat and run his
errands, scored in some cases as against real
merit. But this bad start was, after all,
only a temporary evil, easily to be reme-
died if a good system had been set up
when the tim6 came to make permanent
provision for the control of the Public Service.
The failures might have been graded into their
proper rank—even a gentleman with an amiable
capacity for carrying coats has a use somewhere,
as a hall porter or a messenger, if not as an under-
secretary —and new appointments made with
greater care. But the Public Service Act, as
adopted, represented a close copy of the methods
by which China has managed to kill all progress,
and has handed the Government over to a
herd of well-informed fools who have passed
examinations in a literature representing
the accumulated blunders and superstitions
of twenty centuries. The Chinese system
of government by literati —whereby admirals
are chosen by reason of their vast knowledge of
ancient poetry, and of moral maxims, and of
alleged facts about the manners, customs, pur-
poses, working hours, and remuneration of devils —
is really not very much more absurd than that of
the Australian Public Service, into whose ranks
you enter at an early age by examination, in
which you progress by examination, and from
which you can only be banished by a miracle or
an Act of Parliament. The precautions against
men of talent or enterprise intruding into the
holy preserve are elaborate. Equally careful are
the safeguards against authority driving out any
of the sacred caste for incompetence or ineffi-
ciency. Once a Federal civil servant, always
a Federal civil servant. The “sack” is practi-
cally impossible, A public servant must get into
gaol or an inebriate asylum before he can
endanger his position. He may be utterly in-
competent ; his incompetence may be most
clearly proved by the disastrous effect it has on
the public administration; but in the desire to
obliterate “ political influence,” things have been
so fixed that his position is none the less secure.
Since this argument is against a system, and not
an official, or a group of officials, specific instances
will not be cited. But several instances will
promptly suggest themselves to every one inter-
ested in Federal administration, where an official
has shown incompetence, prejudice, and ignorance,
which in any private employment would be pun-
ished by severe reproof, if not dismissal. But
nothing is done, nothing can be done. Ministers
know, but to interfere requires a degree of moral
courage that they do not possess; so they let
matters drift absolutely into the hands of the per-
manent officials. Government by Responsible
Ministers approaches its end, to be replaced with
Government by irresponsible bureaucrats. In some
cases the official is able and the result is fairly
good. In others he is a complete failure, and the
effect is wholly bad. In all cases there is a
mischievous tendency toentrench the Civil Service
as a holy caste, a power “ behind the throne,” not
liable to Parliamentary criticism, but using puppet
Ministers as shields behind which it runs the show.
* * * *
A remedy can only come from a change in the
system set up by the Public Service Act. There
is no call to revert to an era in which the service
will be wholly run by political influence. But
there is need to restore sufficient political influ-
ence to give back to Ministers th eir proper responsi-
bility ; to make the Civil Service more amenable
to control; and to modify the wooden system by
which almost insuperable obstacles are placed in the
way of any change in the personnel. Parliament,
when discussing the matter before, seemed to act
on an assumption of its own inherent wickedness.
It tied the hands of its members and its Ministers
tightly so that they should not be able to lift even
a dishonest finger; and, at the same time, left
the Service to riot as it pleased. The bad result
is now patent to all who have a means of judging.
from the members of the Cabinet and tLo
Service Commissioner down to the humbS
official, who soon learns to know that, if he can
play cunning ” and keep warily within the
regulations, there is no laziness, inefficiency or
carelessness of which he may not be guilty with
impunity. The Bulletin has no hostility to the
Civil Service as a class. Its plea has alwavs been
for a well-paid service. But as a condition of
good payment there must be good service. That
the Commonwealth does not get in some of the
most important of its departments
PLAIN ENGLISH.
Concentrating Things in Melbourne.
The announcement of N.S.W. State Insect
Carruthers, that he would rather hand over
the Sydney Observatory site to the British
Government for use as a Mint, and practi-
cally destroy the value of 50 years of Sydney
meteorological observations by compelling the
Federal Department to start afresh in anew
place and under new conditions, than sell Sydney
Observatory to the Australian Government —that,
in short, he couldn’t resist the temptation to be
mean and petty and paitry and gratuitously
offensive once more—has had its natural
result. The headquarters of the Australian
Meteorological Department, which were in doubt
before, go to Melbourne till the Federal capital
is founded. According to the Constitution, the
Australian Government is to take charge of
meteorological affairs, just as it is to take charge
of a Federal capital located in New South Wales.
But though the Australian Parliament years ago
selected a Federal capital site, which answers
every requirement of the Constitution, and fills
every detail of the agreement made with
N.S.W., it can’t get an acre of land out of
Joseph, the Dreadful Small Boy, save apparently
by violence; all it can get is Joseph’s waspish
little insults because it doesn’t fix the capital
which he keeps unfixed. Now it appears that
even in the small matter of a meteorological
station there will be the same struggle with
Joseph before it is possible to buy a site.
Joseph’s first polite idea is to turn the
Meteorological Department into the street
as soon as it becomes a Federal institu-
tion, and as the Australian Government
doesn’t choose that its Meteorologist Department
shall be a homeless affair and a vagabond
on the face of the earth, or shall hang out in a
boarding-house or Salv’army Home, it naturally
establishes its headquarters in a State which
doesn’t keep an offensive public kid like Car-
kuthers to run its affairs. The S.M. Herald
professes to see in the decision another evi-
dence of the tendency to centralise things in
Melbourne. Certainly there is such a tendency.
Things have to be centred somewhere, and
apparently they can’t be centred at a Federal
capital in N.S.W. until Carruthers is politi-
cally dead. It was a Reid-Carruthers N.S.W.
Government which broke faith with the other
States, faked the Federal Referendum, annulled
the popular verdict, and, by a dodge worthy of a
land agent or a petty police court solicitor, got a
proviso inserted in the Constitution that the
Australian capital was to be in N.S.W. It was the
officer appointed by another N.S.W.. Government
which recommended Monaro as the best site. It
was the old faking State Government, back in office
again, which refused to surrender the site that its
predecessors had recommended, and the Com-
monwealth had accepted, and which had the un-
speakable impudence to demand that the N.S.W.
State Government should itself select the capital
of all Australia, and that neither the other States
nor the Australian Parliament should have a voice
in the selection of Australia’s political centre.
There has been a deadlock ever since then, and
every Federal Department naturally has its head-
quarters in Melbourne, in order to be in touch
with the Government —the Government being
also exiled to Melbourne. And uni ’ C arruthers
acquires some sense and political there
seems no remedy for this tendency to concentrate
things in Melbourne.
An Extra Special Anti-Federal Lie.
There are so many disloyal, traitorous, Tory,
anti-Australian rags which open their columns to
the anti-Federal liar that The Bulletin is only
interested nowadays when it comes across some-
thing special. It has done so on this occasion.
One of the worst of the unpatriotic rags is the
wofuily misnamed Liberty and Progress, the
Melbourne organ of the Employers’Federation,
v. :.i°h is filled with bitterness against the Aus-
tralian Parliament because it isn’t wholly under
the domination of Fat. This paper, in a recent
issue, published a more than ordinarily foolish
letter —n fact, a sort of a prize fool communi-
cation —under the heading of “Why Federa-
tion has been a Failure.” The epistle contains,
on the average, a lie to every two lines, and the
following is chosen as a fair sample :
Our Federal Government has been a great humbug
with regard to its factory and arbitration laws.... Many
young Australians are thrown out of the factories and
ruined for life Why should a farmer be made to pay
£350 in the case where a laborer hits his finger with a
hammer?
“Paul Mackenzie” is the signature to the
dreadful affair, and he is either the most ignorant
or the most mendacious Paul of whom this paper
has any record. It seems really superfluous to
point out that, unfortunately, the Federal Parlia-
ment has no power to legislate for factories, and,
of course, has not done so. Nor does it legislate
on arbitration matters except when a dispute
spreads over more than one State, and an em-
ployee with a damaged finger doesn’t spread,
though he may swell. That employee, and the
farmer who has to pay him £350 when he hits his
finger with a hammer, are such gorgeous efforts in
the way of lying that they are calculated to
set the public wondering what kind of editor
it is that lets Paul rave in his columns.
Of course, Liberty and Progress represents the
“ gentlemanly ” interest —the interest of the
Toryism which is quite sure it is a gentleman
because of its wealth and its stomach. Australia
has learned a lot about its Tory gentleman since
the beginning of the Commonwealth. After
reading his political utterances, it has come to
the conclusion that a large section of him will
only find his real moral and mental level when he
applies for a job as a goanna, and is refused
owing to the insufficiency of his testimonials.
The Sydney Railway Problem.
Now that Sydney has, to some extent, got its
second wind after being introduced to its new
railway station, it might seriously consider the
advantages of tearing down that garish monument
of imbecility, and building something sensible in a
sensible locality'. Railway stations seem to be
Sydney’s hoodoo, its incurable curse, its persistent
streak of ill-luck. Asa matter of (act, Sydney
has no railway station, and never had one. Tor
years and years it put up with a huge, straggling


dog-kennel terminus in a suburb. Ever since
1860, or thereabouts, it has been worried by its
remote dog-kennel, and has been considering the
question of bringing the railway into the city.
There was not, and is not now, any serious diffi-
culty about the matter ; almost every other great
city on earth has dealt with the same problem,
and Sydney offers no more geographical obstacles
than most other places. But whenever
the subject was mentioned angry experts, and
alleged experts, raged over the question as if it
were a sectarian row. It was, of course, a
matter of some expens' —such matters always are.
The expense wouldn’t iiave been very great, and
any fairly economical and courageous Ministry
could have found the money in a year or two, out
of revenue. But it had come to be understood,
through long continuance of the borrowing
disease, that to pay for any new works out of
revenue was little short of blasphemy, and so
everybody proposed to borrow the money. And
it seemed a good deal of money to borrow
for a mile and a half of line and a station,
especially as the old station that was dropping to
pieces wasn’t yet paid for. So every time the
question arose there were cheap and nasty
schemes for stealing some public park as a site for
a station, and, on the other side, there was
violent opposition to stealing the park ; and a
deadlock ensued every time as a matter of course.
There were 40 years of deadlock, all because no
one thought of the simple device of paying for a
new station out of revenue. And at last
the most astonishing person who was ever a
N.S.W. Minister suddenly borrowed a huge
sum of money, and built a remote station
in the same old, awkward, out-of-the-way
suburb, and left Sydney in the same old hole—a
great city with no railway. People who arrive
by rail have still to depend on tram cars
and cabs and omnibuses to take them into
Sydney; and the narrow, winding streets of
that capital, which is worse off for streets
than almost any other white man’s metro-
polis of its size on this earth, become more
and rpore overcrowded, and more and more worthy
of the name of Suicide Alley. It is still practi-
cally impossible to supply railway communication
with Woolloomooloo, Paddington, Surry Hills,
Woollahra, Waverley, Coogee, Bondi, etc., for
there is no Sydney railway to connect them with.
The long-talked-of railway connection by bridge
with North Sydney is as remote as ever; the new
station brought the railway only a few yards
nearer to North Sydney. It was all a muddle
and a failure, and the best thing Sydney can
do now is to recognise the fact at once. The
city’s urgent need is a great central railway station
about Wynyard-square, near the business centre,
on the rising ground that leads to the only pos-
sible place where a railway bridge can be carried
across to North Sydney, and at a fairly convenient
starting point for a suburban railway system to
the eastern part of the N.S.W. metropolis. The
new station and the new bit of railway should be
built out of revenue. As the new station would
destroy the little pleasure-ground of Wynyard
Square, anew bit of pleasure-ground should be
supplied out of revenue. The present insane
railway station should be regretfully torn down,
and the debt upon it made good out of revenue.
While the State is about the matter, the old,
dead and gone railway station, which still appears
on paper as a permanent and reproductive work,
and good for its value in public debt, should be
paid for out of revenue. In short, there should
be a cleaning up. There is nothing to be gained
by shirking the fact that Sydney’s new station is a
bad debt and a monument of folly and ignorance.
It is part of the price N.S.W. pays for mistaking
Owe’Sullivan for a statesman when he was only
qualified to be a blundering suburban alderman.
The present necessity is to repair Owe’Sullivan
as speedily as possible, and to forget him as soon
as convenient. There is no reason why his politi-
cal ghost should be allowed to permanently retard
the progress of the Cornstalk capital. He is poli-
tically dead, and the kindest thing that can be
done is to politically bury him, and say nothing
about his dreadful white elephant on his political
tombstone.
An Exhibition.
The Australian Chambers of Manufactures having
proposed to hold, at some convenient date, a great
exhibition of Australian manufactured goods with
a view to encouraging Australian industry and
teaching an unfortunate importing people that
their own country’s goods are well worth a trial,
the importing curse has hastily girded up its loins
and bolted into the arena of controversy. The
Mayor of Sydney called a meeting to consider
the advisability of holding an international exhi-
bition which will overshadow and smother any
show of purely Australian products. The inter-
national exhibition, if it comes off, will probably
be a huge dead loss, like all its predecessors. Aus-
tralia will be expected to stand the loss, so that
it may advertise foreign goods, and in order
to encourage its people to buy the products
of every country save their own. There will be
a show of Australian goods, of course, but they
will be so blotted out by the accumulated pro-
ducts of Europe, Asia, Africa, and America that
they will probably amount to less than nothing
in the way of encouraging Australian industry.
And already Australia has before it, in the im-
porters’ windows, a great deal more exhibition of
foreign products than is good for it. Of course
these products are not all foreign, but when the
goods in the unpatriotic trader’s window happen
by chance to be Australian, they are mostly
ticketed as foreign, with intent to deceive. That
public enemy, the importer, has, for his own base
ends, so fostered the idea in times past that
foreign goods are always best, that even if he
changes his mind and sells Australian goods,
it now pays him best to call them
foreign. Under such conditions, this country
requires a whole series of exhibitions of
Australian goods only, so that for once, despite
the importing liar, all articles of Australian
manufacture on view _ will be labelled as Austra-
lian, and Australia will get credit for them. Then
if the public, which sees for the first time what
its country really can do, is satisfied with the
articles, it can decide to buy Australian products.
If it isn’t satisfied, the international exhibition of
foreign goods is always at its disposal. In Sydney
that exhibition stretches from Bridge-street down
to the railway station, and in Melbourne from
Parliament House to near Spencer-street—about
two miles of blastiferous International Exhibition
in each case. Any person who asks for more ex-
hibition than that ranks with the big cuttle-fish
for greed, and is a ravening cormorant to boot.

More Trouble from the North.
Java is starting to give trouble to Australia.
Last year the Commonwealth produced 15,000
tons more sugar than it swallowed; yet nearly
40,000 tons came in from Java, and the importers
were able to undercut Australian sugar on account
of the preposterously low wages ruling in the
Dutch island—lower than the North Queens-
lander ever paid to his Tommy Tanna. If the
Colonial Sugar Cos. were not playing a waiting
game, it would have squealed long before this.
It lies low, however, content with the assurance
that presently the whole crowd of Australians
who are concerned in sugar-growing, will squeal;
and the C.S.R. Cos. knows that it is the
squeal at the ballot-box that counts. Its
rooted belief is in discolored labor of some
sort —any sort of labor, in fact, as long as
it isn’t white. So it seems to have no objection
to Java trickling in just enough sugar to squeeze
down the price to a point at which White Aus-
tralia cannot make a decent living with white
labor. Then, of course, with the industry in
despair, it will have ready-made the best of argu-
ments—from the short-sighted commercial point
of view—in favor of again painting North Aus-
tralia yellow, brown, black, and piebald, and
putting on a permanent coat this time. So Aus-
tralia must keep its eye open. It mustn’t let this
Java trade grow. It must even take steps to
block what trade there already is. If Australia
were justified in adopting the black-labor crutch,
supposing it got one of its limbs of industry
crushed, that is no reason why Australia should
put its limb where it will get crushed. It had far
better put its limb out of danger ; and there’s no
such place of safety in the present circumstances
as behind a high tariff wall.
Items from Monkeydom.
London Daily Telegraph (18/l/’07) casts some
more of the bull’s-eye of publicity on the Jap :—
Loyal as English opinion is to pur Japanese allies, it
would be foolish to blind ourselves to the fact that
Japan’s ascendancy in the Far East is not by any means
giving universal satisfaction. In Korea, feeling against
the officials who are acting under the Marquis Ito and
against the police and military authorities is very bitter
indeed. A Seoul newspaper printed in English, the
Korean Daily News, remarks: “ Korean people have
been, and still are being, forcibly dispossessed of their
property at the nod of a Japanese commander, and
there are already many cases where land taken in this
way has been devoted to uses other than military. In
some instances Japanese farmers are now growing crops
upon ground from which the Korean owners were
evicted less than two years ago. It is true that a
meagre payment has been made for some of the land
thus taken, but, as a proof of its utter inadequacy, we
may cite an instance which came under our notice in
the north, where a Korean who was able to prove that he
had just refused Y180 for his ground, was evicted by
the Japanese Army authorities and only paid Y20. All
ranks act upon an apparent conviction that the Korean
people have no claim to consideration in their own
country. Those in the army are either unaware of, or
ignore, the fact that the position which Japan has
ostensibly assumed in Korea is not that of conqueror,
but that'of protector and guide.”
Discussing the kindred topic of the position in Man-
churia, the China Gazette says: “Japan has taken
Russia’s place in the leased territory of Kuantung, and is
making it unprofitable for a single white man to go there.
Japan, using the Manchuria Railway Company as her
instrument, is creating a monopoly in Southern Man-
churia, which makes the annexation of the country
merely a question of time. The port of Neicchivang, a
British-opened port, where Englishmen have handled the
trade of Manchuria for nearly half a century, is being so
shackled by Japanese ingenuity, and so flooded by
thousands and tens of thousands of Japanese, that the
white trader is almost doomed. The Japanese advance
has reached as far as Kuanchengtzu, which is nearly 500
miles north of Port Arthur, and from the limit of their
railway zone they are now pouring men and women by
the thousand into Northern Manchuria. The Chinese
army is being armed with cast-off Japanese cannon and
muskets, sold at fabulous profits ; Japanese officers are
teaching Chinese soldiers as much as it is deemed wise
for them to know at the present moment; and in
Japanese schools and academies Chinese youths are being
made to believe that the European is now a totally un-
necessary luxury, and his continued presence an insult to
the Chinese Empire.
An astonishing case of brutality to a marine at the
Japanese naval station of Yokosuka is being investigated
by the authorities, and it is stated that over twenty per-
sons are involved. As related in the Eastern newspapers
the story is that a marine, named Yamada, was sus-
pected of stealing a comrade’s cap, and a petty officer,
called Mashita, ordered recourse to corporal punishment
to extort a confession. This he admitted to Lieutenant
Komatsu, who carried out a preliminary investigation.
Yamada was suspended from the ceiling and whipped
with wire ropes. He raised screams of agony, whereupon
the men crammed a tenugui (Japanese towel) into the
wretched victim’s mouth ; but, unable to endure the
pain, he convulsively swallowed nearly half of the im-
promptu gag. The astonished petty officer, Mashita,
drew out the tenugui, but the violence employed
appears to have injured the internal vessels of the
aesophagus, and the poor man vomited large quanti-
ties of blood, became insensible, and afterwards died.
When he was lowered from the ceiling the joints of both
arms were dislocated.
Nowadays it is hard to find one writer who has a
good word to say for the Jap. The Monkey has
brushed off the smile that made him look such a
good little Monkey, and there is plain Gorilla
underneath.
The Disarmament Idea.
The disarmament scheme which the British
Government proposes to put before the Hague
Convention never looked very hopeful, and the
more details come to hand the less promising it
appears. Its main feature seems to be that
Britain proposes to maintain a navy equal to the
next two largest European navies combined, and,
if the other European Powers will reduce the
weight and expense of their sea armaments,
Britain —always maintaining the two-Power
standard—willdo likewise. Supposing the next two
biggest European navies muster nine and 11 first-
class ironclads, Britain will be content with 20
first-class ironclads, and so with cruisers and other
vessels ; if the next two biggest European navies
count 19 and 21 first-class ironclads, Britain will
maintain 40, and so on. But the other European
States are not likely to see the point. Their
view of the case is probably something like this :—
(1) The proposed disarmament is to mean the peaceful
surrender to Britain of control of the sea for all time.
(2) It is to leave the sea-borne commerce of every other
European State at the mercy of Britain while Britain’s
commerce will be fairly well safeguarded.
(3) Ibis toleave every European country with a seaboard
open to invasion, or at least to blockade, by Britain, while
Britain, with its preponderating fleet, will be fairly safe.
(4) Britain has annexed almost all the good, empty,
fertile, convenient places of the earth where white
colonisation on a large scale is possible (barring those
places in South America from which Uncle Sam warns off
all intruders). Britain, with Canada, Australia, and
Maoriland as outlets, may grow to be an empire of
500,000,000 white people. France and Germany can only
send their surplus populations over sea to become sub-
jects of a foreign Power, and to be gradually absorbed
and lost in a foreign race. Therefore Britain’s proposal is
practically a suggestion that, Bull having got pretty
nearly all there is to get, there should be a general agree-
ment to sit down in peace and not call his title in question.
(5) The proposal only comes when, for the first time in
more than a century, there seems a possibility of a real
marine rival appearing on the horizon, and therefore
looks partly selfishness and partly scare.
In short, one Power at least is likely to reply to
Britain in a few short words : “ When we reach
the -position that you are in to-day, we will also
be ready to talk disarmament.” And for a
fiercely ambitious State, the attitude is under-
standable. When Holland had command of the
sea, Britain would listen to no schemes of dis-
armament till it had sunk Holland. When France
held Canada and a great part of India, and
the Dutch held the Cape of Good Hope,
there was no mention of disarmament till
Britain had gathered in the lot. And now
the price of too much empire is ceaseless
vigilance and a chronic inability to sleep o’
nights, and States which grudge Britain all
this empire do not see why they should make the
price any less. If the greatest earth-swallower in
history has acquired more frontiers that it can
defend, and more niggers than it can manage, it
is no one’s business to ease the burden by re-
ducing the danger of attack. Those who were
despoiled in times past are rather pleased than
otherwise if the plunder is too heavy to carry. In
this troubled world a big empire needs a big army
to defend it, and the idea of making a special
arrangement, so that the biggest empire of all may
be allowed to enjoy the fruits of conquest with-
out paying for the big army, isn’t likely to
appeal to a hungry despot like William of
Germany. Britain and Britain’s foreign posses-
sions, with hardly an exception—can only be
attacked by sea, while all Britain’s European
rivals have on their minds the night-
mare of both sea and land defence.
The proposed disarmament would still leave
them with the two nightmares on hand,
and meanwhile Britain, unapproachable by
land, and secured from worry at sea by the
guarantee of its rivals that the biggest of them
would limit its fleet to about half that of the
United Kingdom, could lean its trident against
the bedpost at night and slumber comfortably.
Britain would be freed from what is practically
its only visible danger. Germany would be in as
much danger from France and Russia as ever, for,
even if armies are reduced all round, it is as
unpleasant and often as costly to be licked by
400,000 men as by 1,500,000; and it would be in
greater danger should trouble arise with Britain,
having given up, by the disarmament treaty, all
hope of building a navy that could hold its own
against the floating Bull. That is how Germany
would assuredly look at the matter. Russia
would probably reply that it has no longer
much to lose at sea, and that the only
thing that interests it is the question of
some reliable guarantee that Japan will
disarm on land. All parties will probably want
to know if Britain proposes to renounce the Jap
alliance ; and will suggest that its offer to only
maintain a fleet equal to the two next largest
European navies is a mere confidence trick
if, by way of having a card up its sleeve and a
spare ace in its boots, it has the Jap fleet at its
disposal as well. Also, they will probably in-
quire if Japan is to reduce its fleet, or if it is
simply proposed that Continental Europe shall
lay up its ships and leave itself open to a Jap
blockade. Altogether the prospect isn’t good for
disarmament.

Maoriland’s New Ideas about Land.
Some little part of Maoriland has reached a lucid
interval in its borrowing madness. When John
McKenzie helped Ballance to pass a Land for
Settlements Act, cash for the State resumption of
private estates was obtained from Cohen ; and
ever since that time the circumstance that money
was required for land settlement has been put for-
ward to justify another and yet another visit to
the pawnshop. But Robert MoNab, the mode-
rately new Lands Minister, set out with the
astounding public statement that large estates
may be subdivided without the assistance
of the habitual loan; and Maoriland is
still disturbed about it. McNab’s proposals pro-
vide for the compulsory sale, within ten years, of
that portion of any estate in excess of £50,000
unimproved value, and the limitation of estates
acquired in the future to £15,000. Primarily
through the appearance of the State in the market
as a buyer, the value of land has steadily gone
up, until it is well-nigh impossible for any farmer
to pay interest on the price and make a decent
living. Maoriland Parliament votes a sum
annually for the purchase of estates, and the land
has to be bought. Knowing this, the per-
sons who acquired a small kingdom from
the simple natives in the early days have put a
fictitious price on their holdings. It is true
the Assessment Court finally decides the price,
but that judicial tribunal usually strikes a
medium, and as the sheep person’s quotation is
near the roof, the medium is so high that you can’t
leach it without a ladder. The values of adjoining
lands are influenced, and presently earth on the
ranges will be sold at per foot or by the lb. McNab
proposes to withdraw the Government from
the land deal business, and to compel the sheep-
growing individual to pass on the surplus portion
of his estate directly to the people who want
to grow families. For some time past the arro-
gant large landholder has been casting around for
a suitable brick to throw at the proposal, and he
has found it in the eternal cry for the freehold.
The Farmers’ Union, which the big squatter rules,
denounces the proposed limitation of estates as
an invention of the city Socialist, who, it is said,
meditates the resumption in the hazy future of all
freehold lands, and the conversion of the country’s
backbone into an army of rack-rented slaves.
Money lenders and boodlers, who would be pre-
vented by the £15,000 limit from holding little
principalities for speculative purposes, join in the
anti-Bill crusade, and remark fiercely that they
will be compelled to realise their mortgages, raise
the rate of interest, and play up generally.
Branches of the Farmers’ Union have also
met and passed choleric resolutions about the
freeholds; these were telegraphed far and wide,
tilL the small farmer rose and cursed the “out-
rageous scheme of dispossession.” The freehold
brick has, therefore, so far fulfilled expectations
that small farmers who climbed to affluence by
the leasehold ladder have been induced to address
their fellow dupes at meetings, which have inva-
riably ended in cheers for the freehold. The fact
that, largely owing to the absurd prices which
buyers have been forced to pay (or owe),
£60,000,000 worth of freehold land in M.L. is held
under mortgage —in which case the freehold is
little more than a name—is lost sight of for the
moment. Likewise, the miserable private tenants
who are paying rent on boom-priced land, and
the others who would be tenants if rents were not
so outrageous, are overlooked. Moreover, it isn’t
mentioned that “the outrageous scheme of dis-
possession” only dispossesses one man to give
possession to, perhaps, a hundred others. It is,
perhaps, the biggest scheme for putting the dis-
possessed into possession that Maoriland has yet
seriously discussed. Yet the small farmers kick—
it is a shocking thing that the longer a man
is on the soil, the more readily he becomes
the tool of the immense landowner, whose
barbed-wire fencing is blocking the outlet for
the energies of his own sons and daughters.
Opposition leader Massey and the Tory press
have remarked, to embarrass the Government,
that an increase in the graduated land-tax would
be infinitely preferable to the new Bill, and at
length McNab has grimly advised them not to
tempt him. A heavy tax would be more calcu-
lated to throw the whole of the large estates,
without limitation and sans 10 years’ grace, on to
the market at one time, which would be an un-
pleasant thing for the owners. McNab said he
didn’t want that, so he would stick to his Land
Bill. The Tory press, encouraged, redoubled its
arguments in favor of the land-tax. Then affable
Premier Ward scared it by remarking, at
Auckland, on the eve of his jaunt to Britain, that
after all Parliament might impose a heavier land-
tax. The announcement, which was made with the
idea of leaving things unruffled, caused con-
sternation undreamed of by Joseph. The Tory
press hastens to say that if the Land Bill is
bad, an increased land-tax is not good, and the
angered papers bitterly reproach the Govern-
ment with lack of courage to stick to its
own Land Bill. Meantime McNab is plodding
from platform to platform with his Bill in his
hand, and he is making a fight that does him
credit. He is showing no lack of courage, at any
rate.

Back to the Land.
Austin Chamberlain, who inherits the eye-glass
of his father in the British House of Commons,
has been telling England that “ if the tariff reform
policy were coupled with the cry of ‘ Back to the
land’ many of the difficulties confronting that
policy would be removed.” And the man who
reads this can only say in a faint voice tha,t Saul
has not yet been quite dragged by the hair from
out of the congregation of the prophets. Chamber-
lain junior, looking dimly through the goggle of
his ancestors, just about perceives the beginning
of a great truth. The people of England need to
get back to the land—if only the big landlord
were obliterated for ever, and they could go back
as a nation of small farmers, each cultivating his
bit of soil, and with a tariff that would provide
him with a fairly profitable market for his pro-
duce. But no one in Britain seems to have the
courage to obliterate the big and sacred land-
lord, and Britain wanders from one device to
another in the effort to put things right—things
that won’t be put right while the big landed
incubus is above ground. This is the British
tariff record in brief :—
(1) Britain had Protection and the big landlord. It
had a great agriculture then, and was able to feed itself
almost entirely, instead of being, as it is now, in such a
position that it is the only nation on earth which could
be starved into submission by a very few weeks’ blockade.
(2) But the big landlord charged such high rents that
the price of British-grown wheat had to be high in order
that the agricultural tenant could meet the useless and
bloated person’s exactions. Britain felt this was an evil,
and instead of trying to cheapen food by abolishing the
big landlord, and by encouraging the cultivation of every
possible inch of the country by a peasant proprietary, and
thus making the local food supply exceed the local
demand, it tried Freetrade and the big landlord. Not
daring to abolish the landlord, who was useless, it set out
to abolish the tenant, who was useful.
(3) Britain then, as already mentioned, had Freetrade
and the big landlord. It got cheap food. But its agri-
culturists couldn’t compete with the imported food, and
at the same time pay the high rent to the big landlord,
and also pay the monstrous direct taxation which had
to be levied on the British citizen in order to avoid
taxing the foreigner at the Customs. And the big land-
lord didn’t suffer much after all; he found that he could
make just about as much (sometimes more) by laying the
land waste, and turning it into preserves for wild animals,
to be shot at in the name of sport.
(4) So Britain went on having Freetrade and the big
landlord. It lost the bulk of its agricultural population—
the men who used to fill the ranks of its armies, and win
its battles. Its soldiers so degenerated that the army
which never lost a battle, no matter what the odds, against
the splendid French veterans in the Peninsula, became
the army which never won a battle except with immense
odds in its favor (and often not then) against the Boer
farmer-militia in South Africa. And agriculture was
killed 50 dead that Britain now <*nly growr enough food
to supply its own needs two months in the year, and might
be starved into submission even in a successful naval war
against European enemies. For the fact remains that
unless, in addition to beating or bottling up the enemies’
fighting fleet, it could police the whole route to America
to prevent its food ships being intercepted, it might,
if faced by a combination of two or three European
Powers, starve in the midst of victory. But still Britain
has solved the problem of getting cheap food without
disturbing its big landlord.
(5) And now Britain grows alarmed, for the country’s
dependence on foreign food supplies is so compete, and
there are so many food routes to protect to keep the
Home.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Mr. William Hogan, the well-known importer, is
leaving for Home by the R.M.S. Caractacus on Saturday
next. — Sydney Fawning Herald.
He’s going “ Home ” —
Old Hogan’s Bill,
From Dingo Hill,
Who left the mullock-shifting show
When Sydney called, some years ago.
He struck it rich there, sold old clo’,
Enlarged his sphere, became a co.;
And, groping after fame,
An importer he became.
Each south-bound vessel to his lair
Consignments brought of English ware
(Brown-paper boots
And shoddy suits),
Till William grew in opulence,
Made pounds where others made but pence,
And thought of going “ Home.”
Yes, going “ Home ” !
And straightway we
A vision see
Of William reaching Dingo Hill,
Brown heaps of mullock lying still,
The while a welcome rises shrill
From Hogan pere to long-lost Bill,
Returning home at last.
But, gentles, not so fast;
His thoughts are of another “ Home ” —
The country whence his gew-gaws come,
The land of dooks
And Freetrade spooks ;
He goes to seek a fresh supply
Of shirts and hats and hosiery.
Yes, William’s going “Home.”
The Hogans’ home,
On Dingo Hill,
Is standing still;
But propped and patched and falling fast,
Great gaping cracks admit each blast.
Old Hogan sees the years fly past,
Wondering if each will be his last.
He draws his meagre pay
On Old Age Pension day,
Talks politics and such-like stuff,
And says “ Australia’s right enough ! ”
Whilst Bill hobnobs
With English snobs,
Cries Stinking Fish all undismayed,
And spreads the gospel of Freetrade
Amongst his friends at “ Home.”
N.S.W.
