United Kingdom from starvation, that an almost in-
calculable navy hardly makes it feel secure. It remembers
that in the Napoleonic wars, despite the utter destruction
of all hostile fleets by Nelson and his colleagues, it lost
some 10,000 merchant ships—taken by swift little waspish
vessels which dodged its big fleets—and it kept losing
them till the end oi the war, and it realises that if the
next victorious sea campaign pans out similarly it may
be a lost country. It isn’t afraid of defeat at sea, but
of hunger on land.
(6) So Austin Chamberlain, seeing half the subject
dimly through his ancestor’s monocle, urges the people
to adopt Protection and go back to the land—and toil
again for the big landlord. But the big landlord might
not let the people back, being satisfied with the profits of
his game preserves. Or, if he did let them back, he would
heap up his rents again, and the price of food would be
forced by his exactions just as high as the Protective
tariff would allow. And Britain wouldn’t grow enough
food for its own consumption after all, partly because
the great landlords would still keep a huge proportion of
the land out of cultivation for purposes of sport; partly
because the remainder would be cultivated feebly by a
spirit-broken race bowed down by the exactions of the
big landlord.
Therefore, things get back to the old point that
there is mighty little to be done for Britain till it
gets rid of the big landlord, and establishes a
peasant proprietary, and takes rigid measures to
prevent any new race of landlords arising. And
Britain, apparently, would rather perish than do
anything severe to its big landlord, so it is difficult
to say what it really can do for itself.

The Private Enterprise Muddle.
President Roosevelt arose the other day to
allege that Private Enterprise railway manage-
ment in the United States had practically broken
down. According to Strong Man Theodore,
“ the railroads are no longer able to move crops
and goods rapidly enough to secure the prompt
transaction of the nation’s business, and the only
complete remedy is a system of complementary
water transport.” Yet the United States has
more railway mileage in proportion to its popula-
tion than any other countries on earth save Aus-
tralasia and Canada, and more railway in pro-
portion to its area than all save about half-a-
dozen countries in this world. And when the
amount of production is compared with the means
of carrying the products, the United States seems
vastly better off than Australia, which has no
Mississippi or Missouri, or Great Lakes to
relieve the pressure on its railway system,. The
unspeakable muddlement of Private Enterprise
seems to be the whole secret of the U.S. railway
congestion—a congestion which threatens to be-
come a partial collapse. The U.S. railway system
exists partly to carry goods in the ordinary way ;
partly to give special facilities to favored cus-
tomers and to block the trade of the rivals of these
favored customers; partly to conduct wars and
skirmishes with rival railways ; mostly as a Stock
Exchange gambling counter—a thing to be boomed
or wrecked to suit the exigencies of Stock Ex-
change jobs, a machine for freezing out weak
shareholders when there is a big “operation” on
the board, a vehicle for elaborate financial devices
in the flotation of debentures, and the rigging of
the market with preference stock, and ordinary
stock, and first and second mortgages, and liens,
and all manner of sundries. In fact, there is so
much “high finance” (which is really low swindle)
about the U.S. Private Enterprise railways that
the plain, ordinary purpose of their existence—
the swift, safe and cheap dragging of people and
goods from one point to another—is apt to become
a mere secondary consideration. No man can
drive a coach and play poker at the same time if
he wants to do justice to both undertakings, and
all over the haulage department of the U.S.
Private Enterprise railways there is mismanage-
ment and confusion, and the raw haste which is
twin sister to delay, and the raw muddle which
is twin sister to smash. The U.S. railways, in
fact, are the U.S. life assurance scandal over again
—only worse.
After the Victorian elections the Labor-leaguers
chose a mournful and distressing way of spending
their Sunday afternoon. They put up on a plat-
form in Fitzgerald’s Circus building three or four
Labor candidates who had been successful in
getting into Parliament, and then a long succes-
sion of seven doleful persons who had fought on
the same ticket, and failed. These seven per-
sons made various excuses for the fact that
tlie constituencies had mostly preferred Bent
to the more-or-less remarkable crowd that
now rallies round the Trades Hall. Beard* who
was defeated by a colorless “local ” Ministerialist
in Jika Jika, said that he had been floored by
“poison circulated by vilifying sectarians.”
Oolechin, the weird person who used to sit for
Geelong (which has now chosen an equally weird
representative in the bald-crowned Gtjrr), said
that the bosses had put obstacles in the way of
men getting on the rolls. Solly (thrown heavily
aside by Richmond in favor of George Bennett)
said “the workers did not understand the
movement.” Evans (who tried to displace larky
Willie Watt at Essendon) blamed an insolent
personal attack made by Bent. Wrout, trying
in Gippsland, had expected a licking. Flinn,
who could not get the Melbourne seat, said wildly
that he had contended with “ howling dervishes,
who fought with the weapon of the assassin,”
and he added that “the black-coated working
men were a sneaking lot.” Then they all joined
in a chorus, declaring that the Labor League
politics suffered from “wantof organisation.” It
was right here that they went wrong. The Labor
organisation is exactly the same as it always was.
There is a central body ; and there are branches
all oyer the State ; and there are earnest and en-
thusiastic officials working as hard as they know
how to drag the waggon along. But the political
flag of Labor nowadays is suspected of being half
folly and half tote-shop. The “apathy ” is not due
to faults in the organisation, but to faults in the
politics. Labor gained a great part of the legisla-
tion it wanted, when it worked in alliance with the
Liberal party. When it gave this up, and began
to be boosted by the impracticable and the im-
possible, its progress stopped short, and but for
the fine quality of its organisation and the earnest-
ness of its minor officials, a much more definite
retrogression would have set in.

S.M. Herald, of March 21, in a leading article on
“Young Criminals,” asks for “a taste of the cat”
to supplement the imprisonment and hard labor
the law at present provides. Some years ago the
cat was a lively animal in N.S.W. gaol yards, and
the daily press reporters wrote it up with great
enthusiasm. R. E. O’Connor, however, while
N.S.W. Minister of Justice, excluded press-
men from these performances, and the Sydney
press hasn’t felt really good to O’Connor
since. The S.M. Herald was a heavy-flogging
paper from its infancy. In the early days, when
employers, who were all J’s.P., sent their '“dis-
obedient hands ” to brother employers to be con-
victed and flogged oft-hand, the Herald reckoned
that the system was all right, and that the cat
was the saviour of the country. At one period,
the Governor of the day placed restrictions on the
cat industry, and the Herald threatened a revolu-
tion. Here is what Parson Lang wrote of the cat
party of that day
It was not civil and religious liberty—that pearl of
inestimable price in the eyes of our forefathers—for
which these Australian worthies petitioned ; it was for a
somewhat different species of liberty, the liberty to lash ;
and long and deep were the groans they uttered, through
their favorite organ, the Sydney Herald, when they
found the liberty denied them.
Chowth Africa, as described to Sydney Tele-
graph by Mr. C. D. Rodgers, one of a big batch
of wiser Australians, who returned by the
Hymettus the other day :
The country is in a bad plight. By this boat 130 came
away, and the majority of them are good tradesmen.
There are hundreds who are anxious to get away, but
they have no money, and many of those who are in work
are so disgusted with the prevailing conditions that if it
were not for their wives and families they would throw
it all up and leave the country. Things are getting
worse. Employers of labor are taking advantage of the
bad times, and are now offering wages that a man can’t
live upon. On the railway on which I was employed
there were starting checkers at 6s. per day, but rents
and prices of clothing still kept up. At Capetown, not
long ago, they had married men on relief works at 3s. 6d.
per day, and municipalities gave work to white men at
wages paid to Kaffirs—2s. and 2s. 6d. per day. The
Kaffirs are doing a lot of work that was at one time given
to white men. They live in locations outside the town,
and the tax they have to pay for this, combined with
their food bills, amounts to about 10s. per month.
Trade is absolutely dead. Prosperous merchants of a
year or two back are shutting down, and the small shops
are following suit. This applies all round —Johannes-
burg, Pretoria, Pietermaritzburg, Bloemfontein, Durban,
East London, and so on. Pietermaritzburg is practically
empty, and there is nothing but shops to let. At East
London, also, there is no business, but the valuation of
the borough is kept up in order to get the rates. As for
Johannesburg and Durban, crowds are always to be seen
about the streets looking for work. Of course the in-
troduction of colored labor is a big factor in the de-
pression. Large batches of Indian coolies are con-
tinually arriving. At Johannesburg there must be 60,000
or 70,000 Chinese coolies working in the mines. The im-
portation of Indian coolies is killing Natal. They are
much more intelligent than Kaffirs, and they have
already driven the white man out of some of his former
occupations. For instance, they have the vegetable and
produce trade entirely in their own hands. Everything
the people wear, eat, or use in S’AfriCa is imported—
there are no industries.
Cheap Asiatic labor and Freetrade—the two
devils whose tails Bruce Smith caresses so affec-
tionately—are enough to ruin any country, and
they promise to bring back to South Africa the
good old times when the elephant and the monkey
foregathered in the streets of Capetown.
♦
There is nothing on earth more like Queensland’s
Bob Philp than the railway line which straggles
out to Richmond —a long thin streak, which starts
from Townsville, wanders around promiscuously,
and pulls up at nowhere in particular, leaving a
vast cloud of debt behind it. And there is
nothing which better shows the difference
between Philp and Kidston than what has
happened to that line. A couple of hun-
dred miles further on is Cloncurry. As
M.P. for Townsville for 18 years, merchant of the
same port long before that, and twice Minister
for Mines, Philp has known Cloncurry for close
on 30 years, and for nearly that long he has
talked in the usual vague way about the need of
giving it a railway. But there were rival routes,
and the vague, promiscuous Philp hung himself
up between them, and smiled on everybody, and
did the usual nothing in particular—and mean-
time frittered away a few borrowed millions to
make a pretence of doing something while he
made up his mind what to do about the things
he said really wanted doing. Then Kidston, who
didn’t come from Townsville, whs invited to con-
sider the long streak of line that ended nowhere,
and the prospects of the country beyond it. That
was 18 months ago—only as many months as
Philp has been years M.P. for Townsville—and
now the line is being raced out towards Clon-
curry at the rate of 17 miles a month. That’s
the difference between Kidston and Philp.
Philp promises ; Kidston performs.

S.M. Herald’s “On the Band ” column
(19/3/’O7): —
The cable announced the other day that large quan-
tities of wine made in Germany were perfect strangers to
the grape. These alleged products of the vineyard were
manufactured from any cheap vegetable matter that will
make white spirit, and most villainously adulterated with
chemical compositions, in order that their origin may be
disguised. ... It is well known that pure brandy is one
of the rarest articles in the trade; in fact, very little is
sold in Australia save tiie small quantities which are
made in this country. Our growers, who are so far
disposed to produce a pure brandy, are compelled
to compete with the colored rubbish distilled from
potatoes, and introduced as pure brandy. ... If all
kinds of villainous compounds are allowed to
squeeze the genuine article out by reducing prices
to an unprofitable level, the producer must suc-
cumb to disgracefully unfair competition. At the
present time there are indications that leading people
engaged in the v-ine industry are prepared to invest
capital and display the greatest enterprise in producing
first-class brandies and wines, and making a reputation
for themselves in the large centres of population. A few'
protective regulations, drastically administered, would
enable them to defeat the fraudulent products of the
adulterators.
There is in the Herald’s “ On the Dust ” column
a lot of good, honest, national and Protectionist
sentiment that contrasts oddly with the untidy
howl of the rest of the paper for imports and
Johnson.
At the recent Yic. State elections a
suburban candidate had a rather novel method of
bringing his name before the public. Along the
railway lines that run through his district his
canvassers supplied sympathetic householders
with flagpoles and flags. These were affixed to
the roof, or put on some other conspicuous place.
Each flag bore the appeal—“ Vote for Bloodkins,
your old steamed and fried candidate.” The
innovation was very successful for the first few
days, there being a continuous, gentle breeze
hovering round the vicinity. But, alas ! a wind-
less spell arrived, and the rain fell steadily and
heavily, and the flags drooped, and the legend,
became just blotch.
It is not often that an invention is of general
application. Usually the inventor devotes himself to
filling a want in one trade ; but the Tyree Spray proves
an exception. It has now been adopted
liy Pastoralists for spraying ewes in lamb,
by Farmers for spraying dairy cows,
by Orchardists for spraying fruit trees,
by Vignerons for spraying vines,
by Dairymen for white-washing dairies,
by Butter Factories for white-washing factories,
by Skin Buyers for painting skins,
by the Health Department for disinfecting,
by Warehousemen for white-washing and disinfecting,
by the Public Schools for sanitary purposes,
by Landowners for killing prickly pear,
by Suburban Residents for killing lantana,
by the Government Department for oil painting,
by Gardeners for killing aphis, scale, etc.,
by Hotelkeepers for fire extinguishing and disinfecting,
by the Hospitals for all purposes,
by Schoolmasters for teaching sanitary science,
by Municipal Councils for oiling streets,
by Banks for spraying dirty notes,
by the Public generally for all purposes.
Make no mistake, the Tyree Spray pays for itself th-'
first week it is used. Send for printed matter, 314
George-etreet, Sydney.*

The ass’s skin that the world wears when
it crowns Humbug king has a good deal to do
with the success of pantomimes like “Mother
Goose,” which, after running from Christmas to
Easter in Melbourne, opened at Her Majesty’s,
Sydney, on Saturday night. “The Tivoli, did
you say ? A variety, music-hall show ! and
Mrs. de Grundeigh is surprised that you should
ask whether she has been there lately. But
wouldn’t she dearly love to ! (And, perhaps,
hasn’t she been, after all—“ Just popped in after
the interval, you know,” if you happen to
surprise her there.) Pantomime, as it is made
in 1907, is Australian music-hall or variety
show, with a little more glitter on the dress,
a little more exhibition of the limbs that
are rude than an Australian better-class music-
hall would venture, and a thin wisp of a plot show-
ing at long intervals. In essentials, however, the
two are the same, and when we marvel that
“ Mother Goose ” ran a hundred nights or so in
Melbourne, it really isn’t any more marvellousthan
that H. Rickards keeps his full houses going year
in and year out, with, for months at a time, hardly
any change in the bill worth mentioning. *
It is simply the type of the show that
makes it the success it is. In itself it has no
special, particular qualities. The music is undis-
tinguished. The dialogue, with a few bright
exceptions—Harry Shine’s bogus extract from
“Bradshaw’s” railway guide, for instance
—is not very keen or sparkling. But all
the time it is cheerful, the fare is of
the most varied character, the ballets are
very fine (the best of all is the floral one, in which
Australia’s waratahs and kangaroos are con-
spicuous figures), and the pictorial and mechanical
effebts are as opulent as one might expect from a
J. C. Williamson management and a J. C. Wil-
liamson staff. As principal boy, Miss Florence
Young has nothing to do that will add to her
reputation, and of course she does it in her usual
downright, merry, confident manner; Miss Mor-
rell, as principal girl, is an all-round disappoint-
ment, with a thin voice of average quality, and
no graces in her methods ; Harry Phydora, the
principal comedian, is brisk and bustling, and is
very well seconded by Harry Shine, before-men-
tioned ; the animal “ impersonators ” give a
quaint and clever exhibition ; and Miss Preston,
the Little Dartos, and a host of others, make up
a show that is bound to be a popular success.
* Jif * *
West’s Pictures and the Brescians, who
combine the tuneful song with the moving
picture—West’s pictures move with a minimum of
flicker. The Bulletin has found, come to inhabit
the Lyceum Theatre, Sydney, for the Easter
season.

Regarded as a play, “ Mrs. Gorringe’s
Necklace ” isn’t a jewel of the first water, but it
sparkles brightly, and once or twice—as, for
instance, where the heroine announces her mar-
riage—there is a quite unexpected glitter. That
wedding at the registry-office is sprung on the
audience just as the said audience is thinking
that the necklace would admit of the abbreviation
of a tuck, or a reef or two. The Brough-
Flemming co. presented the jewel to a crowded
and enthusiastic house on Saturday night
at Sydney Palace. The persons who make up the
setting of the “Necklace” are most interestingly
and humorously stupid. Mrs. Gorringe, who
loses her precious gaud, is the kind of woman who
would drive a hostess crazy in a week’s visit, but
with all a week’s stupidity crowded into two-and-
three-quarter hours, and run through the mind-mill
of a smart dramatist, she’s a fascinating person.
Miss Madeline Meredith plays the “guffy ” r6le,
and Miss Bessie Major that of her be-crazed
hostess, Mrs. Jardine. Miss Major is very fine in
her delineation of alternate feminine purringsand
scratchings. Miss Beatrice Day—very charming
with her gold head and white frock, and her inno-
cent role of Isabel —supplies, along with
Herbert Flemming, the principal brains and
seriousness of the play. Carter Pickford, as
David Cairn, represents the tragedy. Fortu-
nately, he trages out of sight of the audience.
Gregan M‘Mahon, who mostly polishes a small,
road-metal bit of a part into a precious stone
aspect, is the dismal detective Jernigan; and
a young damsel, programmed as Miss Gordon
Lee, is the terrible infant Vicky. She i
humorously terrible, but her voice wants a rub on
an oilstone and then a run through a polishing
lrl An ,f ?® yn ? f i Colonel Jardine is a de-
lightful old red-haired blunderer.

The most imposing feature of Wirth Bros 1
huge show and zoo is the finished quality of the
work. Every act has been polished and ,7
polished, and the performances go so smootliv
that people do not realise that it is only the sk i
of the artists that prevents a succession of startlin'
tragedies. Consider what might happen if trail "
Marco just for once failed to keep up the artio'
grace with which he looks down the lion’s ne
and examines the back of the animal’s wisdtm,
teeth. Then there are many possibilities „j
broken necks and other things about the Flyin-
Herberts’ turn. It is one thing to fall into
a net by premeditation, and quite another
to flop into it unexpectedly after missing &
flying trapeze in mid-air. Sometimes, also, the
net proves ineffectual, and the falling sky-rocket
goes clean through it. Then there are more
dread possibilities if little May Martin should
forget how to untie herself. This truly mar-
vellous youngster, although seemingly a baby
still, is quite an old hand at Wirths’, and easily
upholds the twisting supremacy of the white race
over the yellow, which is, in this case, repre-
sented by Sampei Osado. Osado twists to such
purpose that, although he starts brown, he is a
dull purple when he finishes. What the writer
mostly wonders is what would happen if some-
one punctured him justashe got his head and shoul-
ders back over his own hind-quarters and nicely
between his own knees. The desire to puncture
him is overwhelming and fascinating, and the deed
itself is inevitable. Philip Wirth has something
extra-special in his two greys, King Edward and
Emperor William (they ought to have been called
Billjim and Cornstalk, as they were both reared
in N.S.W.). They go through their turn with the
smooth precision of a land agent charging a client
half-a-million. The King is a wonder; he does
the white horse rampant, just as it appears on
Tooth’s beer label, and as it used to float on the
flags of Hengist and Horsa; and he does it
perfectly. Philipina, the lady bare-back rider
and somersaulter, is the embodiment of reck-
lessness. In most circus performances the
horse goes at a very easy pace. Philipina’a
gallops, and that graceful young woman hurls
herself at the brute. Sometimes she lands and
sticks, other times she goes right over, somer-
saulting so as to land on her feet, and a horsey
population goes quite mad. Hugh Patrick Lloyd
seems to have an endless variety of tricks for the
elastic cord, and some of them have blood-curdling
possibilities. Altogether, Wirths’ Circus, which
has always been a good show, is now better than
ever.
** * *
At Sydney Royal “ Robin Hood ” is in its
last fortnight, though not on its last legs. It
showed no sign of being winded as it turned the
corner into the straight, and on present appear-
ances it will finish quite fresh, and without turning
a hair, and full of running, and not bathed in
perspiration at all.
*# * *
At Sydney Criterion Jimmy Ryan’s com-
pany filled in a two-nights interval with a produc-
tion of “East Lynne.” This wet, melancholy
upheaval, which outrages all the proprieties
by having two long, lingering deaths
in one evening, was rendered still more
humorous by a gentleman with a pro-
nounced Irish brogue who played the part of
Archibald Carlyle. He played it excellently—as
excellently as any man could play such a dreary
affair, and the brogue gave a bright relief to
a part which is a calamity. However, Satur-
day night was the last of “East Sin,”
and the company will, by the time this
reaches the public, be in full blast with “ For the
Woman He Loves.” As this paper went to press
a day earlier than usual on account of holiday
exigencies, it had no chance of seeing what he
did, or how he did it, or why he loved her. These
mysteries may be cleared up in next issue.

The Tivoli has put a holiday face on its
programme by adding the Charlenes and Joseph
Dewhirst. The new Joseph hasn’t yet lived long
enough to develop the streak of unsatisfactoriness
with which Josephs have been afflicted from the
time when Mrs. Potiphar was the judge of the
matter. This Joseph is a boy with a bird
in his throat—a sweet soprano bird that
sings “Killarney” to the gallery and brings
it down every night. A remarkable voice, w'hieh
makes you sad, because you know it can’t long
stay where it is. The Charlenes, Mr. and Mrs.,
bring juggling back among the fine arts; and
Fred Russell’s coster continues to make the same
rude remarks to his ventriloquist boss, to the
great joy of the man who is fresh down from
where the great natural resources grow.

THE PANTOMIME.
Take a pound of potted prose
And a pint of purling rhyme ;
For the part of Mephistos,
Let the Muse move on in a statelier time.
Pluck a joke from here and there,
Add a quip on current things,
And when there’s a space on the bill of fare,
Drop the lady in who sings ;
Then nail a tack in the pound of prose
(And any old tack will do),
And she’ll hang a song as she wears her clothes,
And Heaven only knows what she hangs them to
(And Heaven don’t tell half the things it knows,
Which is good for us and you).
Then you take the fay with the well-trained toe,
And set her in the whirl that the baldheads know—
Heigh-ho !
(If we’re fifty-five—well, it’s but the flesh ;
The spirit’s twenty, and still a-thresh.)
Take a yard of calico ;
Take a brush and painter’s pot;
Thus mask a man, and so
He masquerades as the ass he’s not.
(Ah, this grey old world of ours,
And lined with furrows of care !
It’s the ass’s skin that has sapped its powers—
The shams it has had to wear !)
But—ah! tripping toes, and the lights burn high—
Nay, tempt us not from Truth !
We’ll write thee an ode, 0 Truth—by-and-bye.
For the present, say was ever a youth
With curves so grateful, so good to the eye ?
(What is fifty-five forsooth !)
Nay, Truth, we love thee, without alloy,
But why won’t you learn from the pantomime
“boy”?
He-hoigh !
(Fifty-five, you say? Nay, we’re all in our prime
When the fairies wheel in the pantomime.)

souls, hymn-howlers and dance-devotees. Never-
theless, the “Parsifal” playwright has failed,
except fipm a spectacular point of view, to score
an artistic success. The thought of the box office
has been his undoing, and has led him to spoil
his sermon by dallyings with the rosy flesh
and rounded limbs of lightly-clad ladies, and
to dishearten his devilment by scriptural
screed and snatches of sacred song. In
the matters of scenery, wardrobe, and stage
mechanism, “ Parsifal ” is so magnificently
equipped as to be able to ignore any accusations
of insincerity, impropriety, sanctimoniousness,
and cruel lack of artistic value. The earthquake
is the best effect of the kind seen in Melbourne.
* * * *
Ridding the “ Pars’ fal” parable of its
quaint allegorical mask, and putting the inci-
dents into modern form, the tale might be told as
follows: Kundry, a beautiful culprit, loses her
heart to the chief elder of a religious community
which lives at (say) Mt. Macedonor Moss Vale,
and spends its time in Ritualistic services. For
love of her respectable admirer, the beauty wishes
to turn over a new leaf, but suffers from relapses,
when she laughs hysterically at the dull, churchy
ways of the settlement and its King. Also she
receives letters and telephone messages from her
former pals in the Gilded Hells of Melbourne or
Sydney inviting her back to the glad old frisk. One
day Kundry accepts the invitation, to the scandal
of the good men of Lome or Bowral, especially
the elderly ones who, having been taken down by
Kundry’s mamma in their youth, are now in a
position to say, “I told you so,” to the lorn and
lamenting lover. Latter is rash enough to follow
his siren by train to her gay haunts, taking with
him (in the luggage van) the “sacred spear,”
which means youth, good health, and all the
virtues. Kundry, instigated by her low associates,
puts on the thinnest of clingsome white robes, and
in this guise welcomes her mountaineer. As a
result, the demoralised youth takes to alcohol
and opium, loses his money, health, and spirits,
and is not rescued by a deputation of
church members till he has become a wreck.
Contrary. to expectation, his health of mind
and body is not restored by Omeo or Mount Vic-
toria air, partly because he misses his beer, partly
because of Kundry, who, though warned off the
premises, keeps hanging round with sago and
jelly for the invalid. Then a boyish curate,
arriving at Bogong (the place is getting mixed) to
shoot swans, and promises to do what he can to
reform Kundry, and to bring back the spear to her
disconsolate hypochondriac, Rev. Parsifal J. E.
Percival. Now the scapegrace goes to Lilly
Buck-stleet,. and is duped by the hussy’s push
into believing that one pure kiss from a
clergyman will bring the minx to her senses.
Bold girls do shocking dances round poor
little Percy while he stands glaring at a crucifix
held opposite the end of his nose. In spite of
Kundry’s audacious costumes and high kicking,
the kid remains calm, and imprints a fatherly
kiss on the young person’s brow. Just then Aus-
tralia passes through the comet’s tail, and the
Lilly Buck-stleet establishment is dashed into
ruins, only Kundry and his Reverence escaping
with their lives. The prodigal is now repentant,
but before Mr. Percival takes her to the Gipps-
land Lakes, near Bombala, he puts her into the
deaconess’ home, where she stumbles and slips on
the straight path, and faints by the way to re-
spectability owing to the poor thing’s hankering
for champagne, and cigarettes, and fierce red
frocks with no sleeves and very little skirt. At
last her morbid cravings are conquered, and her
spiritual adviser escorts her to Katoomba and
marries her to her lover, who hands over his rule
of the community to Percy and decides to do
mission work (Missions were called “Quests” in
those days) in Lilly Buck-stleet with Kundry.
All the spinsters, who have been decorating the
chapel and wearing away their youth waiting for
the bachelors of the community to perk up again
after the loss of the “ spear,” get married at last,
and only Mr. Percival remains unwed.
* * * *
Miss Titfcell Brune does excellent work as
Kundry, though neither physically nor otherwise
is she cutout for the part. Her most attractive
performance is a simple little recitation at the end,
addressed to the prodigal daughters of to-day.
Kingston is weak as Parsifal, his happiest
moments being those spent as the simple wander-
ing youth of his first scene. Vivian Edwards, as
the Sick King of Grail Mount, has some good
lines to say, notably when they raise him
to his feet to try if the change of posture gives
him any relief. Gaston Mervale cuts a superb and
impressive figure as the high priest of Satanas,
while Miss Susie Vaughan also gives valuable aid
to the picture. Atholwood tosses tempestuous
grey locks as an ancient King, and Beauchamp’s
old knight is an estimable item in a glamorous
spectacle.
** * *
Last week’s mention of the forthcoming
Grand Opera season, at Melbourne Princess’s,
erred in its reference to the scale of prices.
Admission to the d.c. and reserved stalls will cost
seven and sixpence (not ten shillings) for the
evening performances, whilst the matinee prices
remain as usual. “ Lohengrin ” opens the season
on Saturday next, and three more performances of
that stately work of Wagner are to transpire
during, the first week, two pairs of principals
appearing alternately in the parts of Elsa and her
superhuman young man. “Romeo and Juliet,”
on Tuesday and Thursday, will introduce one
other prima donna on one of the nights, and the
matinee bill for Saturday (6th April) will be
“ Hansel and Gretel.” There are sound reasons
for expecting good things of George Musgrove’s
grand opera forces in all respects, and. from the
uttermost depth of its moderately musical soul,
this journal hopes that general gratification will
wait upon expectation, and everything in the
garden will be lovely.

The Anderson company is breathing its
last at Melbourne Royal to-night (Wednesday),
and The Squatter’s Daughter ”is receiving its
farewell breath unless the management has made
other arrangements since this Holy Week edition
of The Bulletin went prematurely to press.
“Camille was to cough herself away on
Monday ; “East Lynne” was to dispose of little
Willie on Tuesday. Between the departure of
Anderson and the return of Bland Holt, a Good
Friday concert will happen at the Royal, and on
Saturday the 12 months’ absence of Bland and
his co. will terminate in a first Melbourne produc-
tion of “Revenge,” written by E. Hill-Mitchelson,
and pronounced with a long burry roll on the
“R.”
** * *
Grade Grahame, late of Sydney Tivoli,
looms large on the Easter bill at the Opera House ;
also Frank Hartley, the “Juggling Schoolboy,” is
a new student at the Rickards academy for youths
of uncertain age. As regards the other features
of the entertainment, it may be remarked, with
gratitude, that Brinn no longer invites the Mel-
bourne populace to earn £5 by lifting a sack of
flour. It was a dull depressing subject for any-
body to handle, and the audience in general
would have preferred an invitation to earn a sack
of flour by lifting £5.
** * *
The Rickards Co. has ended its season at
Adelaide Royal with several performances of that
crude assortment of thrills, “ A Modern Judas,”
and one of the good old Tom Taylor “ Ticket of
Leave Man.” Sidney Howard is more effective
in character-parts than as the conventional hero.
The Comic Opera Company is coming for Easter.
Meanwhile, Rickards’ own house, the Tivoli, is
going strong with a bright variety show.
AT POVERTY POINT.
“ The Kelly Gang ”is breaking out everywhere. In a
Brisbane version, Mary Byrne, alleged to be Ned Kelly’s
sweetheart, is the heroine. Ned strikes an isolated
mining camp, where he becomes known as “ The English-
man,” and performs many acts of chivalry. When the
police scent him, Mary partly walks and partly rides
thither from Melbourne—soo miles or thereabouts—to
warn him. The play is announced as “ the great, thrill-
ing, vivid, Australian, historical drama;” also it is “a
cyclone of comedy.” Seems no reason why it shouldn’t
be called an oratorio, too, and adapted for special use at
Christmas and Easter.
Benno Scherek, back from Europe, popped up unex-
pectedly in Melbourne the other day with the news that
Madame Theresa Carino, the famous pianiste, will soon
be out here.
Claude Leplast.rier, the Sydney sketch artist, completed
last week his 100th consecutive performance at Sydney
Tivoli—a fine record made possible by the extensive
r ange of his sketch subjects.
• “Melb”: No Australian drama ever caught on here
like “The Squatter’s Daughter.” The play is part of the
Exhibition of Australian Manufactures. Nevertheless it
is a very inferior affair, merely coopered and carpentered
up out of the salmagundi of Andersonian dramas. It is
much below “The Sunny South,” “The Forlorn Hope,”
“ The Squatter,” “The Trump Card,” and others com-
pou ided by George Darrell. It is nothing like so good as
what Walter Cooper used to give Sydney and Mel-
bourne. Julian Thomas (the Vagabond!) had his “No
Mercy ” acted, and it was many cuts above Bert
Bailey and Edmund Duggan. Then there were such
things as the various versions of “Robbery Under Arms,”
and “His Natural Life,”andGrosvenorßunster’s “Class,”
which was acted at Melb. Royal. Former Australian
plays were only regarded with curiosity, like Barnum’s
faked mermaid.
UNDESIRED PROTECTION.
We are trying to protect the Commonwealth.— Argus.
The absence of the Johnnie type of hanger-on was
noted by everybody on the occasion of the departure of
Williamson’s pantomime crowd from Melbourne. The
co. had changed its duds, bundled its belongings into a
bag, and galloped for the special, timed to leave at mid-
night-just one hour after Tuesday night’s performance
—and it arrived in a hot and dishevelled condition.
All the circumstances tended to provoke interest and a
public demonstration, but only the plain kind of regular,
steadfast bloke attended to kiss the chorus good-bye, and
not a single Johnnie boy turned up to hurl a violet at the
insensate train. In point of fact, the swagger youth who
used to dangle about theatres is extinct. All the dang-
ling is done about pubs. now.
“S.”: Since the time of Eve (presumably'), snake-
charming has had its fascinations for the human race.
It suggests meddling with deadly dangers, and any feat
that is likely to cost a life will draw the crowd. I sup-
pose Cleopatra’s turn at Melbourne Opera House is
attractive because the vivid popular imagination invests
it with terrors it does not possess. The snakes used are
as harmless as kittens, but although the not uncommon
idea that a snake exudes slime like a snail is quite false,
snakes are nasty things to handle, and leave a grimy,
greasy track where they have passed several times over a
white surface like Miss Cleopatra. The snake has also a
faint but peculiarly fetid odor, and the sight of a girl
being crawled over by dozens of the sleek, squirming
reptiles is nauseous to most men, who show a tendency
to take drinks while Cleopatra is on. Women, however,
seem to take an unearthly kind of delight in the exhi-
bition. Certainly, Sara Bernhardt is not the only woman
who has inherited Eve’s weakness for serpents.
“M.”: “Mother Goose” stops short of 100 nights,
which was the famous run achieved by “ Tambour
Major” in Melbourne, with Pattie Laverne, Nellie
Stewart, Mervin, Brennir, Harwood and Wallace. So
“ Mother Goose ” has the second run. The third, 80
nights, was that of “Uncle Tom’s Cabin,” at the old
wooden Princess’s. I have not seen the London runs
tabulated. The first great ones were “ Colleen Bawn ”
and “Our American Cousins,” each about 400 nights.
The longest have been “Our Boys” and “ Charlev’s
Aunt.” “Sweet Lavender’’and “The Chinese Honey-
moon” come about next—then “The Mikado,” with "a
heap more money in its two years than in the three of
some others.
“ Melb.” : I drop in from time to time at the A.N.A.
competitions, now going on at the AthenEeum, extending
for the portentous length of from March 4, when Justice
Barton opened them, to April 13th. And one show a
day is not enough. On some days it is both afternoon
and evening, and sometimes the performance is in two
halls at once. lam surprised at the excellence, particu-
larly in the girls’ piano struggle. Yet no woman alive is
in the very first rank as a piano-player. The girl only
becomes a pianola. What awful sacrifices of time ! What
scorn of domestic work 1 Is this the ambition we crave
for our women ? Do competitions encourage race suicide ?
Melba is the noblest woman ever created—that is the
ideal 1 So every girl squeezes and thrusts to get before the
public. You create what I call the Biltong Girl—a jerked-
beef creature, hard in every way. H r aim is to become
a wife without becoming a mother. Bonaparte was right:
“The noblest woman is the one who has the most
children.” She will emerge from other competitions when
the Biltong Girl is forgotten.
“J.A.C.”: Ada Ward, erst actress, now a somewhat
mature Salvation lassie, was very successful here
in “The Woman in Red,” by which play she will
be best remembered. She was a tall, graceful woman,
with a fine auburn mane, attractive rather than beauti-
ful, with splendid eyes and teeth, and a peculiarly win-
ning smile. She was regarded by her associates in the
theatre as what people call “peculiar." Nobody was
surprised at anything she did. Although very sedate and
somewhat reserved in manner, everybody liked her. Of
her kindness of disposition there could be no doubt;
among other deeds of charity, she nursed an old Mel-
bourne actor, Sam Woolfe, through a severe illness—she
had had experience in nursing, having been attached to
an ambulance corps during the Franco-German war.
Among her theatrical exploits was the playing of Romeo
in “ Romeo and Juliet.”
“Melb”:. That pious singing comedian, Charley
Alexander, is here again. He has dropped Torrey, the
dry barracker, and is on his own. Mr. Alexander is
accompanied by his wife, daughter of Mr. Cadbury, the
millionaire cocoa man. When Grattan Guinness was
here, some callow member of the Y.M.C.A. left an
awful joint in the armor for Symes to thrust a
lance into. The Y.M.C.A. gentleman wrote to the
Southern Cross, saying what a happy-looking man
Guinness was, and what a mournful-looking man Jos.
Symes was. Symes came down like a cubic mile of bricks in
the Liberator. Said he : “ Yes, I am, like the Messiah,
a man of sorrows and acquainted with grief, but I am
told that Dr. Guinness is going to marry a young lady
with £29,000. If I had such luck I would look as happy
as he does.”
Post Mason, the popular American baritone, is at
present touring Queensland, supported by a concert and
vaudeville company, and more than satisfactory business
is reported.
A melodrama in a lift was enacted in a
big building in Collins-street, Melbourne, one
afternoon recently, and the gripman and con-
ductor of the lift, a youth of 16, composed the
audience. The actors were three —the wife, the
other fellow, and the husband. The lift picked
up a lady and a gentleman on the ground floor,
ascended, and stopped on the second floor to take
in a second gentleman. Gent. No. 2 stepped in,
the lift shot up again, and the action of the drama
commenced almost in a moment. The youthful
conductor was thrown down in the confusion,
and considerably trodden on ; the lady was
wedged tightly in
a corner, and the
confined space was
full of battle.
When the lift
reached its limit,
the rampant hus-
band yanked the
wire, and started
it down again. By
this time people
had gathered at
each landing to
watch the battle
flying past. It
ended at the
ground floor. No.
2 seized the lady
and took her
away. No. 1 stood on the floor with his nose
bleeding into his hat. Inquiry revealed the fact
that No. 1 had apartments at the top, and was
taking No. 2’s wife up to afternoon tea, when the
strategical No. 2 intercepted them on the first
floor.
Recently dead in London, aged 80, Peter
Paul Labertouche, one-time Victorian Secretary
for Railways. He arrived in Melbourne in 1853,
and became chief clerk in the Roads and Bridges
Department, which was amalgamated with the
Railway ditto. When John Stevenson relin-
quished the Railway Secretaryship, Labertouche
took his place. He retired in 1892, and was
succeeded by Robert G. Kent.
As was previously suggested, Judkins had
one chance of achieving rationality. The chance
was D.C.L. Whisky in instalments suitable to the
smallness of his vital capital. And D.C.L. is still
his best hope of raising himself above the level of
his unhappy name. Properly applied to the right
spot—it will produce sense from the most un-
promising soil. Of all intellectual fertilisers
D.C.L. Whisky is the best.*
ADA’S VOICE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Miss Ada Ward’s reading of the Bible conics as a revela-
tion to those used to hearing- the usual clergyman or
reader drone the Scriptures.— The Age.
I remember, years ago,
When I was a pretty child,
With my golden curls arow—
And my nature undefiled,
Father unto church away
Drove me every Sabbath day.
Oak leaves carved on column caps,
Oak leaves on the steel-bound door—
Cool and gloom in choir and apse,
Shadows on the grey stone floor ;
Heard the organ roll its waves,
As I wagged it 'mong the graves.
Cave-like gloom and organ breath ;
Beauty in that stone and wood ;
Sigh as of regret for death,
From where carven angels stood ;
Kept me studious, rapt, devout—
Till the parson droned me out.
In the choir, where all the maids,
Dressed like angels fleshed with youth
Voices mounting to the shades,
Singing courage, hope and truth.
When the parson’s drone did hurt,
All those girls began to flirt.
I, who always vvas devout,
Driven was to go alone,
Forced into the world without
By the shepherd’s shriek and drone—
With a girl—’twas parson’s fault —
Kittling near the family vault.
And if I be not a soul
Of the saved and timid sort,
Blame that parson, for the roll—
For the nasal bleat—in short,
For his ghastly monotone ;
And his drunken blowfly’s drone.
Drone of Jacob and of Ham—
Bleat of David and of Noah—
Squeak of Tophet and of “ Damn ”
Mispronouncing “ Moab ” and “ Moah,’
Drones reciting yarns of rakes—
Amnon, Tamar and the cakes.
There can be one voice that saves !
Ada’s—when she speaks the verse—
(No more kittling ’mong the graves,
Wagging it from church, and worse)—
Saved by histrionic tone,
Who was lost by parson’s drone.
Yet, I doubt, tho’ Ada’s voice
Called me from the grass and sun,
Other sounds would more rejoice
Me, if life were new begun ;
I would hear some youthful she
Calling, “ Martin 1 Come to me ! ”
Tree of sap-green—ray of sun—
Earth made heavenly by the light—
From the cavern’d church I’d run—
From the drone of hell’s affright—
Where the sun with glory laves
All the grass on all the graves.
Deacon’s whines nor parson’s drones
Should not keep me from my fair ;
Ada’s deep and Thespian tones
Should noo drive me from the air.
Hear the cry from yonder tree !
“ Martin ! Martin 1 Come to me ! ”
Not for me the greater sin—
Slighting Life for church of stones !
Soul of mine they cannot win ;
Nor by bleat, nor “ East Lynne ” tones,
Take my liberty from me—
Joy of sun and air and tree.
When all sectaries are dead,
When their strut and acting’s done,
When all ghouls their say have said,
Still will shine the splendid sun—
No more heard the killjoy’s drone,
Buzzing round life’s beauteous throne.
Queensland.
MARTIN LUTHER.
A GALLANT CLERGYMAN.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Despite the orders given by'the captain that the women
and children were to be saved before the men were
allowed in the boats, two male members of the Salvation
Army and a clergyman were among the first boatload who
left the vessel. —Glad tidings from the Suevic wreck.
’Twas in Trafalgar’s Bay
(Or near by),
Upon that fog-bound day
The stranded Suevic lay ;
Huge waves splashed angry spray
’Gainst the sky.
Firm, at the fatal wreck,
Captain, crew
Kept panic under check.
The Lizard reared its neck.
Like men, girls marched that deck—
Kiddies, too.
The skipper calmly cursed,
Iceberg-cool:
“ Let ocean do its worst,
Let the ship break or burst;
But kids and women first!
Is the rule.”
Wild splashed tumultuous foam,
Rat-ta-tan !
“ Dear friends, seek not to roam.
Stand hack !. .. Heaven is our Home.”
Spoke up brave Brother Loam,
Clergyman.
All hearts his bold words fill
Full of Hope;
As through the crowd, so still,
He stole, clutched Baby Bill,
And pushed him off the sill
Or the rope.
The reckless, first to flee—
Bulldog breed—
That pastor w'as, who the
Rope ladder clasped, as he
Swung safely o’er the sea ;
Gallant deed!
And in the life-boat lists,
Like vile kelp,
Sat Bags and Sergeant Twists,
Both staunch Salvationists —
(Salvation aye consists
In self-help).
Crew, passengers all cheered
Heartfelt, when
All safe that life-boat neared
The shore. The first who cleared
Were (how the sinners jeered !)
These good men.
It was a novel plan
Tried upon
This amateurish clan ;
But for wild dash, elan,
That gallant clergyman
Takes the scone.
VIC.

SOCIETY
PENFOLD'S
WINES
“Hamer”: For sheer monotony, com-
mend me to the evening party. In my younger
days I used to attend a number of these functions
mainly because I was invited and could think of
no adequate excuse, and I met invariably about
the same people—the girl who recited a bit
from Baconspere, and the woman who sang
“Sunshine and Rain,” and the man who
sang “Out on the Rocks,” and the other
man who sang “Island of Dreams,” and the
various people who did the same old things on
the same old instruments, and the same polite
applause when the same impolite conversation
suspended itself with the close of the same old
items. Also the same old refreshments, which
were not particularly refreshing, eaten with the
same old teeth—mostly. The teeth were probably
the most changeable items on the programme.
TAPERING OFF.
HE : “ I beg of you—give up society.”
S HE: “ Very well, I’ll begin with yours.”
Professor Berry, of Melbourne University,
has a grievance against the Bent Government,
which, he says, is most penurious in its dealings
and keeps the dissecting rooms short of bodies.
There are times when the students have nobody
to cut up, and this, in a great city where there
are so many people to spare, is a most reprehen-
sible state of things, Several Melbourne public
institutions, like the Benevolent Asylum and
the Morgue, are supposed to provide cadavers
to the University, but only a casual, disfranchised
corpse, with no relations and no influence, finds
its way there. Dr. Berry does not say definitely
what he expects Bent and his colleagues to do,
but The Bulletin has an idea. It proposes that
Bent and Prendergast should coalesce, the terms
being that the Labor party dissolves its caucus
and accepts the main features of the Bent pro-
gramme ; while Bent, as a graceful concession,
hands over the House of Rich Landlords to the
University to be cut up.
From “ Jimmie Pannikin ” :
Lots oi fittle people have had a big fling at the new
syllabus issued by the Public Instruction Department of
N.S.W., and lots of big people have had their little shot
at it ; now I want to have a short say. One of the wisest
features of a wise syllabus is the encouragement given to
gardening. When I was imprisoned in the Big Scrub, a
decade or two ago, I marvelled, as a fruitgrower’s son, at
the fact that many of the Richmond River cane-growers,
owning blocks of some of the most fertile land in Australia,
used to ride 20 miles or so, week after week, to wild, weird
Wardell, or bright, breezy Ballina, to buy common vege-
tables. Cane is proverbially the lazy man’s crop. Cane-
growing gives lovely spells of rest, just as wool-growing
does, and just as dairy-farming doesn’t ; yet scores of
cane-growers used never to grow their own vegetables.
They’d buy “ greens ” from the local Chow, or get Sydney
stuff at the local greengrocers’ shops. Their own land
was capable of producing much of the finest fruit and
vegetables in the State. They came in time to admire
the Experimental Farm at Wollongbar, and the school-
gardens at Ballina, Wardell, etc.—and to profit by the
example.
Here in the Yass-Gundagai district, I find the same dis-
inclination to cultivate garden-plots on the fertile flats of
the Murrumbidgee. All the land is used for sheep or
cattle or corn. A good vegetable plot is in my experience
a rare thing. I saw better gardening along the Darling
even in drought time. When I ask why vegetables and
melons are not grown, I am usually told that “ there ain’t
no time,” or “it ain’t worth while.” Recently at Jugiong
I saw on the ’Bidgee Flats, the biggest and best rock-
melons and water-melons I ever came across. The enter-
prising grower slept with a loaded two-barrelled gun
beside him ; but he was making money. The man on the
land ought to produce an abundant supply of fruit
and vegetables ior home consumption, and eat less meat.
He should also cultivate flowers. Here’s good luck to the
school gardens. May they grow and grow and grow 1
“ Blank ” : A Mel-
bourne paper says that
Miss Florence Young
lately received through
the post a “ Chain
Prayer,” and wa s
threatened with things
horrific if she didn’t
send the prayer to nine
other persons, starting
on the day she received
said supplication. That
prayer first struck this
station a fortnight ago.
Since then prayers have
simply showered on it.
Dirty and clean prayers,
well-written and unde-
cipherable prayers.
With the prayer was an
intimation that if the
receiver didn’t start
sending copies of it
away at once to nine
persons some great
affliction would, on the
ninth day, smite him or
her hard. If, on the
other hand, the person
did immediately start
sending out the prayer,
he or she would, on the
ninth day thereafter,
experience some great
joy. The chief’s family
(15 in all) each received
two or three copies of
the prayer, and, being
naturally anxious to
avoid the affliction,
started by letting the
book-keeper have one
apiece. The book-keep-
er, who is a most
irreligious person, was
already in receipt of
several prayers. In fact,
everyone on the station
had got one or more
through the post, and
everyone passed one on
to everyone else. * It
was the same old
prayer, with variations
as to spelling and fist,
but each copy con-
tained the threat and
the instructions about
sending it on. We are a simple and super-
stitious community—all but the book-keeper,
and he is an angry man. No one dare ap-
proach him now with a piece of paper. He
stands at his office door with a gun and receives his
instructions from afar. The governess, being
highly strung, has taken to her bed, because she
can’t think of any more people to send the prayer
to. The maids and several of the more super-
stitious and religious of the men have drawn some
screw in advance to buy stamps with which to
send out the prayer. The post office is besieged.
Extra mail bags have been called into use, and
they are full of prayer. Wild, demented women
are ordering stamps from the nearest township,
the station office being sold out. No one has ex-
perienced any joy ; everyone wears a worried
look. lam prostrated—also broke. Would The
Bulletin mind letting me work a few prayers off
on the office ? And is this ‘‘ Oh ain Prayer ” a deep,
dark joke, perpetrated by the P.M.G. with a view
to increasing the revenue ?

“ C.P. : ” I saw a one-act drama last week
from my office window, in Collins-street, Mel-
bourne. A dark, nervous-looking man had
been waiting for some time in evident im-
patience, and scraping the pavement with
worried, prancing hoofs. Then a bright,
fair, plump, serene-looking girl swung round
the corner from Russell-street, and greeted
him with a smile like a sunrise. He replied awk-
wardly, and jigged about some more on his two
worried, shamed-looking hoofs. Her smile faded,
and she looked surprised. He said things with
his eyes looking everywhere except at her face,
and his feet still going on as if they didn’t belong to
him, and her face slowly whitened, and she became
tense and motionless. He gesticulated in awk-
ward explanation, he spoke volubly, he explained
things at great length, he wiped his perspiring
forehead, he looked everywhere except at the girl,
and he almost tore up the pavement with the
scraping of his feet. Still she remained dead still.
Then he awkwardly handed out a bottle of scent.
She took it, and let her hand drop again among
the folds of her dress. He got away awkwardly,
taking his hat off twice, and dabbing it on again
real hard.; and when he was gone the girl dropped
the bottle of scent in the gutter, and watched the
fragments roll away in the turbid stream. Then
she took off her glove, slowly drew off a ring, and
dropped it also. By this time the nervous man
was a hurrying blot far down the street. The
whole thing was obvious, barring the scent. Why
should a mean-looking man, who was obviously
breaking off his engagement, bring along six-
penn’orth of cheap perfume ?

If your muscles ache it may be your blood
—Wolfe’s Schnapps cures rheumatism. If you
need a bracing tonic for general exhaustion,
Wolfe’s Schnapps is the best in the world.*
N.S.W. law fell with unusual weight on
Kathleen Dwyer, of Sydney, when it gave her
and Joseph Dwyer three years apiece for black-
mail ; but though Kathleen is only making slow
progress through a two-years’ sentence, and the
Court’s liberality thus gives her five years in all,
it can’t be said that she doesn’t deserve all she has
got. Kathleen was the nice-looking nurse who
conspired with Daisy Greville, also a nice-looking
nurse, and the aforesaid Joseph Dwyer, to get
large damages from Benjamin for alleged assault.
Benjamin had hired the two ministering angels
to flap their wings officially in his wife’s sick
room, and the after-proceedings cost him a heap
of damages, costs and publicity. And he would
never have got any satisfaction out of the
matter but for the fact that the two ministering
angels afterwards got two years each for false
pretences. Kathleen took hers dry, but Daisy
wept greatly, and before she had been an hour in
her cell the lonesomeness of it had so got on her
nerves that she had to speak to somebody.
Merely, it would seem, for the sake of the Judge’s
company she got herself taken back into Court
and told the whole story r of the anti-Benjamin
conspiracy. It did her no good, so far as
can be seen, but it seems to have struck Kathleen
Dwyer just where some solid damage was most
required. Also, it clears Benjamin’s character as
far as the false charge of battering a nurse is
concerned, but it doesn’t bring back the damages
and Costs. The angels spent the damages long
ago, and Costs never come back.

The action of the owners of the recently
lost Carnarvon Castle, in stopping the pay of the
survivors as from the date of the wreck, is another
illustration of the blessings and benefits of Private
Enterprise. Think of the, consideration and
charity of those gentlemen ini not even charging
the castaways for the rent of the boats in which
they reached shore —and not a word has been said
about the value of the bisbuits which the men
admit taking from the vessel!

Incidentally the Herald reverently referred to
Ranji as “ His Jamsbip,” and dragged jn by the

“Thomas Clodd” from Maoriland
Maoriland’s boss geyser, Waimungu, has sullenly
refused to blow up since Seddon died, but the accumu-
lated missuses of the unstable isles at present make
Waimungu s finest turn appear a pale penny fizzle. The
lately-formed Domestic Workers’ Union has served upon
every missus in Wellington a sheetful of claims that
simply play Antiquated Henry with the wives’ tenderest
feelings. The said wives are commanded by the political
woman who drew up the circular and made Mary revolt,
to sign an agreement herewith enclosed, accepting the
demands of the 18 downtrodden slaves who have decided
to increase the political woman’s salary. The circular
says the signing of the agreement will “ save unpleasant-
ness.” In the meantime, the missuses aren’t signing
anything, which means that they will all be sum-
moned before the Arbitration Court and browbeaten
by the polit.cal woman. Mary, to be quite frank,
is biting off a great deal more than she was formerly
asked to masticate, for the claims say she shall begin to
work at 6.30, knock off at 7.30 in the evening - , knock off
at 2 p.m. on Thursdays and Sundays, but shall, if re-
quired, help get tea on Sunday evening every fortnight.
Other “claims” state that all the sta utory holidays
shall be observed—by the girls, not the missuses ; that
Mary shall staj' out till 12 (midnight) on her off-day ;
unionists shall have preference, and uniform be free. If
Mary is asked to work on a statutory or weekly holi-
day, she claims Is. an hour overtime. But the point
that really charms the M.L. missus is the one that makes
it lawful for an inspectress to invade the kitchen to see
if she is doing the right thing by Mary. Inspectresses
are getting their hair glued on.

Harry Thaw, the United States millionaire
degenerate who blew Stanford White into a
future state, becomes more interesting with each
succeeding batch of revelations. In addition to
the fact that his knees wobble, and his feet cross
each other like those of Kipling’s “Man who
Would be King” after his crucifixion, and that he
is suspected of having a tail, it is now stated that
he always stripped his young wife before he
cowhided her. He seems to have done this on
several occasions, but the first time it happened,
if the lady’s story is to be believed, the matter
was attended to in a very thorough fashion, for
she says she was in bed for three weeks after the
ceremony. But there are so many aspects of the
Thaw case, and the girl-wife seems to have
whooped things up so energetically in her brief
career, that a cloud of doubt hangs over the whole
business.

From one who saw them off :
The fact of Tommy Bent and Reginald Talbottle depart-
ng on the same boat led to complications in the farewell
demonstration. It was difficult to say which part of the
public emotion was Bent’s and which was Talbottle’s, so
they agreed to lump it, and both hastened to acknow-
ledge every whoop that went up from the crowd. They
even came two steps fonvard, and swept the decks with
their cadies when a roar was excited by the outbreak of
hostilities between two terriers on the pier. In truth, very
little of the demonstration was intended for the vice-
regal person. He was scarcely recognisable by the
average Melbournian, to whom he has been merely a tall,
vague official shadow discovered occasionally in news-
paper illustrations approving of a prize bull at an
agricultural show, or planting a spring onion on Arbor
Day. The band played “ Will Ye No Come Back Again?”
and the Governor nodded “ yes,” bub the crowd displayed
no overwhelming joy at the prospect, and devoted itself
to the efforts of a lank lady who was trying to hit Bent
with a bouquet. Bent kept dry nearly to the end, but
he broke up and wept when the people raised a farewell
cheer. There was no attempt on the Hon. Tom’s part to
disguise his weakness; he wept openly, mopping up
tears as big as grapes in a voluminous bandana, and the
crowd, which dearly loves a soggy bit of sentiment, see-
ing the big man so deeply moved, cheered more than
ever. There is a suspicion abroad that Tommy was
“ kidding,” bub this writer, who has seen him snivelling
wretchedly over the woes of an Andersonian heroine,
d >es not believe that for a moment. Too solid flesh dis-
solve s into tears easily, and T. Bent is very solid.

The Sydney Observatory site, which the
Carruthers Government, in its sour desire to in-
convenience the Australian Parliament, proposes
to present to J. Bull for Mint purposes, was origi-
nally known as Fort Phillip, and had a battery
and signal station, communicating with the signal
station at South Head. On the ridge, at one
time, were three windmills. The Grosvenor
Hotel was about the site of one, the second was
close up to the present Fort-street school, and
the third close to the fort itself. They were
Government mills, grinding corn for the military
and the prisoners.
“ STOP IT, MISS, OR I’LL SCREAM
Melbourne is solving’
the domestic maid
problem by the
employment
of house-
boys and
house-men
in private
houses and
boarding
establish-
ments.
Here we
have the
wicked
elderly
lady
chucking
the house-
boy under

Melbourne College of Domestic Economy
is now in full blast. Passionate orators, turned on
to enthuse about the institution, describe it as an
establishment for the creation and cultivation of
the perfect housewife, just as the Agricultural
Colleges are for the propagation and formation
-of the perfect husbandman. The College of
Domestic Economy, we are told, will teach its
students all a complete housewife should know.
This seems a large programme, and the announce-
ment is creating a good deal of uneasiness in the
minds of Melbourne men, married and engaged.
The question is, how much should the complete
housewife know ? Married women will probably
reply- “Just as much as her husband doesn t
want her to be aware of.” There is an impression
abroad that one branch of study deals with the
proper control and management of husbands, and
is presided over by a lady Professor of 50 who
has buried two husbands and is now training a
third for the sepulchre. This class is likely to up-
set a lot of illusions, and if persisted in, will tend
to subvert the real intentions of the College of
Domestic Economy, since the wise man, while
willing that his intended should know enough, is
quite anxious that she should not know too much.

BALLADE OF A WIDOW.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
A WIDOW, elderly, active, Christian, alone, owning
three cottages, clear of debt, wishes introduction
elderly Christian Man, similar circumstances, view matri-
mony.—Melbourne Aon.
Elderly, active, Christian, alone,
Owning three cottages (clear of debt);
Widowers, who have had wives of your own,
How much longer is she to fret ?
Are not your seared hearts softened yet?
Hear her plead in a gentle tone—
“ Christian Man, whom I have not met,
List to the wail of a Widow lone ! ”
“ Elderly.” Ay ! but do not let
Fancy picture some ugly crone,
Grey and withered, with cap and net,
Mumbling prayers in a dreadful drone.
.Still in her there is something to pet—
All her beauty has not yet flown—
“ Active ” means “ bouncing,” do not forge f,
List to the wail of a Widow lone !
“ Christian.” Ah ! let the word be blown
Far as the bounds of the world are set ;
Her affinity is not known,
But he’s a “ Christian ” —so we may bet!
What a prize he is going to get!
Flesh of his flesh and bone of his bone —
Dustman, or duke with a page in Debrett,
List to the wail of a Widow lone !
Envoi.
Bachelors, widowers—come, be met;
Chances like this are not widely strown.
Think of those cottages (clear of debt)—
List to the wail of a Widow lone !
VIC.
O. C. CABOT.
THE FEAR OF THE LORD!
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Cable item on the wreck of the Sue vie : “Mr. W.
Young, of Melbourne, and other passengers declare that,
despite the Captain’s orders to the contrary, when the
rescue work started the first boat-load contained two
male Salvationists and a clergyman ! ”
“Behold the fear of the Lord, that is wisdom!”
Job xxviii., 28.
The terrified trio looked down at the boat,
A pang at each heart-string, a clutch at each throat!
How long would the vessel continue afloat?
Might possibly sink in a second !
The skipper commanded in resolute tone
That men of all ranks, not the seamen alone,
Must wait till the women and children were gone !
A terrible risk it was reckoned !
Three craven hearts quavered in common accord
With shameless, contemptible fear of the Lord !
“ Stand back ! ” cried the skipper. The shivering three,
Three cowardly Christians all anxious to flee,
Unheeding the order appeared to agree
To rush the first boat in their terror !
Their faith in religion was merely a name !
Their dread of their Heaven had conquered their shame ;
They might have shown pluck and been saved all the same !
Oh that was the pitiful error !
This happening seems to the scribe to afford
A deplorable instance of fearing the Lord !
N.S.W.
W. T. GOODGE.

Nowadays at the bush picnic it is not un-
common for half the girls to bear “Jubilee” as
the whole or part of a front name. It is a desig-
nation that is coming home to roost. Jubilee is
aged 19 or 20 now. Five years ago she was still
at school, and the fact that her name gave away
her age didn’t matter. To-day she is in long
frocks and has her hair up, but she is still
young enough for her name not to matter. By
1912 the unmarried section of Jubilee will begin
to get worried; by 1917 Miss Jubilee will be a
miserable old girl, and by 1924 she will be an
anguished frump. She will be dated all the time,
and her little strivings to conceal the procession
of years will count for nothing. It was the same
with the fisrt crop of girls named Alma. Alma
didn’t mind from 1872 till about 1876; then she
began to worry as no husband loomed on the
horizon ; and in 1884 she bought a parrot and a
cat. But she was better off than Jubilee. The
name of Alma lingers, and an occasional girl is
still christened by it, therefore Alma isn’t in-
delibly branded “1854.” Jubilee is a name that
lasted one year only, and Miss Jubilee Smith
might just as well have her date burned into her
shoulder, as if she were a heifer, so that the public
can see it when she is in evening dress at the
festive hop.

The Sydney thug now hits you hard on
the elbow while you are waiting at the corner for
a tram car, and then publicly goes through your
p ickets while your thoughts are concentrated on
the smote bone. The bone attracts your atten-
tion so much that you don’t really notice that you
are being robbed. In fact, you might be un-
dressed and your clothes taken awuxy without the
fact attracting your attention at the moment, so
deeply do you ponder about that bone.

The blowing up of the French warship
Jena, in dock at Toulon, shows the trickiness of
high explosives. The French use melinite, a
variant of lyddite—a compound of phenol boiled
with nitric and sulphuric acids. Under the name
of picric acid, it had been in use as one of the
coal-tar dyes for 40 years without doing anything
remarkable. Then, one fine day, a fire broke out
in some chemical works where it was largely
manufactured. A stream of blazing picric acid
ran down a gutter in the yard. It ran into a shed
where there was a heap of litharge (a lead com-
pound). Then there was a tremendous blow up.
I hat was in the early nineties, and was the first
hint that picric acid was explosive. Its useful-
ness has developed since ; but it is notoriously as
capricious as a young woman in a new bonnet.
Some of Melbourne’s registry-office keepers
think they are on the way to solve the Mary Ann
problem. For a while past they have supplied
“houseboys” as fast as they can get them, and
want more. The “ boys ” run from 16 years to 22,
or thereabouts, and engage as cooks, launders,
house-and-chamber coves, and afewas “generals.”
Wages run from 12s. upwards and “found.”
Meanwhile Mary Ann has gone into a factory or
an office, and for 15s. a week does the work that
the man used to get 30s. or 40s. for. How the idea
of “service” strikes the “ boy” hasn’t transpired
yet; perhaps it never will, for a hash-house or pub
“boots” doesn’t reckon he is “in service” —he
contracts to do certain work for certain wages
within stipulated hours, and nothing more or
outside his agreement; and as “houseman” he
will regard his work similarly. Also, nine women
out of ten show more consideration to men
servants than they do to women, so there’s no
reason why the plan shouldn’t be satisfactory.
But what is to happen to the “ boy ” when he is
25, and hasn't learned anything except how to
fold nighties, make beds, and clean windows ?
Presumably after that age he will drift into the
job of general rouseabout and yardman —and
Heaven save us from becoming a nation of hope-
less general rouseabouts and yardmen !

“ The Bindaei Referring to your par in
Bulletin (14/3/’O7), let me have a word. Sup-
pose the Bell River had a fresh in it, could you
reasonably call the Macquarie a branch thereof ?
If not, how can the line from Nyngan to Bourke
be the branch because there is more traffic on the
Cobar line? Talking of the railway, there are
dumb witnesses (nailed round the walls of the big
loco.-shed at Bourke) to the biggest drunk that
the town, perhaps, ever saw. The line was
opened there on the 3rd September, 1885, and the
floor of the loco.-shed —a huge building to stable
about a dozen engines, but now mostly stabling
some thousands of rabbits—was boarded over and
all Bourke danced, drank champagne, ate the
fatted pig, and generally painted the West ver-
milion for days in the presence and company of
Governor Loftus. The aforementioned witnesses
are palms brought from Sydney for decoration
purposes, and there they hang to-day—about the
only things the old inhabitant has to remind him
of the first step Bourke took to its own destruction.
Houses are empty, pubs deserted, and desolation
reigns supreme ; and as to Bourke losing its head
over the matter of its 126 miles of rails being
called a branch, it is questionable whether there
is head enough to get lost if the said rails were
torn up and sold to Sandford, and the village was
re-named Gehenna.

In a little hotel at Hauwiti (M.L.) the
other day some strolling tourists had their atten-
tion “respectfully directed ” to a notice, written
in Maori and English (more or less) on a sheet of
beer and sweat stained notepaper. The English
(more or less) part of it read as follows :
A NOTICE TO MAORIS AND PAKEHAS.
A dance is going' to be held here, in Hauwiti’s ball, on
the 19th of this month at 7 o’clock p.m. The main
things of this dance is to buy stoom stones. Dancer 55.,
Spot 25., Ladys Free.
The effete pakeha sometimes has the bad habit of
dancing on his ancestors’ tombstone ; the Maori
dances first and afterwards buys his ancestor a
tombstone with the proceeds—helped by the Spct,
which is Maori shorthand for spectator.

“A.M.”: Saw two men in Pitt-street,
discussing: forcibly the question, “Are Sydney
streets dirty?” Evidently neither would be
convinced. Then one, in eloquent silence, crossed
the pavement, and with his umbrella poked the
thick coating of dirt which, from year’s end to
year’s end, makes a cornice along the wall of the
Herald office ; and the other, who looked like a
friend of “Mr. James ” himself, sorrowfully
bowed his head in admission. Never was argu-
ment settled more conclusively.

“ T.F.M.” : In these days Pentridge
Stockade (Melbourne) may be all that Tommy
Bent says it is—a quiet family hotel, where every
attention is paid to the comfort of its patrons;
but, having occasion recently to hunt through its
records, I became convinced that it was a good
place to keep away from in the days when John
Price was, according to his own irreverent dictum,
“ the God of Pentridge.” A sample sentence :
Isaac Hurst, charged with taking his boots off dis-
respectfully. Twenty days’ solitary confinement on bread
and water. (Signed) Richard Youl.
The visiting Jay-Pay who handed out justice in
this generous fashion was Dr. Youl, who became,
in after years, a well-known Melbourne identity.

The proposal of Carruthers to cut a wide
avenue from Macquarie-street through the Sydney
Domain to the Art Gallery deserves the same fate
as the old Lyne proposal to cut a carriage road
around the water front of the Botanic Gardens.
It will take away a certain portion of the park
land, and is of no particular use, anyhow.

“ Hassan ” : I cordially endorse “Paul
Cupid’s” remarks (B. 14/3/’O7) anent the subtle
country person. But why did he leave out the
wiliest of the bunch ? Pa Hayseed’s clever ex-
hibits of wheat and chaff and Ma’s disingenuous
preserves are mere trifles compared to Girlie’s
“ art ’’ exhibits. I have frequently judged in this
section, and am a confirmed cynic upon
the subject of the innocent country girl.
One trick is to paint in oils over a colored
magazine supplement. The result enters
as a “Figure subject, copy.” A similar
trick is resorted to in the black and white
section. I saw, in one show, a Norman Lindsay
drawing cut out of The Bulletin, and manipu-
lated by going over every line with a pen. Nine
out of 10 of the black and white “copies” are
tracings. I also saw a copy of George Lambert’s
“Black Soil Plains” shown as an “original
animal subject,” and, after the judging, was in-
formed by tlie young lady who perpetrated the
crime that she had got three firsts for the same.
I apologised profusely, and said that if I had
known that it might have made a difference.
Who was the judge who had given the prizes?
With tear-dimmed eyes, Girlie informed me that
it was the local Bobby Magistrate.
* * * *
Those who want a lesson in municipal
Socialism, and at the same time a proof of the
advantages of municipal consolidation, should go
to the top of Melbourne Exhibition dome or the
Fire Brigade tower some dark night and let the
street lights preach to them. The whole metro-
politan area lies stretched out like a huge map of
municipal competence. Carlton, North Mel-
bourne, West Melbourne, East Melbourne —all
the suburbs governed by the city corporation, and
served by its municipal lighting station, are
brilliant with electric light. The long lines of
glittersomeness are only streets, but they look
like paths of radiance. Fitzroy, Collingwood,
Kew are black blotches to the north-east, through
which Tait’s dim trains move like angels of light.
Over to the south of the Yarra there are the
domains of the Australian Electric Light and
Traction Co. There are lights south of the Yarra,
but scattered and faint and poverty-stricken in
appearance. Still even these patches mark some
stage of municipal endeavor. South Melbourne
is murky. Port Melbourne lies in utter brown
gloom. Yet the municipalities hesitate and
quibble, and take referendums, and get violently
agitated when the City Council invites them to
come in and help to make a bigger and better-lit
and more cheaply-run Greater Melbourne. They
prefer to wander home in the dark, tripping over
their badly-laid kerb-stones, and falling into holes
in their ill-kept roads, so long as they have their
local council, and their own Mr. Mayor, and their
“civic individuality,” whatever that may mean.
“ F.L.J.” : Am staying with a fairly
prosperous N.S.W. dairy-farmer. His wife and
daughters worried him, and he in turn worried the
member for the district, and the member worried
the Government, with the result that Mr. Cow was
made a J.P. The eldest girl was in Brisbane
when the great event occurred, and arrived home
a few weeks afterwards. Seated at breakfast the
morning after her arrival, she noticed that the
fare was similar to that before Dad was “ele-
vated.” It hurt her under the pinny. Standing
up she sniffed at the atmosphere, and left us with :
“ A nice breakfast for a J.P.’s daughter to have
to sit down to ! ” Only one person could equal
her—George Rignold when he was Henry V.

Charity takes some strange forms in these
days of advertising. George Jeffkins lost one of
his feet through an unfortunate accident in a
Footscray (Vic.) quarry. By a simple form of co-
operation he was enabled to secure an artificial
limb, and writes this letter of thanks :
1 wish to convey my sincere thanks to the public of
Footscray who so kindly collected the Havelock tobacco
tags (25,000) for me, which enabled me to apply for the
artificial limb offered by the Havelock Tobacco Co. Also
to return my thanks to the Havelock Tobacco Co. for
their handsome and useful limb, and the kind and generous
treatment 1 received at their hands.
* * * *
“ Honest Sweat ” : Have recently come to
Dunedin (Maoriland) to live, and find it pre-
cipitous. The bulk of the city is on the hills ; and
the way up is so steep that the noble horse can’t
pull even an empty cart up straight, but has to
zig-zag from side to side at a slow walk. In
Stafford-street, where my diggings hang on to the
wall of the hill, an express-man isn’t game to back
his vehicle square up against the kerb —the horse
would fall over sideways if he did. Even dogs zig-
zag up the Dunedin hills. Some of the footpaths
have got steps cut in the asphalt. One cross
street near my show is grown green with grass
through disuse. Another healthy feature about
this city is the time everybody has lived here.
Thirty years is the minimum length of service
that counts for anything in the way of recom-
mendation. Have spoken to lots of prominent
and ordinary men, and it’s a rare event to find
anything with white whiskers and with a bit of
boodle that hasn’t been here 40 years. Another
feature is the difficulty I experience in finding
anyone who knows a better place than Dunedi na
outside the Bible ; and even Heaven hasn’t got —
First Church and a new mud dredge.
