THE MODEST LOVER.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
TXONORABLE young GENTLEMAN, quiet, indus-
trious, scant means, would like to correspond
good-looking, healthy, affectionate, sensible Chrisl ian
girl, about 20, with means, view above. Triflers
refrain. Address W.W., G.P.O. —Argus Matrimonial
Advt.
I seek not first-sight love, nor.peerless beauty ;
Only a healthy, wholesome, Christian girl
Who’ll save my scanty means as her first duty.
Not for an eyebrow, nor a golden curl,
Do I give up my bachelor condition ;
Only a girl, described above, to gain ;
Affectionate ; with means for the position—
All other applications are in vain !
Triflers refrain 1
Note—l am honorable, industrious, quiet,
And do not wish a burning passion near.
Good looks I want—the health for any diet—
Christian and sensible must be my dear.
Scant means have I, but My Queen must have plenty ;
Note that I say “ with means” —prospective dove 1
Healthy, and wealthy, fair, and young, and twenty—
Give me but these, and I’ll dispense with love,
With “view above.”
Comely and wholesome, sensible and healthy,
Her faults I’ll face with Christian fortitude ;
Good-looking and religious, fairly wealthy—
I care not if she have a wayward mood—
Twenty and fair—-her love need not be burning
(My Rechabitish blood is cold as rain),
And if there ain’t no cash, there ain’t no yearning ;
Bethink thee, ladies who are poor, ’tis vain !
Triflers refrain !
VIC.
RANDOLPH.

Westralia .. 14,862,287 3,196,266
Tasmania .. 2,284,031 7,516,600
On the basis of the best year from a revenue point of
view that Australia has probably ever had, the second
column above shows the debt that must be paid off
before the works, &c., represented by loan money will
pay interest. And that is exclusive of sinking fund.
If a sinking-fund contribution is called for, as
it ought to be, then the amount of unpro-
ductive debt is much larger. Of course, no
sane commercial concern would look at things
from the height of its most prosperous year, and
if it proposed to float itself into a company on that basis
it would soon have to write down its capital, presuming
that the public didn’t treat the prospectus with scorn, as
the public would most likely do. Yet the foregoing
is the best the States’ loans will pan out at, even on that
basis; and the second column in the last table above shows
how much at the very least must be paid off loans out of
revenue before the books are square.
* » * *
As anticipated here some weeks ago, the Bank
of New South Wales is about to increase its paid capital,
and the Commercial of Sydney takes the same step.
Both these cos. pay 10 per cent., and, as the Bank of Aus-
tralasia and the Union have put up their dividend to 12 per
cent., shareholders in the two big Sydney banks have been
urging that they should be treated' the'same way. Very
wisely the managements propose to benefit the share-
holders while giving depositors and other creditors fur-
ther security, and thus increasing the banks’stability.
The new capital presumably will carry the 10 per cent,
which the old has been receiving, yet in the market
that capital is worth only 4 'per cent. That
is where the shareholders will come in—they
will practically have their dividend increased while
keeping the rate at the old 10 per cent. See what
it is worth to the investor, presuming the new capital is
issued at par, which no doubt it will be. The present
Commercial of Sydney shares, £l2 10s. paid, have been
worth lately about £33. That gives a return to the in-
vestor of £3 15s. 9d. per cent, on a dividend of 10 per
cent. Now, by paying £3 2s. 6d. more, the dividend is
increased by one-fourth. Add that £3 2s. 6d. to the
market price, and the same dividend on the increased
capital gives an investment return of £4 6s. 2d. per cent,
on the old and new capital put together. The details of
the Bank of N.S. Wales’ increase have not yet been
announced.
* * * *
Trouble which has been threatening among the
fire-insurance offices has been patched up, and a war of
rates has been averted “Dual representation” was
the bone of contention ; some companies, which had
swallowed other cos., wanted the right to have an agent
apiece for themselves and another for the swallowed cos.
On account of that, two of the big foreign cos. gave notice
of withdrawal from the association, which was how
the Westralian war started. But a conference held last
week in Melbourne came to a settlement. Only in two
special cases is dual representation to be allowed, and
then only until next year; the two big cos. withdrew
their notices, and all is peace again.
* * * *
Sixteen years ago it was barely possible to get
anybody to look at Australian coke. It was the old
story. Australian coal wouldn’t make good coke. If it
would, Australians were too careless to make it, anyway.
The Longworths tried their hardest to find a market;
when they couldn’t, they resolved to find a mine so that
they could smelt their own ore with their own coke.
That was the start of Great Cobar—the re-starting of it,
rather. Up to about that time Australians weren’t
reckoned good enough to manage big mines, either ; so
they imported foreigners, and the foreigners imported
coke. It wasn’t their fault; they took Australians’ word
for it that they couldn’t make coke. But the price of
the German and Welsh coke was deadly—up to £5 per
ton. In 1890, when the Australian coke business began
to feel its feet, the Australian article was valued at £1
6s. fid. per ton at the works. The N.S.W. Statistical
Register for 1890 shows that 38,003 tons were imported
to N.S.W. in that year, at a cost of £192,140—£5
Is. per ton ! Then, with the Dibbs duty on coke
to help them, the smelters began to ' find out
that it was all an unpatriotic lie that Australia couldn't
make coke ; and works went up in several places. From
5245 tons in 1892 the output increased to 55,991 tons last
year. But as important a thing as that increase in out-
put is the fact that from the £5 just mentioned (the cost
of the imported article) the selling price has fallen to
11s. lid., last year’s average. So Australia makes all its
own coke now, and makes it for less than one-eighth of
what it once paid to the foreigner. What chat means to
the mining and smelting business is too obvious to need
mentioning. That is how Protection “puts up prices.”
Yet remember how the S.M. Herald and Argus en-
couraged the metal miners (paying £5 a ton for coke) to
howl at the Dibbs Government on account of “ the blow ”
it aimed at the mining industry when it put on the coke
duty?

As to these assetsthat have been written up : If the}' are
over-valued, then the funds are really not what they are
represented to be. Of the assets, £24,600 is freehold pro-
perty and £10,946 Government securities, policy loans,
&c. Assume.that this £10,946 earns 3} per cent. =£4lo.
Total interest and rents are £lOlB ; thus £6OB is left to
represent the earnings of the £24,500 freehold ; and that
means only £2 9s. Bd. per cent, per annum. Either, then,
on this basis, the co. doesn’t charge itself sufficient rent
for the premises, supposing it occupies them, in which
case its true expense rate is higher than is represented,
or the property is over-valued, and the insurance fund
shown does not exist. In addition to the fund there is,
however, shareholders’ capital amounting to £9227, and
under the circumstances, as dividends are not often paid
(none was paid in either 1905 or 1906), this is a material
advantage to policy-holders.
The co. now closes its books on December 31st
instead of August 31st, and two statements are, there-
fore, available—one for four months to the end of 1905,
the other for 1906. For the purposes of the following
comparison the 16 months are treated as one period :
Outgoings (apart prom Claims) Compared with Income.
1899 .. £7O 7 0 per cent. I 1903 .. £BO 10 6 per cent.
1900 .. 63 4 5 „ I 1904 .. 76 19 1 „
1901 .. 59 8 7 I 1905 .. 66 18 11
1902 .. 107 14 3 ~ I 1906 .. 62 6 4 „
Both ordinary and industrial business is done, but the
two are not fully distinguished. It is, however, men-
tioned that “in the ordinary department 503 policies
assuring £41,650 were issued.” When in the ordinary de-
partment the average size of the policy is £B2, anything
like a low expense rate is simply out of the question.
(In 1905, by the way, it was mentioned that 2280
industrial policies assured £51,165, which would mean
an average of £22.) This is insurance on the very lowest
scale—for a shilling you get four pen ny-ha’porth of in-
surance ; the other 7.',d. goes in chasing you, first to
insure you, and then to keep you insured, it is a shock-
ing waste, for which not the < ffice but the system is
responsible. The question at present is whether it isn’t
better to have even this insurance than none at all.
Certainly the Miners’ Accident Relief Fund, under which
the miners of N.S.W. are insured without any expensive
chasing, points the way to cheap insurance. For the
present, anyway, it seems, no voluntary system can be
cheap.

The Civil Service Co-operative Society of
Victoria, Ltd. (Melbourne), which began with a deficit
representing preliminary and flotation expenses :
Dividend
(5 p.c.) Written To Re- De-
Profits. and Bonus. Off. serves, licit.
May, 1903 .. £lO7l
Jan., 1904 .. £1450 £456 £994 77
July, 1904 .. 258 638 457
Jan., 1905 .. 1896 £1331 265 300 157
July, 1905 .. 1637 1441 215 176
Reserves.
Jan., 1906 .. 2661 1786 440 435 259
Julv, 1906 .. 1884 1864 267 12
Jan., 1907 .. 3450 2458 471 521 533
Six months ago the bonus on purchases (6d. in the £)
was estimated to call for £llOO, which would have left
reserves of £215. As a matter of fact, they called for
£1303, and left reserves of only a poor little £l2. This
sort of miscalculation may easily deceive the directors into
declaring a larger bonus than the circumstances warrant.
The present bonus has been increased to 9d. in the £.
The figures certainly look better—profit has increased
very well. But it is still a question whether the bonus
should have been increased in view of (1) the very heavy
debt, (2) the large risk of depreciation of stock (valued at
£23,562) and the insignificance of the reserves, and (3)
the fact that, though profits are shown as over 80 per
cent, larger than in the preceding half, sales increased
only from £54,762 to £55,703. The capital now is £29,655 ;
liabilities (debentures, &c.) amount to £29,917. Instead
of using profit to wipe the debt out, the society is urging
shareholders to put in more capital—which is risky, any-
how, because it can be' withdrawn on short notice.
Possibly the Civil Service shareholders are a poor lot of
co-operators, after all, who grab at bonuses whether by
doing so they imperil their own society or not; but they
might at least reflect that if they imperil the society they
not only imperil their own capital, but threaten to
deprive themselves also of all those advantages which a
well-established co-operative society would bring them
later on.

But the year has been better than that, as this shows :

1902 .. £10,621 £3,847 £14,468

The co. owns Thurulgoona and Bundaleer, on the N.S.W.-
Queensland border, where it sometimes rains, but gene-
rally doesn’t. Lately it has been having a good run of
seasons ; and shares (£3 paid), which were quoted down
to 4s. in 1901, have crept up to 355. The figure shows how
investors still mistrust the business, in spite of its recent
brave show ; for at that price a 5 per cent. div. means a
return of over 8£ per cent. From first to last, however
—the co. is now 24 years old—it has only averaged about
1£ per cent, on its capital. But it has an advantage now
which it did not have for many years. Its rent and
tenure have been improved, and after deducting the value
of the wool clip, the overdraft, &c., have been reduced to
£26,805. Even as recently as 1898 the mortgage alone
reached £70,000. If in 1883 the co. had paid £250,000 in-
stead of £420,000 odd for its Thurulgoona and Bundaleer
and their 300,000 sheep, the results would have been, on
the whole, fair. But, like most people, it miscalculated
the capacity of the inlands, and where it ran over 300,000
sheep it now has under 150,000. That was the beginning
of the whole trouble. As it has paid off its heavy debt, it
has written down the book value of the property ; and
those writings-down account for some of the heavy losses
shown in the table. It is still a gamble, but its outlook
is not, for the reasons mentioned, to be judged by its
dividend history.

fund.

The society is 35 years old now, and very small for its
age; the South Australian State Land Bank to a great
extent takes the place of a building society, and it forces
other concerns into making advances of this sort at low
rates. The society’s capital is now only £14,561, apart
from accumulated profit, and even that is a trifle more
than it was. Expenses are naturally heavy ; just about
half the gross interest, rents, &c., goes that way. There
are no debts to the public—in fact, there is a credit
balance of £2169, which represents more than an eighth
of all the assets. The society certainly can’t be accused
of riotousness.

Equitable Building Society (Launceston,
Tas.): —

1905 3264 7 „ „ 2946 318 14,365
1906 2774 7 „ „ 2445 329 14,694
And this shows in more detail some recent ups and
downs—mostly downs :
1901. 1904. 1905. ’ 1906.
Capital £50,883 £52,273 £46,904 £39,633
Deposits 83,677 59,827 61,959 63,135
Mortgages 140,453 118,166 114,162 111,809
Management 1,046 1,198 1,130 1,172
Steadily for the past four years capital has been shrinking
and deposits have been increasing. Deposits are a first
charge ; capital comes in last. If Tasmanian securities
are held in poor esteem, and a shrinkage in value is
looked for, then, of course, investors will lodge their
money as deposits instead of capital, since the capital
has to guarantee the repayment of deposits. This move-
ment of capital down and deposits up is a bad feature of
Tasmanian finance ; it suggests that Tasmanian investors
haven’t got faith in the little State’s future ; and, in view
of the drift of population, investors are no doubt right.
In 1903, £1 of shareholders’ capital only had to protect
about 235. of deposits ; now it has to protect nearly 335.
And shareholders are getting no more for taking the
extra risk. The drift is serious. But the remedy is not
in the hands of the society, as a society: it is in the
hands of the members as citizens. They can keep their
Legislative Council, burking progress, but they will have
to pay for it.

* * * * ■
On the strength of the 25 per cent, dividend
(10s. per share) paid last year, and the 50 per cent. (£1)
expected this year. Wallaroo and Moonta (S.A.) £2 shares
have sailed up past £6. A wonderful pair of old mines
these are ; but they certainly don’t improve with age any
more than other mines. Two things in particular make
the present profits—the metal boom and the cementation
process. Those who believe that the metal boom isn’t a
boom will not see anything wrong' with the price. But
there are others, who reckon it a good deal fairer to take
the past dividend record. Here it is for 12 years:—
1895 .. 5 per cent. 1901 .. 5 per cent.
1896 .. 5 „ 1902 .. Nil.
1897 ..10 ~ 1903 .. 2 £ per cent.
1898 ..5 „ 1904 .. 2£
1899 .. 5 „ 1905 .. 2| „
1900 .. 12J „ 1906 ..25
That is 80 per cent, for 12 years, an average of 6f per
cent, per annum on £2. But if the co. pays 50 per cent,
this year, that will be 130 per cent, for 13 years, or an
average of 10 per cent. It is very doubtful; however,
whether 10 per cent, is too much, remembering that a
mine is a wasting security ; if so, on the 13 years’ record,
£2 is the fair price of the shares, plus the surplus of
liquid assets, equal to about £1 6s. per share on Decem-
ber 31,1906.
As mentioned just now, the cementation or
salt-water and acid leaching process has greatly helped
profits during the past four years or so. The leach-
ing started at the end of 1901, and it was then estimated
that it would take about seven years to get most of the
copper our,. The estimate has had to be varied since;
but it will be seen, anyhow, that revenue from this source
will not be spread over very many years. While it lasts,
though, it is an extremely profitable branch. The 772
tons of fine copper which it produced at Moonta last year
cost, including smelting, shipping, &c., only £32 19s. lid.
per ton, while all costs in connection with the smelting
ores ran to £72 11s. per ton. This last is a very stiff price.
Take off, say, £3 11s. per ton to represent the value of
Moonta copper over standard, and £69 still remains. In
1905 (making the same deduction) it was £5B per ton. The
difference is partly' accounted for by higher wages and
more expenditure on plant, &c., charged to revenue.
Even at £5B per ton—supposing those costs could be
maintained—there would be mighty little margin in most
years. In fact, in only 10 years out of the past 25 has
the average price reached £5B. Between 1889 and 1899
the price in no year averaged more than £54 10s.; and
that was the 10-year period, it is worth remembering,
which immediately followed the previous great copper
boom. It isn’t suggested that the two booms are in every
respect the same; there is a very large increase in the
consumption of copper now, and there was no very
marked increase then. But both had the same effect in
encouraging copper prospecting, developing new mines,
and re-opening old ones. Also, the high prices on both
occasions sent people off looking for substitute metals or
alloys.
At the best Wallaroo and Moonta are not cheap
mines to work. Last half-year Mt. Lyell produced its
blister copper(which contained 740 z. silver andnearly2£oz.
gold per ton) for £35 4s. per ton. But cheap pyritie
smelting is unknown at Wallaroo. The stuff has to be
laboriously dressed before it goes to the smelters. It is
worth while following the output of 147,190 tons from
Wallaroo: —
Ore, &c. Copper. How disposed of.
Ore .. 45,355 tons 4314 tons Smelted
Tailings.. 61,694 „ 983 ~ For cementation
Waste .. 40,141 „ 127 ~ Underground filling
Total.. 147,190 tons 5424 tons
The smelting ore thus contains only 79'53 per cent, of the
metal in the whole body of ore ; the other 20 - 47 per cent,
is either dumped to be treated by cementation in years
to come, after the air has got at it, or it is treated as
mere waste rock.
■*■***
Horseshoe Creek (Northern Territory), after 12
months’ work, affords a striking illustration of the
methods of the Chinese tributor and miner. The Ven-
ture syndicate, which holds 480 acres, let the property to
Mongolians on a 10 per cent, basis—all except one show,
Boylin’s. One rich hill was pig-rooted till it resembled
a rabbit-warren ; now, since the wet season set in, the
workings have fallen in. This will make it very hard to
pick up the leaders. The Mystery, a claim which in its
last month produced six tons concentrates from 30 tons,
valued at £6lO, has also collapsed. All these workings
should have been condemned long ago ; but in the Terri-
tory “ mining ” is carried on anyhow.
* * * *
At Mount Todd (N.T.) a ten-stamper battery
should have started by this time. The people are more
progressive here. White men are employed in the bat-
tery and have erected it. A Progress Committee (the
only one in the Territory) has been formed. Salway and
party have 60 tons material at the mill averaging 12 per
cent, black tin. Morris has 500 tons of 6 per cent, material
paddocked, and Elliot 170 tons of 8 per cent, stone. Mr.
Buttle’s charges of £1 a ton for crushing will give these
men a lift, and a large amount of 2Jper cent, and 4 per
cent, material that has been lying at grass will be con-
verted into cash. The future of this field looks good.
The deepest shaft is down 100 ft., where the lode measures
3ft., and will apparently live.
“ Mainshaft ” : Par in B. 21/2/’O7 suggests that
the Northern Territory Mines of Australia, Ltd., had to
turn down its Iron Blow proposition because of “ expen-
sive staff and frills and a horde of Chinese, who are past-
masters at dodging graft.” This may have been so in the
early days of the co., but it certainly is untrue of T. I.
Dyson’s management. The fact of tributors getting on
to 50 tons or so of very high-grade ore in the Ellison is
nothing against the management either. The little, in-
expensive tributor can make money where an equipped
co. would lose it.
* * * *
L. C. Ball, of the Queensland Geological Sur-
vey, was lately sent up to Cloncurry (Q.) to get an
opinion as to the permanency or otherwise of the lodes.
His full report, with map, will be published in a, few
weeks; meantime he has drawn up some “Advance
Notes.” Possibly he wasn’t given time to sort out his
opinions; anyway, they amount to about this—that
some will and some won’t, or, if they don’t, they look as
though they might. “ Their occurrence was found to be
in many respects similar to that in the Cobar and Mt.
Lyell districts ” [by the way, Oobar has only two div.-
paying copper mines, and Lyell has never had more than
one), “ the most noticeable difference being the greater
number of smaller, but richer, crops.... The ore deposits
are of three kinds: rich lenticular bodies in shear zones,
which may be considered almost as permanent as true
fissures; lenticular bodies and impregnations of the
strained rock in crush zones; and poorer
nations of originally porous bands of country. ’ The cruet

supply of ore has been from bunches of secondary
sulphides, enriched by leachings from poorer ores that
have disappeared. “ The primary sulphides, having been
derived from underlying acid igneous intrusive magmas,
may be expected to continue up from water level, and
bunches of secondary sulphides may be expected at least
down to water level. However, when the rich shallow
bodies have pinched, instead of sinking and driving being
continued in search of other bodies, work has ceased,
and a tradition has thus originated that Cloncurry lodes
are simply surface accumulations.” Byway of a general
conclusion : —“ The generally scattered and isolated
occurrence of the ore,bodies and their comparatively
small size (for the high grade deposits of large size yet
found are few in number) must always handicap the
field ; but there is an immense amount of ore to be won
in the Cloncurry district while the price of copper
remains high.” Which last is pretty poor comfort,
seeing that the railway cannot possibly get out to Clon-
curry for nearly 12 months, and a lot of things may
happen in a boom copper riiarket in 12 months.
Surveyor Ball’s notes on individual mines are
fragmentary and generally very inconclusive. Of Hamp-
den he says that “ all the richer ore has been removed by
tributors, and the character of the workings indicates
that its occurrence was very irregular ” ; practically none
payable under present conditions is visible ; and no esti-
mate of the ore-bearing zone can be made. Of the Mt.
Hope the crop is 20ft. thick : “the richest parts do not
appear to carry more than 10 per cent, copper, while the
greater part is quite barren. Leaching must, however,
have taken place, and the lode is therefore worth pros-
pecting.” The Duchess (“ varying between 6in. and
7ft.”), “besides being one of the richest and most ex-
tensive,” “ has every prospect of being persistent in
depth.” Mt. Elliott outcrop is dense limonite; the
average value is from 15 per cent, to 20 per cent. “ One
shaft has been sunk in ore to a depth of 225 ft. On
the 125 ft. level the ore body has been proved by
driving over a length of 160 ft. and by crosscutting over
a width of 50ft. in the central part. Here also the ore
(mostly limonite), though sometimes barren, is believed
to carry on the average between 10 per cent, and 20 per
cent, copper, there being considerable bodies of rich
black oxide scattered through the poorer ground. At
the ends of the main drive and in the lower workings
developments have not been altogether satisfactory, but
enough work has not yet been done for a definite state-
ment to be made.” Altogether, in fact, the whole thing
is something extra in the way of a gamble.
* * * *
You can tell from the recent experiences of
Peak Downs (Q.) what it means for only one small market
to be interested in a mining stock. “ Squeakies,” as
Brisbane insists on calling them, were rushed up from
their 6s. paid to about 655. There is only a handful of
shares, and the line had been oversold ; but the Wall-
street smash was used to rake in a few to cover with. At
present the market is miles apart. Sellers ask anything
up to 605., and buyers mention 38s. ; ana as there is no
other ’Change interested, the gulf remains at that. In a
market like this a sale of 50 shares or so fixes the nominal
price. But if a holder tried to sell a solid lump of shares
at the price, he usually finds that things are not what
they seem.
* * * • ’(
It is much the same with Mt. Canninflah as it
is with Peak Downs, though a few shares in the former
have now trickled down south. This is, however, counter-
balanced by the absurdly large swag of shares : 300,000
were out before, and now Bewick, Moreing and Co. have
got hold of 60,000 at ss. On that the market jumped;
but the dead weight was too great. Still, apart from the
price, which, all things considered, is good, the former
shareholders ought to score ; for there is a chance now
of a better market being made for the stock —one of the
conditions of the sale was that a London register should
be opened. Metallurgist Fawcett was at the mine, and
overhauled it on 8.-M.’s behalf, and the chances are that
he will join the Board. Said Board hasn’t been too
happy a family; but J. D. Campbell was recently
appointed as a fourth director, and 8., M. and Co.’s
nominee will make the fifth. When the deal is com-
pleted, the co. should have about £20,000 in hand.
Monthly profits are not far short of £2OOO ; but £2OOO is
less than ljd. per share per month. The mine calls for
more rapid development; the mill has got ahead of the
mine, and can’t keep the smelter supplied—which is the
usual way with Queensland mining.
Mt. Hector (Central Queensland), one of the
few northern copper concerns that don’t have to carry a
big swag of shares, has had a lot of trouble, but things
appear now to be about straightened out. Thomas Gibb
—Gibb the elder—has been up seeing the new furnace
blown in, and the first tap was expected to be taken off
at the end of last week. His faith in Mt. Hector doesn’t
waver. The Gibbs are putting their backers still further
into Central Queensland, though Glassford Creek shut-
down has been a wet blanket to them lately. Reports
from there are still conflicting. But, though it is
reported that there is now plenty of smelting ore avail-
able, late manager Bailey (who was understood to have
come down to Brisbane to consult about things) doesn’t
return. He has gone up to look after the properties of a
new co., the Gibb Copper Mines, Ltd.—mostly resurrec-
tions.
* * * *
Kelly’s Block has lately been fashionable
property at Charters Towers, for, though it has but a small
output, it yields decent stone. It is 14 years since the
co. started as a producer. In that time 77,819 tons have
been crushed for £393,522. The total dividends run to
£188,400, or 375. per share. The co. had some leisure
moments a couple of years back, and devoted the time to
much needed prospecting work, as the output had fallen
to a cap-full or so per month. This improved things so
that, for the past six months, 3265 tons were beaten out
and treated for £12,153. The make of stone that
came in from the Phcebe hangs out well, and has every
appearance in Kelly’s of reaching the Brilliant Central s
ground. Kelly’s is operating upon three formations ; but
the principal workings are on the top, or 1212 ft. reef. All
through the workings the reef is irregular in size and
strike. On the 1212 ft. reef, two winzes 100 ft. apart are
down, one 263fc. and the other 180 ft., and a level con-
nects the two. In one part of the level there is 4ft. of
stone showing ; 30fb. further in there is but 9in., with
another make going up into the hanging wall. Where the
reef is 6in. thick it carries as much mineral as the
“bulges”do. Stoping prevents any reserves of stone
from accumulating, so it’s a hand-to-month existence
that the co. leads.
* * * *
Pfeiffers Day Dawn, Charters Towers, con-
tinues to improve with age. The output is steadily in-
creasing, and now has reached 400 tons per month. The
main underlay that starts from the No. 4 level is down
365 ft., with a healthy-looking reef overhead. The No. 1
level carries 2ft. of payable stone for a distance of 387 ft.
A crosscut into the footwall has discovered something
new ; a 10-inch reef of fair quality stone is showing in a
rise that is going up. Development at the rate of 140 ft. a
month is being done. It looks as if the old Consolidated
is in a better position to reward backers in the near
future for their confidence than any other show at present
in sight at Charters Towers.

G. P. Doolette, the Anglo-Westralian, has
taken an option over the Fitzroy (Q.), from Captain
Philip Charley. The Fitzroy (the old Mt. Chalmers,
Eoekhamptori district) has the makings of a very big
copper-gold property, but it calls for well on to £lOO,OOO
to bring it to the front. Charley has large piles of
Broken Hill coin behind him, but it is a big contract for
even him to take on ; and the option, therefore, needn’t
surprise anybody. #
Ooakes, of Catherine Reef, Bendigo, having
printed a report, now finds a printer’s devil to recollect
it. After all, it is not unnatural that Coakes should be
keel-hauled a little. The Bendigo boom has burst, and
one of the biggest contributors to the burst was the
Catherine Reef collapse. Not that Bendigo people
suffered much, as the crowd there sold high and
have been waiting ever since to buy low from out-
siders, which is how Bendigo makes much of its
money. The slowness with which the new Catherine
plant has been got needn’t be dwelt on ; for that is
another way that Bendigo has. Besides, it hardly matters
in the present case. Adelaide lias a big pot of Catherines,
bought on the up-grade, and, in its chagrin, it is firing
at the Board. Examined, last half-year’s work shows
that stone panned out about 9dwt.—a big decrease on
what Was expected. Still it was highly payable if there
had been enough of it. Results, however, indicate that
there wasn’t. Out of the shoot up to the time it went
underfoot, the co. made £32,600, or about 10s. a share.
There is still stone overhead to stope, and that gives
some show of a dividend. What is needed to brighten up
things is to pick up the shoot at 2300 ft., if it is there. If
the mine had been outside of Bendigo, that little point
would have been nearer solution than it is to-day. Some
of the fathers of the Golden City might trip to Walhalla
to learn bow they get doWti there, though far greater
difficulties stand in the way,
* * * *
Certain directors of the South German, Maldon
(Vic.), have decided to shut the mine to outside inspection
(except as regards the press) for a month. Presumably,
they realise the responsibility they take in doing so. The
directors are trustees for the shareholders, and as such
may say, “ We think the best way to study your interest
is to close up the property.” But the directors are also
partners in a joint enterprise, and what right have
they, without the express authority of their partners,
to deny the latter admission to their own property?
Solicitor Moloney, one of the directors, says that the
Board never gave any of the directors authority to take
the step. That makes things worse, for it looks as if one
or two of the Board are running the claim as they like.
W. H. Gray did that in the old days, but the practice is
not to be commended, though it is one that some min-
ing men like—if they are the one or two. After all, what
is the use of closing the mine ? News has been filtering
as freely to the Stock Exchanges as ever, only probably
it isn’t so reliable ; as it has been favorable, shares have
jumped forward. That indicates the folly of the pro-
cedure, as well as the injustice. If the mine could be
closed up with as little chance of news escaping as if the
thing were a Chubb’s safe, the Board might be excused.
When, however, there are dozens of news leaks, then
shutting out the shareholder means that the broker or
speculator who can buy information can do pot-shooting.

Confidence Extended, Bendigo, has been pursu-
ing a dilatory course for a generation or so. Now it is
having a shot at the north ground, through which “ centre
country ” runs. The work should have been done long
ago. Still, better late than never. There is a local up-
heaval of enthusiasm because a crosscut put out in the
north ground at 1150 ft. cut a cap, and still more jubila-
tion when another was got at 1500 ft. But the local
market has to be exclusively depended on, since Bendigo
in tbe boom-tide would not have anything to do with the
rest of the universe, so a cold shoulder is being turned
towards Confidence Extended scrip now.
* * * *
The Victorian coal-lords wail that their Rail-
way Commissioners will not pay above 12s. a ton to them,
and they say they cannot accept that figure. The fact is
that Victorian coal-mining expenses cut into more than
12s. —yet the .cos. expect to live alongside Newcastle,
where costs are much lower. There, however, is a more
cheerful note at the finish of the Jumbunna directors’
report: “ The household trade is being more than main-
tained, and we are hopeful that, at no very distant date,
the co. will be restored to its prosperity.” This remark
hardly fits in with the rest of the report. Or, if the
household trade is sufficient to restore the co. to its
former vigorous financial health, why bother to sell
to the railways at all ? Better bring some
business acumen in, and work the household
trade for all it is worth. There is a pretty big market in
Melbourne ; the co.’s coal has a good reputation, and the
Board ought to be able to push it. But in this connec-
tion, didn’t the co. jump up rates when the coal crisis
was on in Newcastle, and that at a time when the public
was making a concession in railway freights to Victorian
coal ? It may have seemed good business at the moment,
but the readiness with which the co. moved out to ex-
ploit its public has not been forgotten. Therefore, any
ad misericordiam appeal only falls flat. The co., like its
brother, the Outtrim-Howitt, had better make up its
mind to the fact that it has to play the part of a buffer
between the N.S.W. collieries and the railways, and so
must expect knocks.

This is the story of the directors of the Mul-
hausen Glen Wills (Vic.). A reef traceable over a range
1200 ft. above the creek level; lode continuous for 2000 ft.;
formation 4ft. to 20ft. wide ; free gold, 4dwt. to lOdwt.;
pyrites, averaging 30z., and water power galore. Vic-
torian gold yields now have a chance to revive, for with
an “ inexhaustible supply of ore,” the Munchausen can
so swell the total as to make up for the shortage in Ben-
digo and Ballarat. Yet there has been no rush after
shares. 15 is a strange, perverse world.
* » * *
The Zinc Corporation has made almost its
first sensible move since it began operations on the
Broken Hill tailings dumps. It has borrowed the
Central mine’s (Sulphide Corporation’s) zinc expert,
and turned over to him the patchwork zinc ex-
traction failure on the British mine. As a granu-
lation plant the previously unsatisfactory flotation
pans are turning out a concentrate worth 45 per cent,
for zinc, and the disheartened staff is regaining its cheer-
fulness. But the Zinc Corporation has yet to face the
reality of things. Experience has shown that no zinc
process has yet been discovered that will yield a profit
on 10 and 11 per cent, tailings when they have to be pur-
chased even at ss. or 6s. per ton. The Zinc Corporation’s
tailings won’t average more than about 12 per cent, for
zinc, for in the old British dump which they bought there is
a lot of carbonate. An attempt to surmount the difficulty
is being made by the installation of magnetic separators
for the recovery of the lead from the zinc concentrate.
This scheme does not hold out any very bright hopes,
seeing that in the process considerable loss must ensue.
In magnetic separation there is always a middle product,
which is valueless, although in this case it will rob both
the zinc and the lead concentrate. Buyers of zinc con-
centrates now stipulate in their contracts for 8 p.c. of
lead in the product, and for every unit less than that the
seller will be fined. The reason of this is that experience
in smelting has shown that the lead carries about 95 p.c.
of the silver recovered. Altogether, the corporation
seems to have a long way to go before it will be able to
sail in smooth water.

Broken Hill Block 14 is making a brave show
with its carbonates, which are essentially a surface ore, but
what about its sulphide zone ? Last week’s supply to the
mill was of very poor grade, and only for the fact that
there were some 200 tons in the bins when the
week’s run started, the output would have sung
very small. The fact is now becoming very patent
that Block 14 sulphides are poor and scarce, and
that almost everything was staked on the prospect
of get tine a full hand of trumps at the 600 ft. level; and
the 600ftr level has, after much probing, been thus far not
much better than a blank.
m * * *
Sydney will presently own practically all the
Broken Hill Junction North. The sharebroking Palmers
(one of the family is manager) say, in their monthly cir-
cular, that during this year—in less than three months—
“between 40,000 and 50,000 have been bought through
our office, mostly , for investment.” For months the
Palmer circular has been urging the firm’s clients to snap
up the shares “for investment.” Now, of course,
Palmers have to hold up the market, and when another
capital has a thousand or two shares to sell, Palmers are
expected to buy. If they don’t, a hint follows that the
shares will be thrown at the market; and there is now no
group outside Sydney sufficiently interested to go to the
the shares have come Sydney way; and when the
Sydney mining market—the poorest in Australia —
gets the stock in its own hands, it will be
a very sick stock indeed. "For investment” purposes
this, of course, won’t make any difference ; the milling
machines won’t care whether shares are 355. or 35d. _ But
99 people out of 100 always look to getting out of mining
scrip at some time or other. Looking at the Junction
North as an investment, there isn’t a doubt that it is a
wonderfully improved mine, so far as ore developments
go ; but, apart from the fact that its workings are not
secure, the results of its milling are still in doubt. The
extraction is fair, but nobody supposes that it is the most
refractory ore that has reached the mill, and costs are
not yet mentioned. And, above all, the person who buys
for investment with lead at £l9 10s. is liable to have a
cold time, seeing that in only four of the past 25 years
has the yearly average been above £l5.
* « * *
Things won’t go right with the Ringarooma
(Tas.). In other words, the official estimates and ex-
pectations were all wrong; First the dredge was delayed ;
then alleged inferior materials led to breakages; then
when an attempt was made to get the dredge over the
river, the flood spoke up ; meantime it got to work on
the west side, with poor results, till the river-flow
slackened. Then in the second week after it had crossed
it got into poor ground sprinkled with submerged
timber ; and now new dredge-manager Brown says of the
bucket dredge to which Grayson pinned his faith and the
faith of his Long Tunnel following “ The plant has not
been working satisfactorily ; the bucket pins are the
worst that it has ever been my lot to have anything to
do with; the band has parted three times in 11
weeks.” Still, the new man says he has been boring,
and—" 1 hopestly think that the dredge will be
worked at a fair profit till we get back to the bottom
end of the lease, when we shall follow a splendid run
of ground proved by boring.” But it will take more than
that to shake up the dry bones. Financial position is
that the co. now has liabilities £368 ahead of liquid
assets; and, assuming that mine (£7500) and plant
(£8694) are worth what they cost the co., it still has
£3477 of profit and loss leeway to make up.
* * • *
“ M.C.” : Be sale of Hercules’ zinc ore (Tas. )to
the Germans. The folly of exporting enormous quantities
of Australian ore to foreign smelting works has not yet
struck Australia as it struck Canada, where it suggested
a bounty on smelted lead, or as it struck the Malay
States, which imposed a heavy export tax on tin ore.
It naturally doesn’t occur to the Australian Freetrader
that there is anything mad in exporting ore from a
country even though it has numerous smelting works
and cheap coal. But what are the Protectionists doing ?
America and Germany guard their smelting trade with
jealous care, while Australia does nothing. It scouts
the idea of bounties, subsidies, railway rebates, or an
export tax. Yet these things have become part and
parcel of competitive trade, the foreigner uses them, and
Australia must.

From a good judge in the West : — “ Barrambie
Ranges (W.A.) has proved a fiasco ; the crushing for an
average of 14dwt. is said to have astounded the directors.
Manager Aldridge is to return and resume management,
while Manager Morgan is to take charge once more of the
South Barrambie. In November last it was stated that a
north-and-south reef traversed the property, and that it
was sft. to 6ft. wide, of which 2ft. were rich, and good
returns were predicted. Mine is fully equipped with an
up-to-date plant, and there is no apparent excuse for the
poor return, if 14dwt. can be called poor. The value of
the reef should be pretty well known, as so long ago as’
last November 200 ft. had been driven at the 45ft. level,
and 400 ft. at 100 ft. Manager Aldridge reckons that the
crushing must have been taken from a poor stope. If so,
it was a shocking piece of bad luck, considering the
amount of development done. It is more likely to be
another case of inability to judge values.”
* * • *
Gwalia Consols, Wiluna (W.A.), is beginning
to attract attention. It is a reconstruction of three con-
cerns, and had till recently a capital of £50,000 in 400,000
shares of 2s. 6d. each. In September last the co. took
over the assets of the Lake Way Goldfields for 28,000
shares, a new issue to that extent being made out of
100,000 new shares created. The purchase included a
20-head Fraser and Chalmers mill, three winders, air
compressor, boilers, &c. All these are now being trans-
ferred, and, with 30 head of stamps at work, the output
is expected to very shortly run to nearly 6000 tons
monthly. The ore in reserve above 100 ft. is said to be
117,000 tons, worth 30s. per ton. No work has been done
below this level, as it was reckoned that the ore in sight
was more than enough to keep the old 10-head mill crush-
ing for five years. The lode opened up is 40ft. wide, but
appears to be very erratic, as monthly crushings have
varied from 17s. to over 395. per ton. There is
another lode on the property, on which very little
work has been done. The Government Engineer, in
his annual report, confirms the manager’s state-
ment that this lode was 180 ft. wide, and richer
than the one being worked. The concern has been con-
trolled by a London crowd without any go, and the mine
has not had a chance to show itself off. The co. has also
been much tinkered with. In 1898 the capital was
£160,000 in £1 shares. In 1900 it was increased to
£625,000 in £1 shares. In 1904 the capital was reduced
to £50,000 in 2s. 6d. shares, and £561,532 was written off.
If the reports on the mine can be accepted, it is one of
the biggest low-grade mines in existence ; yet, after nine
years’ run, it is still in the prospecting stage.
* * » *
Golden Link, Kalgoorlie, is once more on the
rocks, and has promulgated a novel scheme to raise the
wind : £20,000 in 5 per cent, debentures of Is. each are
offered to shareholders. There are 353,190 shares, and
each holder can apply for one debenture for each share
held. The remaining 46,810 debentures can be applied
for pro rata. Of the £20,000 to be raised, if possible, by
this fantastic scheme, £12,000 is ear-marked to pur-
chase and erect a sulphide mill. Judging by the
cost of other mills on the fields, the projected
mill will be a one-horse affair. Shareholders who do not
take up debentures will be left out in the cold, as a lien
on the assets of the co. is given to the debenture holders,
who may forfeit the property if their interest is not paid
at due date. Nevertheless, Australian holders, if there
are any, get no show with the debentures, as applications
closed on January 21, and news about the scheme was
not cabled out. The old co.’s 20-head mill was lately
struck by lightning and wrecked by fire. This
mill and accessories were valued at £BOOO, and were un-
insured. Insurance agents were always sooled off the
place, even during the recent war of rates, when risks
were being accepted at 2s. 6d. per cent. The mill was
useless for hard sulphide ore, and was for sale, and, had
it only been insured, the co. would now be in a moderate
patch of clover.
ft * * *
North Boulder, Kalgoorlie, having again come
to the end of its tether, is to reconstruct with an assess-
ment of 2s. per share. But Australian shareholders got
no chance of applying for scrip, as old scrip had to reach
London by March 27, and notice was not given till too
late. The mine once did fairly well, and paid two divs.
of 9d. and one of Is., but that was back in 1897-9. The
lode then worked underlaid into the Associated; and
although that was known six or seven years ago, the
Associated laid low and did nothing. But owing to the
persistency of tributors in applying for tributes, Manager
Roberts recently started prospecting from his No. 7 shaft,
and cut ore 2ft. wide, and at this point of very high values.
Enough work has not yet been done to judge what the
find is worth. The North Boulder levels have never been
any good, and although a lot of prospecting has been done
down to 1300 ft. nothing payable has been located. The
property has been thought to be situated too far east off
the run of any payable lode ; and the lode worked in the
early days, and now being exploited by the Associated,
has hitherto not been touched in any of the adjoining
properties. On the whole, colonial holders, who have
been deprived of their chance to apply through shortness
of notice, are probably not much the poorer for it. They
may even be richer.
* • ♦ *
Bellevue, Mt. Sir Samuel (W. A.), is to suffer a
further reconstruction, and shareholders are once again
asked to contribute 2s. per share. The co., whose mine
has been worked since 1899, was reconstructed in 1902,
when £75,000 was raised to purchase and erect a 40-head
mill. From first to last, some 300,000 tons of ore have
been raised and treated for a yield of about £480,000, but
the mine has never paid, and, from present appearances
is never likely to. The total working capital provided to
date has been £139,000, and no div. has ever come from
it. Last year’s returns only panned out 56,000 tons for
£68,000, the resultant profit being £3OO, without allowing
for depreciation and London expenses. The last four
months’ returns read November, loss £1920 ; December
profit £710; January, loss £330 ; February, loss £lo3o*
—Thia_shflW-S a net loss of £2570. A proposition such a,
this seems a good venture to leave alone. The ore is so
refractory, owing to the presence of copper, that the
tailings cannot be treated by cyanide, although they are
said to be worth about £2 a ton. The first two managers,
Bizant and Koehler, were worried into their graves in
trying to solve the problem of successfully treating the
ore.

for the British and Sulphide Corporation (Central) Mines
at Broken Hill are fine examples of present-day skill and
technical knowledge. The Mines will effect considerable
saving by means of these Aerialways as compared with
any other known mechanical contrivance for handling
ores on a large scale. The British Mine will economise
to over 2s. per ton. These tw plants have already been
subjected to severe tests, and have amply demonstrated

by W. and J. Lampriere, of Melbourne, who represent A.

The introduction into the Broken Hill and
Western Australian Mines of a small In.i.d rock-drill for
boring “ pops ” to break up large blocks of ore, cutting
“ hitches ” for timber, and squaring up shafts arid levels,
has quite revolutionised the old method of working with
hammers and gad. The best machine for this work is the
“ Shaw ” Hand Rock Drill and is manufactured by the
Austral Otis Engineering Company, Ltd., Melbourne,
who are the sole agents. The machine weighs only 161bs.
and has onlv one working part—the hammer—which
serves the purpose also of a valve. One man with this
machine can easily do as much work as four men with
hammer and drill. In some of the hardest lodes in the
Boulder these machines bore over lin. a minute, and in
softer country as much as 2Jin. per minute. Unlike other
types, the operator does not experience any excessive
vibration due to the recoil of the hammer. The working
pressure is 801bs. per square inch and the machine uses
about 25 cubic feet of free air per minute. Ten of the
biggest mines in West Australia are now using the Shaw
Hand Rock drills. Fullest particulars may be obtained
from the Company’s offices in South Melbourne, where
the machine may be seen working.* [c]

The whirling and whizzing at the Royal Agri-
cultural Show this Easter week just about establishes a
record, and the whizziest of the whirl is on Arthur
Leplastrier’s quarter-acre Machinery block, where all
kinds of “ Robey” Steam and Oil Engines are adding to
the hum, the more conspicuous being Compound Trac-
tion Engines, Portable Engines, Thrashing Machines,
Electric light Engines, Dairy Engines and steam and oil
engines for everything. The most unique contrast ever
displayed in powers are two Improved “ Robey ” Portable
Engines in motion, representing the largest as well as the
smallest Portable Engines ever seen in the Southern
Hemisphere, viz., an 87-h.p. Portable Engine, which
weighs 15 tons and is en route to a bush saw milling in-
stallation, and a “ Tom Thumb ” type of one-tenth horse
power with one-inch cylinder and of a few.pounds weight,
yet with every detail absolutely perfect; the exhibit is
consequently an instructive example to those interested
in this most convenient type of Portable steam motive
power. “Robey” Engines emanate from a British es-
tablishment with a reputation exceeding half-a-century,
which is appropriately named “Globe Works,”and asthe
Engines have in a variety of ways pioneered the world,
including the Australian mining, pastoral, sawmilling,
dredging, etc., industries, the representative exhibit on
Arthur Leplastrier’s ground is exciting even the most
blasO mind to keen interest.*

ALOITINS
‘ ‘ Hassan ” : Along our eastern seaboard
lives a fish which, in its color-changing capacity,
beats the far-famed chameleon. It is the common
blackfish (Girella triouspidata). This accom-
plished person feeds on a certain green weed
from April to October, flinging itself upon the
rocks just awash at high tide, and worrying the
stuff off as a dog worries a bone. Having fat-
tened themselves all the winter, the fish go to a
quiet corner of the beach to spawn, and swim
about near the sandy bottom, refusing bait.
While they are on the reefs they range in color
from a beautiful ruddy bronze to a purple black,
but, once upon tbe sand, they change to a deli-
cate fawn color, with slightly-darker bars across
the back. They come in from the reefs dark, and
in five minutes are the color of the sand. But
the most remarkable changes-of color I have ever
seen were displayed by a small squid I caught a
couple Of years ago at Cronulla.. One of his
transformations was a beautiful ruby-red,.another
a vivid fluorescent green. Just as a dying man
has paroxysms of muscular action, so this beast
had paroxysms of color, wave after wave of chang-
ing tints running through the transparent flesh,
gradually lessening in intensity and frequency as
the life ebbed.
“The Prooslian ” : Re “F.B.’s” inquiry
(B. 14/3/’O7) as to how a person dies from snake-
bite. I may say that, so far, I haven’t died on
snake-bite lines, but can soberly inform “F. 8.”
that he won’t wave his arms and yell in his last
moments. Quite the opposite. The poison of
our Australian snakes causes no paralysis to speak
of, but failure of the heart’s action, without im-
peded respiration. There will, however, be a
rapid obliteration of consciousness, with dis-
ordered sensory functions. The painful feature
about snake-bite isn’t the snake, but the patient’s
relatives, who break in on him with violent
remedies to keep him from sleeping into the next
shift.
tr a * *
“ BurnG.” : “ F. 8.” (B. 14/3/’O7) queries
whether death from snakebite is painless, or is it
etiquette to yell. Cannot give conclusive answer,
but can say, from experience, that different snake-
bites have different effects on the bit one. Have
seen a nigger, bitten by an adder, die in great
agony; and on another occasion saw one, when
bitten by the small Westraiian black snake, give
up the ghost without a remark. But, of course,
niggers can’t be taken seriously. Besides, they
weren’t the same nigger, and the difference might
lie in the man, and not in the;snake. What ! I
would like to know is—supposing a man were
bitten simultaneously by two totally different
varieties of snake, would anything unusual
happen ?

“Batlow,” in reply: Ee the borso’s in-
telligence. It seems that I, like 1 my stable mate,
“Prooshan,” have struck a “ John Moran” in thd
person of one “ Duroc ” (B. 7/'3/’o7j. He says he
has been among horses all his life,"arid haS come,
to the conclusion that, “for crass idiocy,horse’
is the limit.” Well, it’s rude to tell a man he’s
mad, and silly to argue with a lunatic, but I must’
do both in this case. Does “Duroc’’not know
that the common sense in man that rises superior
to ordinary common sense is known as “ common
horse sense,” or “horse common sense”? Did
“Duroc” learn at Fitzgerald’s Circus that the
bullock’s intelligence exceeded that of the horse ?
Some horses, like some men, are idiots. Some
men own horses, and some horses own men.
“Duroc” has owned all the idiotic horseflesh.
Well, set an idiot to buy an idiot. Yet, all
is well, for if I bought “Duroc’s” horses
for him, they’d have him running in a sulky
or hauling wood in a week. A horse an
idiot! Ye gods! Did “ Duroc ” ever see an old
rogue “ rumping ” round a big tree in the bush
to elude the searcher’s eye in passing? Did
“Duroc” ever try to ride his “idiot” up to a
sapling for a switch. Has “ Duroc ” ever owned
an idiot that could muffle his bell in grazing? I
have. I also knew one “Brewer” employed in
raising stock-water on Nap Nap, that required
no driver. As the full bucket reached the
bumper at the top, a knock was given, and the
concussion, operating on the bucket trigger, re-
leased the water. The moment “ Brewer ” heard
the knock, he stopped dead, and listened till the
last drop of water was out, then he reversed, to
let the empty bucket down, another full one iis-
ing as it descended. Promptly at the knock he
stopped again till the outflow was completed,
then off the other way, and so on till the 10,000-
gallon tank was full, when he calmly waited till
a man came to take him out. He never had a
deficit, and must have multiplied the 30-gallon
bucket by the number of knocks. Let “Duroc”
send one of his bullocks to do that, or, failing that,
let “Duroc” (if unacquainted with the language
of man) take the job on himseP

“The Moodier”: Horses have lots of
sense. Often when riding along a strange timber
fence looking for a slip-rail have had to fairly
kick Carbine past a certain panel. Riper ex-
perience has taught me, in such a case, to let him
have his head. He may pull up at a most un-
likely-looking panel. There won’t be the least
sign that you can see of a slip-rail; the mortises
look tight, &c. But try it. Ten to one it has
long ago been used as a slip-rail, and you can get
through it. As a slip-rail finder the horse beats
mere man to shreds. He is the surest slip-rail
divining-rod on earth.

What is suspected of being a relative of
the Tantanoola tiger—if not the very identical
itself—has been causing a scare in South Gipps-
land (Vic.). George Heslop, who feeds his flocks
on the scrub-covered sandhills between the Pow-
lett River and Gape Paterson, recently found that
his jutnbueks were mysteriously disappearing,
and went forth to investigate. He found tracks
“like those of a great cat” leading to a water-
hole, but couldn’t see the thing that owned the
tracks. Next morning there were more tracks,
and the sheep man suggested a hunting party.
The local sports weren’t wildly enthusiastic at
first; they didn’t understand the business, and,
besides, they were busy with the. ploughing, or
the cows, or something ; but at last a band of the
more daring spirits got its gun, and advanced in
fairly good order into the scrub. It failed to get
in touch with the enemy. Tracks there were
galore, and slaughtered sheep, and a dim, mys-
terious air of impending tragedy over all the bush,
but no visible wild beastie. The populace took to
staying at home o’ nights, and putting the parlor
sofa against the door when it went to bed. Also
it kept the children at home from, school for
safety. A plaster cast of the hoof-marks, measur-
ing Gin. by sin., was sent to vet.-surgeon Kendall,
of Melbourne, who couldn’t make much of it, and
Le Souef, of the Zoo, didn’t know either, because
the impression was too indistinct. Then someone
found three hairs on a bush, and asked Le Souef
what he made of them. , but they proved to be off
a dog, and it isn’t a dog that the Gippslanders
are looking for. So a terrified community still
takes its gun when it goes cow-hunting.
* * * *
“ L.0.P.”: Of the horses I have ridden,
have never yet struck one that wasn’t a reliable
homer. Even in the city, have known horses to
find their way home at night through miles of
closely intersected thoroughfares, which were
quite strange to them, without even turning a
wrong corner. As for bush horses, I reckon
every horse in this district is a homer. Have
been out shooting on the Welshpool Plains,
riding an old white horse called Ben, and have
repeatedly left it to him to take me home after
dark, a distance of over 15 miles. This country
is covered with grasstree and honeysuckle in one
monotonous stretch, interspersed with many
dangerous bogs, and here and there a wire fence.
A new chum would have no hope of getting back
to where he started from, once he got well out
on the plains after dusk, as there are no con-
spicuous landmarks and no worn tracks. Yet l
have given this old horse the rein on a pitch-
dark night, and he has never failed to land me
home safely. He doesn’t pull up at other home-
steads, like “Duroc’s” horse, possibly because
he knows I am a respectable married man.

It is the commercial traveller who finds
the many changes of climate and water trying.
The experience of Mr. Chas. G. Chapman, who
represents a large Brisbane concern, is not the
exception. He had been troubled for years with
chronic diarrhoea, and was especially bad when in
North Queensland. On one of his trips a fellow
traveller recommended him to try Chamberlain’s
Colic, Cholera, and Diarrhoea Remedy, which he
did, and what he has to say is most interesting :
“ I procured a bottle the first opportunity, and ex-
perienced great relief after taking a few doses,
and was cured before the bottle was finished. I
have not been troubled since, and wish to recom-
mend this wonderful medicine to anyone suffering
from the same complaint.”
Carry a bottle of Chamberlain’s Colic, Cholera,
and Diarrhoea Remedy in your hand-luggage
when travelling. It cannot be procured on board
the train or steamship. For sale everywhere,
Is. 6d.*

in a hundred miles of Perth
(W.A.), although nearly a

spent by one foolish Govern-

attempts to dam them back.

goorlie, simply caused bunny

low. He found the sand nice
and friable, and shifted it from
under the fence. Prospectors
away-back smiled at

vaguely hinted that
they gave the rabbits a

a welcome change from
everlasting tiiihed

en At any
. fate, rabbits
' were soon
as numerous
on one side

the other. Then
the authorities
started an inner

The result was just
the same ; so now the

Kangaroos are becoming
more plentiful, too, since
close districts were proclaimed
for them. They are of great
size and of prodigious appe-
tite : one mob recently, in a
single night, cleared a 40-
acre wheat crop as it was
ripening. Yet the distracted
farmer was told by the Agri-
cultural Department that he
could only shoot a kangaroo
occasionally for food !

“ David H. Richards ” ; “John Moran ”
B. 7/2/’O7) is howling up the wrong vegetable
when he says that no Australian snake slimes its
prey. I can from personal observation testify
to the practice in four Australian brands at least,
and recent inquiry on the subject has adduced in-
numerable instances of it in others. Up near
Bingara (N.S.W.) once, a mate and I watched a
tiger snake, after catching a small bird, slime it
and then bolt it. Another time on Donald
Munro’s Widgelray selection (Walgett, N.S.W.),
I saw a bird that had been slimed by a brown
snake when I disturbed it. Saw some time since
at Tooloora Bore (near Walgett, N.S.W.) a,young
bunny slimed by a carpet snake that was disturbed
in his labors by a man with a gun. Also, at
Carinda (N.S.W.), saw a jew lizard that had been
slimed by a snake whose make neither I nor my
mate could determine. I showed “John Moran’s ”
par. to a miner and an opal-buyer at Lightning
Ridge, and they swore till they got so thirsty that
they shouted, and when they were moistened
they recited a bushel of instances of snakes sliming
their prey. Afterwards I found out nearly every-
one has seen prey slimed by snakes.

NEARER ’OME.
A man has left Creswick (Vic.) in order to die in Aber-
deen when the comet strikes the earth. —Age.
A Rivevitia (N.S.W.) stock inspector re-
cently mentioned one way in which the deadly
anthrax germ can be propagated. A cow died
from the disease, and its economical owner buried
it in a cultivation paddock. A luxuriant crop of
oats grew over the spot, and some of the growth
was given to a horse as green stuff. Ihe horse
promptly contracted anthrax, and the farmer had
another funeral on hand. He burned the horse,
but it would have been a lot cheaper to have
burned the c<nv. Sometimes one feels inclined to
burn that ki. d of farmer.

MY HOESE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
My old brown horse has but one ear,
One eye with which to see,
And only some short hairs appear
Where his tail used to be.
He has a classic Roman head,
Four legs of sorts —and thus
He’s still a useful quadruped,
By name Caractacus.
Some folks might say he’s not equine,
The way his napper droops,
And though his snort be gen-u-ine,
He’s rigged on barrel hoops.
He’s got the halt, he’s got the jolts,
He’s got the housemaid’s knee ;
His coat is shaggy, though he moults
With continuity.
His wobblesomeness is to blame
If he is hard to follow,
For never horse was half so tame,
Nor horse’s back so hollow.
His front hoofs cover lots of ground,
And one hind leg’s peculiar ;
But still his pedigree is sound
(His mother’s name was Julia).
Across his loins my wallet flaps,
Before me is my swag,
And ’gainst his chest, secured with straps,
There ain’t no water-bag.
I sit me in the vacancy,
My chin among the flour,
And we pass through the scenery
At quite a mile an hour.
Anon I say, to cheer him up :
“ My horse, thou art an ass,
To sulk like some distemper’d pup,
Because there is no grass.
“ One of these days I’ll find a job,
And you will get a spell ;
I'm sure you’ll fetch quite twenty hob
When you are fat and well.
“ There are some rivers in this part
Could wet the two of us,
And grass enough to fill a cart—
Gee up, Caractacus ! ”
I haul upon the steering gear
To elevate his head ;
He agitates his only' ear,
And staggers back instead.
I whack him with an infant log,
I bob me high and low,
As one who rides a jiggery-jog,
To make the crawler go.
I click my tongue, and hiss and shoo,
Gesticulate and swear ;
I pound him with my leathers, too,
And pull his caudal hair.
He never alters in his stride
(He takes quite ten a minute) ;
He merely snorts and swerves aside
To show that he’s agin it.
I settle down unto the trip,
And whistle “ Yankee Doodle ”:
And then he treads upon his lip,
And chucks the whole caboodle.
If anybody wants to buy
A low-priced household neddy,
I’ve got that article, and I
Can guarantee he’s steady.
Though parts of him are lost, and he
May look somewhat peculiar,
He’s got at least some pedigree
(His mother’s name was Julia).
He has a classic Roman head,
Four legs of sorts —and thus
He’s gen-u-ine, and, till he’s dead,
His name’s Caractacus.
N.S.W.
EDWARD S. SORESSON.

The great task of organising the production of
a monthly which will be worthy of Australia now
approaches completion, and the proprietary is able
to announce that The-Lone Hand will be in the
hands of 50,000 Australian readers on May 1.
The Editor can now take Bulletin readers —
from whom he expects a generous support to The
Lone Hand— somewhat into his confidence with
regard to the “ bill of fare.”
In the first number will begin “The Memoirs
of J. F. Archibald, Founder of The Bulletin.”
For the first time J. F. Archibald breaks the seal
of press anonymity, and takes Australians into
his confidence. These memoirs make a human
document of absorbing interest. A Photo-
graphic Study of J. F. Archibald, by W.
Maclpod—printed on fine plate paper, suitable for
framing—will be presented with the magazine.
Articles by “ Hop” on fiddle-making; by Frank
Myers on W. B. Dailey; by representative Parisian
journalists on the literary life of Europe ; by
Australian writers of note on literary, dramatic,
and art subjects will form the solid background
to a bright, varied mass of reading. There will
be many short stories from well-known pens—
Henry Lawson contributing the shortest, and one
of the best ; original verse by Victor Daley,
Edward Dyson, Arthur Adams, Roderic Quinn,
and others ; and scores of glittering sentences of
wit and paradox.
Artists represented will be “Hop,” Norman
Lindsay, Lambert, Minns, Spence, Lionel
Lindsay, Weston, Vincent, Rodway, Sass, and
others.
The Lone Hand, besides its mission to amuse,
will have a serious purpose in guarding the public
against frauds and cheats. Watch for the ex-
posures in the first and succeeding numbers.
The surest way of securing The Lone Hand is
to send MOW a subscription of. 12s. for a year,
either direct to this office or to your news-agent.
Those who trust to casual buying may be dis-
appointed.

The OLD FIRM
To a business man the statement
that an old-established Firm turns
out a better and cheaper product is
a truism.
Choice Diamonds and 1
Pearl Ring, £l2 12s.
The stamp of old customers’ continued approval has made it an
established firm.
WHY IS IT ?
Experience has taught the short cuts to success.
The old firm has been down most roads—been there before ; then,
of course, capital counts.
Cash buying shows on cost tickets.
Output influences, too, as big buyers buy best.
Reliability—after three-quarters of a century you feel a firm can be
relied upon.

They were man and wife. He was 4(5 ;
she, perhaps, a couple of years younger. They
cirried swags of liberal poundage, and they
c imped on the roadside, amongst the trees, near
Cody’s holding. After dinner the woman rigged
the tent while the man went up to the road-
making job, a quarter of a mile away, in quest of
work. The tent was the craziest thing Cody had
ever seen, and there were more patches than
original canvas, but the woman rigged it in a work-
man-like style, and then threw up a breakwind of
large branches with the expertness of a black gin
and the energy of three. Next she cut two seven-
foot logs, and attached to them a sail-cloth
contrivance on two battens, which so excited
Cody’s curiosity that he stopped ploughing to
investigate. The woman explained that it was a
collapsible double bed, and described how it Was
rolled up for a seat when not needed as a couch.
Halban-hour later Cody heard the woman’s voice
raised in anger, and, looking across, discovered
her shaking a stick at a slouching man with a rag-
bag swag on his back. Said the woman to the
agriculturist a few minutes later: “I hate them
tramps. They never have a decent house them-
selves, and just put in their time tryin’ to impose
on those as has.”

“Ropata”: What follows may be con-
sidered a sample of the Hinglish language as
spoken by some whites at the Bay of Plenty
(M.L.). jack, from the Oripi heights, was down
at the hostelry for rest and recuperation. Oneeven-
ing, fortified by sundry long beers, he had much
to communicate. In the midst of his monologue
he used the words “Yous was;” immediately
paused to consider, then said, “You was.” He
explained the change this way: “You noticed
the mistake I made ? A lot of people round here
don’t know how to speak properly. I told Paddy
in the bar last night that he should never say
‘ Yous was ’ when he spoke to one person. ‘ Yous
was ’is for two ; ‘You was’ for one. Amn t I
right? Bless me, but they don’t know much
here. Paddy said to me ‘ Yous,’ meaning ‘ You ’
—that was one. ‘Yous’ by itself, of course, as I
told him, meant y-e-w-s—female rams. Was I
right?” I assured him he was right. Thus the
light is spread abroad.

“ J.M.” describes the making of a
champion:—
The bookmaker and his clerk stood watching the old
fellow suffering a recovery. Presently the bookie said—
“ Suppose, Dad, when you were a young fellow, you were
a pretty good man ? ” His beery eye travelled slowly up
and down the bookie. “ No, young fel’r, I never was no
blanky good for nothing.” The bookie promptly gave
him half-a-crown. “ You’re the first old man I’ve met ”
he said, “ that wasn’t a champion in his young days.”
The old man with the limekiln in his mouth, staggered
into the bar and said “ Rum ” three times. In ten minutes
he lurched out, with a ruddy glow on his hard old dial,
and swaggering up to the clerk said, “ Givvus a match.”
“ Get out, you’re drunk,” said the clerk. “ What’s that,
you weed?” he demanded. “I don’t allow no blanky
waster to sling off at me. Forty-five year ago I was the
best blanky man on Gulgong, and” —here a greasy
beaver was dashed in the dust —“ I’ll take a round out of
any on you now.”

“Rail” : When you work for the Bogan
River (N.S.W.) squatter you’ve got to take the
lean with the fat. Out Dandaloo way, a rabbiter
was entrusted with a Winchester rifle by the big
man. He didn't come back ;so it was reckoned
he was bushed among the gohannas and ants.
The squatter, with an eye to his repeating rifle,
organised a search party, and, after two days, they
got back, full of failure and despondency. Later
on, the local trooper brought word that Bill had
been seen down at Orange, sans the rifle. Then
the squatter wrote a letter cursing Socialism, and
when the rabbiters want to draw their cheques
they found two days’ pay stopped for being
absent from work.

Queensland’s black labor devil was really
created by the grasping mercantile soul of one
man. It was Bobbv Towns, whose name still
haunts wharves and
stores in Sydney, who
first harnessed the glossy
Polynesian to the
plough apd the harrow.
He had been using Poly-
nesians on his copra-
gathering boats in the
South Seas, and when
he started to grow cot-
ton on the Logan River
he quietly landed a lot
of his darkies in an un-
frequented part of the
bay, and conveyed
their, to his farm with-
out the knowledge of
the Government or any-
one else. One dAy
Brisbane was startled
by seeing black bullock-
drivers switching Bobby
Towns’ cotton teams
through the public
streets. It almost led
to a riot at the time.
Queensland had got rid
of assigned convicts,
and Chinese had been
found useless for heavy
farming or station work, but now the State was
confronted with the coolie, who took almost
nominal wages and dossed in a dog kennel. Out
of this sprang the Government provisions for-
recruiting, and all the other sham regulations
for the “humane” management of the traffic
that went on till the Commonwealth smashed the
system. # ,

A CAMPING IDYLL.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Beneath the trees ’bis wondrous sweet
Canoodling girls with shoeless feet;
And in the sultry summer days
To lie at ease and idly gaze
Across the ba\- from our retreat.
To hear the sheep’s complaining bleat,
And mock the grilling summer heat,
As Christmas plums we slowly eat
Beneath the trees.
Friends come and go on busy feet,
Departing soon with cough discreet;
In coolest shade ’tis good, you know,
To lounge here for a day or so,
When Ruby’s near and time is fleet,
And idly talk of joy and woe,
And flirt and kiss a time or so,
And hear the summer breezes blow
Among the trees.
And when we’re back perchance we’ll fret
For bygone times with vain regret,
For scattered friends and sunburnt knees
And wind-blown hair and idle ease,
And loathe the city etiquette.
The camping days must pass, and yet
The camping days we won’t forget,
Nor friends that you and I first met
Beneath the trees.
M.L.

ANSWERS to CORRESPONDENTS.
No notice (except of a hostile character) will he taken of
communications insufficiently stamped. Name and
address must appear on MSS. No liability is under-
taken re contributions voluntarily submitted, whether
sent by post or handed in; and no MS. will be
returned unless stamps (of any State) accompany.
Keypiece : Questions rather intricate. But
this is perhaps the information you require : (1) Under
present gunnery conditions it would be impossible to
laud every shell at a distance of 12 miles inside a space
10ft. x loft. ; no patent sight would make that possible ;
variations in the gun, in the charge, in the atmospheric
and »'ind conditions, all affect accuracy of shooting. In
snrapiiel shell shooting the idea is to'burst the shell over
the target, not to hit it. (2) No propulsive force has yet
been invented which can for lofig resist the attractive
force of gravitation. (3) A shell.in flight travels at vary-
ing speeds. The greatest velocity is at the muzzle of the
gun ; the velocity gradually decreases until the moment
wnen the attractive force overcomes the propulsive, and
the shell begins to drop at an accelerating rate. (3)
Yes, it is a true arc — i.e., part of a curved line,
but, not necessarily a true arc of a circle

ANOTHER CRY FROM THE TOMB.
Voice from the Cavern: “No. I don’t want the Federal Government to get me any immigrants. I don’t want it to help any N.S.W. people that
are stranded in Africa. I won’t sell it the Sydney Observatory; and, all the same, if the Federal Observatory isn’t in Sydney I’ll say it’s
another Victorian conspiracy. What’s that ? No, I won’t be civil. What do I want ? 1 want to be let alone to enjoy my grievances.
What d’ye say? No, I won’t come out!"
( Owing to the blackness of the interior of the cave the artist wasn't able to draw the voice.)
