PERSONAL
ITEMS
John D. Rockefeller, the U.S. Oil
Monarch, is said to intend devoting £10,000,000
to the work of christianising China. All his 60
millions have failed to christianise Rockefeller.
Died suddenly, in London, lately, Thomas
Bewick, who used to be senior partner of the
mine-engineering firm of Bewick, Moreing and
Co. He went out of the hustling business in
1901, leaving the Australian, Algernon Moreing,
at the head.
Queensland ex-Premier Philp has had luck
lately in his mining specs. That accounts for his
policy speech. He thought he was writing a
wild-cat prospectus, and promising everybody
100 per cent, dividends.
Drowned, on March 10, while bathing in
the Richmond River, at Casino (N.S. W.), William
Duncan, building contractor, of Paddington (Syd-
ney), who, at the late Federal elections, contested
Wentworth against Willie-Motor Kelly in the
Labor interest.
Few men of long standing in the Public
Service have left N.S.W. with so little comment,
favorable or unfavorable, as Barling, late chair-
man of the Public Service Board, one time
Under-Secretary for Public Works, and the holder
of many high offices in the State. He was a color-
less, ordinary man, who cast no shadow worth
mentioning when he was there, and created no
visible hole when he ceased to be there.
Another heir to lost millions and a title
has turned up in Melbourne in Thomas James
Henley, late licensee of a country pub on the
road to Fern Tree Gully. Thomas says he is the
grandson of Sir Thomas, a Gloucestershire bart.,
with a mansion and property worth five millions,
no less. The only unusual point about the tale is
that the five millions don’t appear to be in
“Chancery” ; they are apparently just lying
around loose, waiting for someone to annex them.
These English aristocrats are a frightfully casual
lot,
Sydney Evening News discussed naval
defence, the other day, in relation to the build-
ing of torpedo boats and destroyers,* and decided
that “ it is probable that, together with the mate-
rials, both machinery and experts would have to
be imported, in which case the vessels might, to
much greater advantage, be made in England.”
Almost on the day that the News poured this edi-
torial hogwash over its countrymen, it was
announced that “Mr. V. Bennett, a young Aus-
tralian mining engineer, son of the late Christo-
pher Bennett, sailed by the Ventura, having re-
ceived an important mining appointment in
Mexico.” Even the News Bennetts can produce
Australian engineers for export to do great things
in Mexico, but it holds these engineers not good
enough to build a cockleshell in Australia.
DEAKIN, THE DECORATOR.
Mr. Deakin declared that the defence of Australia was
an Australian responsibility, which could not be effec-
tually discharged without benefiting the Empire as well
as the Commonwealth. On the subject of the Imperial
Council, the Prime Minister said: “ The more plastic the
organisation is the freer its powers of adaptation must
be. The oversea dominions have made, and are making,
great strides in the development of the immaterial re-
sources and the creation of sentiments of local as well as
of general patriotism. When the populations become
adequate to their territories they will be in every way
fitted, and, I believe, most willing, to undertake their
full share of the responsibility in the task of governing
the vast dominions which are growing and prospering to-
day in every quarter of the globe under the British flag.”
Australia: “ That's very pretty, Alfred, but
what the dickens does it all mean ? ”
Captain William Foskett, who sailed last
week for England, is an old N.S. W. civil servant.
He entered the Stamp Duties Office as a stamper
in 1865, when William Hemming, the man who
introduced the house sparrow to Australia, was
Commissioner (Hemming had been in business in
Sydney as a law stationer, and when the Stamp
Act was passed was appointed as an “expert”
Commissioner). When the Stamp Act was re-
pealed and the office disbanded, Foskett remained,
the Robinson Crusoe of the place, with, a salary
but no work. Then another Stamp Act was
passed, and he set to work again, and stamped
things for all he was worth for the best part of a
generation. For 30 years he was secretary of the
Rifle Association.
E. D. Riddell, the early N.S.W,
Colonial Treasurer whose grandson has
revived the old man’s memory in
Australia, was the direct cause
of Governor Richard Bourke
suddenly quitting N.S.W.
Bourke had made a
favorite of Roger
Therry, and
heaped offices
upon him.
The chairman-
ship of Quarter
Sessions, at
£4OO a year,
fell vacant,
and, although
themagistracy
had the right
to elect a
chairman,
Bourke nom-
inated Therry
for the post.
Riddell, Colonial II
Treasurer, offer-
ed himself in op-
position. Bourke,
on the ground that
Riddell had one
billet already,
erased his name from
the list of Legislative
Councillors. Riddell
appealed to the Secre-
tary of State, pointing
out that Therry had
several billets. The
Secretary of State ordered
Riddell’s name to be rein-
stated as a member of
the Council. Thereupon
Governor Bourke gathered
up his hair-brush and his
other collar and resigned.
All over two hungry billet-
hunters and a four-hundred-
pound job.

Joseph Hector Garrick (not Carruthers),
who died in Auckland, the other day, aged 60,
was brother to the late Agent-General for Queens-
land. As a young barrister of 26, he went to Fiji
in ’73, and became Associate Supreme Court
Judge with Charles St. Julian, binder the whitey-
brown Government of Cakobku Rex. After the
British annexation of the islands, Garrick was
Attorney-General for a while, and subsequently
had a considerable Bar practice. In later years,
he was made Crown Prosecutor at Suva. Alto-
gether, he put in over 30 years of service in Fiji.
Shortly before London “universal pro-
vider” Whiteley was shot dead by a man who
claims to be his unrecognised son, he was one of
a number of contributors to Cassell’s Magazine
on “ Clues to Success in Business.” Some of the
upright Whiteley’s moral maxims have a prophetic
ring :
The real secret of commercial success is, I think, to be
found in two words : “Be honest.” However successful
deceit and trickery appear to be at first, it will be found
that in the long run they will cost you very dearly.
Observe the utmost discretion in all your business trans-
actions, and never take a risk that is likely to cause you
inconvenience.
The shining light took just one risk too many, and
the risk shot at him.
John Forrest, temporary political boss ot
the Commonwealth, is one of the few men who
have kept a full purse while holding high
office in Australia. John See held on to
his money, and Braddon had a pension to
rest on, but most other prominent politi-
cians were stalked by the ghost of those
two eternal ends that won’t meet. Some
were overtaken and killed by the uncanny
follower. Forrest not only has a pile of
his own, but there is a pension of £5OO a
year on account of his participation in
Westralian affairs before the days of Re-
sponsible Government, which comes due
when he ceases to collect a salary for poli-
tical services. What a gallery of poverty-
stricken citizens Australian Premiers look
when compared with the pig-dealing, calico-
importing, beer-and-whisky gangs of thrifty
commercial men for whom they make laws !
The Pitt-street Congregational tabernacle,
where'" “Mr. James” was married 50 years
ago, has given the importing habit best. After
Tremayne Dunstan and pastor Hennessy, it
wouldn’t trust itself to go further from home than
North Sydney. Curiously, at the moment when
it offered the job to N. J. Cocks, of that place, he
was considering an offer to go to North Adelaide.
After the usual “ prayerful consideration ” —so he
told the North Adelaide people—he decided on
the stand in the main street. The result seems to
be the same whether you give prayerful considera-
tion, or just the plain, grocery sort.
Walter Bennett, the new N.S. W.
“ Honorable,” has been a quarter of a cen-
tury in country journalism. He gave Aus-
tralia the straight tip in the Moruya Times,
the Dungog Chronicle, and other great organs
of public opinion long before he first squashed
the cushions of Parliament. Bennett was a
popular man with members from the day he
entered the House. He is a Maorilanderby birth,
and his family goes back to the first stealing of
the brown cannibal’s country.
Died, at Cloncurry (North Queensland),
Alexander Sykes Macgillivray, one of the com-
paratively early men of squatting on the West
Queensland downs country. He came to his
father's station, Eddington, half-way between.
Richmond and Cloncurry, in 1870, and went
storekeeping in Cloncurry in 1882. He was ruined
by his “whiteness.” All the wasters of the
country owed him money, and a fire in ’9l blew
him out. He started squatting again at Leila
Vale, had more ill-luck, and went to live in Bris-
bane. Coastal air didn’t agree with him, and he
had to return to the highlands. Finally, having
fought drought and ill-luck, and untrustable cus-
tomers, and fire and drought again, he started on
scratch in Cloncurry as an agent and auctioneer,
knowing that valvular heart trouble was ready to
kill him if he let himself go for a moment. After
15 years, it struck him the other day. He was
54 when he passed out—a quiet, plucky battler
against bad seasons and misplaced confidence in a
few others, a good Australian, and a real white
man. Peace be with him !

Judge F. E. Rogers (N.S.W.), who has
again had temporary possession of a Supreme
Court seat, has had varied judicial experience.
He has done acting business on every Bench. He
was even on the Bench of the Land Appeal Court,
when it was proposed to make the billet of Presi-
dent a judicial appointment. He had £2OOO a
year then, and left, it is understood, because he
was refused the pension and status of a Supreme
Court Judge. When he first accepted an Acting
District Court Judgeship, it was on the under-
standing that he would be offered an early
Supreme Court vacancy. In the end, however,
he settled down as D.C. Judge.
Premier Bent is going for a holiday and
rest, on his doctor’s advice —and this is his pro-
gramme, SO far: —
Keep an eye on the Imperial Conference, and chip in if
he gets a chance.
Talk to the London and Westminster Bank re inscrip-
tion of stock.
Argue about reduced passages for immigrants.
Inquire about electrifying the suburban railways.
Investigate Agent-General Taverner’s comings and
goings.
Give illustrated lectures around the island.
Attend the Education Conference.
Gather information re Commonwealth loans to cover
State debts.
Allegedly get married (though he has denied this).
Also, any little matter that needs attention or any
statement that requires contradiction will be
attended to in spare moments.
Mrs. Rebecca Martha Meares, of Stan-
more (N.S.W.), who died in January last, left an
estate, valued at £52,218, to her sons, Frederick
Devenish Meares and Ashley John Devenish
Meares. The “Devenish ” Meares were a large
family. William Devenish Meares was police
magistrate at Mudgee, and retired, at the age of
60, on pension in 1889. Then there was Alexander
Devenish Meares, sheriff’s officer at Grafton ;
F. Devenish Potter Meares, C.P.S. at Newtown;
John Devenish Meares, a superintendent of
police; and another "Devenish” was Council
Clerk at Leichhardt. Also there are sons who
are likewise “Devenish.” And half the time the
hurried postman pronounces it “Devilish,” just
as the slavey handed in Mr. Swiveller’s letter
with a wet hand, and said abruptly that it was
for Mr. Snivelling.
Retired from the Victorian Railways, E.
J. Goode, chief storekeeper, aged 61. Reaching
Melbourne in ’52, youngster Goode saw the rush
and riot of the gold days, and at 16 entered the
service of the St. Hilda and Brighton Railway
Co., as clerk and embryo accountant, was passed on
to the Hobson’s Bay Railway Co., and then taken
into the State service. Goode possesses a quaint
time-table of that first St. Hilda line —seven trains
a day during the week, and five on Sundays, and
the suburbanites had to keep good hours, for all
traffic ceased at 8 p.m. Later, a theatre train
was granted one nigh* a Week, at Is. 6d. per pas-
senger, in addition to the ordinary fare. Thus
did private enterprise “ develop the resources ”in
’62. A quaint feature of this Brighton line was
the viaduct on trestles, to span what was then the
Albert Park swamp, from near St. Hilda -Jo
Windsor. The soft ground let the trestles down ;
the viaduct grew as lop-sided as the company’s
policy, and the trains had to stop at St. Hilda.
The co. never thought of repairing its viaduct; it
just abandoned the rest of its line. That old line
ends at St. Hilda to this day, but before the year
is out Bent’s" electric trams will probably run
right through to Melbourne, and supersede the
steam service altogether.
“Dodgyknees" writes;—
My lantern ! Ho ! there have only been two honest
men in Melbourne—George Higinbotham and Joseph
Symes—when you come to real out-and-out Quixotic
honesty. To be honest as this world goes is to be one
man among ten thousand—such a one as Duffy the First.
He went to John Stuart Mill, in London, and said : “I
am a Freetrader. Victoria is committed to Protection,
Ought Ito sacrifice my career? ” Mill answered (accord-
ing to Duffy), “ No.” Symes seemed to ask : “ What is for
my pecuniary interest?" and he would go direct in the
teeth of that. You can say of Joe Symes as was
said of John Knoxlt “ Here lies one who never
feared the face of man.” What a fine, upstand-
ing, stalwart, straightforward man he was! He
looked you straight in the eyes, with none of the
furtive, sly, cunning, Job Trotter instinct that is the
bane of the clerical. He was individualist throughout,
and reckoned all the world had to do was to abolish the
priesthood. Essentially, he was a modern Englishman.
The Englander never becomes an Australian. It is notin
the blood. Symes even opposed the Boers fiercely. The
strongpart of hislearning was astronomy. This led him to
his blank views. An intense Methodist told me of Symes’
death, and added, “ There was never a man more highly
esteemed in our denomination.” The literary outlook of
Symes was strangely cramped. I asked him, “ How do
you account for the magnificence of the Bible? ” “ Oh,”
said he, “ you are only deluded i y the splendor of the
English translations. There is no beauty in the originals.”
Thus Samuel Briggs ;
Even swelled head has its uses. There recently re.
tired from an Australian bank an old and pompous mes-
senger, who carried with him the goodwill of every clerk
in the institution. Theaneientretaineraped the haughty
air and heavenly deportment of the general manager.
He didn’t dare to wear a frock-coat, but he wore a black
silk dust-coat reaching almost to his knees, which came
mighty near it. Clad in this garment and carrying a
document in his hand, he walked about the bank at the
buiiest times of the day glaring majestically through his
glasses at awe-stricken strangers, and nodding with grave
patronage to those he knew ; and if someone mistook
him for a high official, his happiness was complete. But
what endeared him to the clerks was this : When a cleik
had offended some sour and important client of the insti-
tution, and the said client hied him away to report the
offender to the general manager, word was hurriedly
passed around, and as the wrathful depositor approachtd
the manager’s room, a tall, portly form, with much paper
in its hand, would emerge, looking as if it owned the
globe. Nine times out of ten the client complained bit-
terly to this figure, and the messenger’s assurance that the
offending clerk would be immediately boiled over a dead
fire sent many a man away happy.
“ Goolwa ” writes re Frederick Ogle, who
was a mighty spieler before the Lord, and who
recently, at the age of 61, was “ sent up ” for five
years in Melbourne :
I knew him once. It was some two years ago, and I
was boarding in a fashionable seaside suburb. One of
our boarders was a middle-aged widow, who was pos-
sessed of property. I heard, in profound secrecy, that
the lady was to be married shortly to an earl. The
landlady told me with great pride that his earlship
would call to tea to-morrow evening. I meekly inquired if
it were necessary to dress for dinner, because, if so, I’d
take my meal in the kitchen, as I’d lent my dress suit.
Great was the preparation (we had never had such a feed
before), and when our guest was announced you could
have heard a fly swimming in the soup. It was Ogle. He
entered dramatically, and it only required an orchestral
burst (worked up from pianissimo) to make the thing
complete. And he looked an earl. Dutton, who sat
next Frederick, called him “Sir,” which seemed to suit
down to the ground. The blue-blooded gentleman talked
a good deal of his Irish property, of a prospective trip
“ home,” and of placing his youngest son at Eton, ft
seemed he was a widower. We all retired quietly after tea,
and left him with his lady love. Ogle often called, and the
novelty of dining with earls wore off. Arrangements were
made for the wedding, the trousseau and matrimonial
gear got in order, bridesmaids selected, and even the
spirit-lamp, for use on the voyage home, was not over-
looked. Then delays started. Each night Ogle arrived
agitated. Cables were pouring in. Great legal compli-
cations had attacked the estates. Postponement fol-
lowed postponement. Still he went strong with the
widow, until Black and myself reckoned him and his
donah a damn nuisance. We couldn’t get in the dining-
room of an evening (where they were performing like a
pair of kittens), and, as it was winter, we sadly needed
the comfort of a fire. The landlady couldn’t think of
thwarting the Earl. Two of us left. I heard that Ogle
did the same shortly afterwards. It was only recently I
found out who he was. No doubt a clever spieler, but
I’m inclined to think that widow was cleverer. As far as
I could find out, he didn’t get from her even enough to
buy another pair of those beautiful pants he wore.

There is a tremendous amount of “act-
ing” about Australian politics and public service
just now. There is an acting Prime Minister,
acting Minister of External Affairs, acting Minis-
ter of Customs, acting P.M.G. ; and in Victoria
an acting Governor, acting Premier, acting
Treasurer, acting Minister of Railways, acting
Under-Treasurer, acting Chief Railways Com-
missioner. Papua has an acting Governor; and
in N.S. Wales the Public Service Board is mostly
acting, and the heads of the Treasury and the
Lands Department are acting, and the Sydney
Criminal sittings have been taken by an acting
Supreme Court Judge, and the Full Court has
been partly acting Judge. The “acting” doesn t
always mean the same thing, but a lot of times it
means that Australia is paying two lots of salaries.

THE VARNISHED BOARD
A WOMAN’S LETTER.
Sydney, March 26, 1907.
My Dear Arini,—
Lady Northcote’s at home on Thursday was
pervaded by the suburban mayoress, the execu-
tive committee of the Exhibition of Women’s
Work, and the weaker vessels of the press. It
was prompt business, and then tea. Her Federal
Ex. and JVliss llawson made a tour of the ball-
room, where all the useful persons were assembled
and standing against the wall. She shook hands
with the human dado, and then it moved for-
ward and sat at a long, long table, off which it
carefully kept its elbows. Miss Rawson, as vice-
president, read an interesting account of the
character of the projected Great Work. When
the closing words, “represent our State and win
laurels to its credit,” were reached, LadyNorthcote
arose, and for forty minutes the atmosphere had a
flavor of the “ Almanach de Gotha.” The names of
Serene Highnesses, Royal Highnesses of England,
and Tremendous Highnesses of Austria and
Greece were signed to the letters her Excellency
read, and if only the Akhoond of Swat and their
High Mightinesses the States-General of Holland
had been represented all would have been com-
plete. (Why were these High Mightinesses
allowed to die out ?) There was rather more than
a peck of crowned or coronetted notepaper. Then
the reading lapsed to letters of mere peeresses
and ambassadresses. Probably at the tail-end of
things was a note from Mary Smith, but there
wasn’t time to get down to Miss Smith’s letter.
Lady Chelmsford, Lady Edeline (pronounce it
’’edlin, two syllables, or you’ll have to go in by
t e tradesmen’s entrance) Strickland, Miss du
< me, Mrs. Harley, Mrs. Mayoress Hughes, and
1 *B. Allan Taylor were in the seats of the favored
few. Mrs. Ethel Turner Curlewis, who was pre-
sent, has the task of making a collection of the
works of Australasian women writers of prose
and verse for the Exhibition.
Lady Northcote, who is an exemplar of smiling
diplomacy, gave a cunning little curl to the tail of
her brief speech when she intimated that although
the Exhibition was one of women’s work the
assistance of men was necessary to carry it to a
successful conclusion. There was an apologetic
facet to her smile—a sort of after-all-the-man-
thing-has-liis-uses suggestion. This was, no
doubt, an indirect reply to the newspaper protest of
certain ladies that men should not run a Women’s
Work Show. The opinion of most women is that
if there was a woman secretary the other women
would bully her. If the whole executive were
women, there would be too much room for what
“Epacris,” in the D.T., calls “that detested
specimen of her sex, the * society pusher,’ or worse
still, * the organising woman.’ ”
The Feast of Lanterns was held at the Royal
Yacht Squadron’s Club House-by-the-sea last
Friday. A thousand lights of rose and orange, a
half-grown moon, a baby breeze, and a few picture
hats and evening gowns were divided amongst the
300 or 400 persons present. Feminine Sydney
huddles on its theatre coat and says, “ How dread-
fully cold ! ” if it chances to be abroad after 8 p.m.,
and if the temperature is below 65. Seventy
years ago, when the late Mr. Milson built him a
house (now the Club House), and planted him a
tree and a garden, and later than that, there were
blacks on the North Shore. That is one of the
crumbsof small talk that the Royal yachtsmen drop
into the ear of the visitor to their moonlight f&te.
The steeply sloping lawns merge into the sea and
the trees on the marge stand for ever with their
feet in the salt water. At the back of the Club
House a wealth of towering tree and interlaced
leaf looks like a tent of silk- A great place to
talk of Me and You. And the members have a
standing joke—now a bit shaky from long stand-
ing. . The stranger is told she can have the anchor
(or win a liberal wager if she can lift it) that seems
to be lying on the lower trunk of a mammoth
Moreton Bay fig. Said anchor was thrown on to
the tree when the latter was a young thing, but as
the tree grew it took in the anchor, and one prong
is now buried in its heart. Some day a visitor will
borrow the Strong Woman from a circus, and bring
her along disguised as his youngest daughter, and
she will tear out that anchor and spoil the jest.
The usual crowd of members and their friends
were present. In the absences of King Edward,
the president, and of Commodore Fairfax, the
vice-president, Mr. Reeks said the civil word in
the scented darkness of the jetty. The Fairest
Circumstance present was a visitor girl from
England—an eighteen-year-old youngster, in a
white silk frock, and big, pink velvet hat. Such a
perfect study in blue (eyes) and gold (hair) and
scarlet (lips) and faint-rose (face), and as mem-
bers of the Squadron caught sight of her under
the blaze of the electrics, they paid homage with
a note of exclamation. Her maternal progenitor
would have taken the eye if the daughter had
not been there. No mistake, but the dear old
Bull country is kind to its women’s complexions.
Mrs. Hemingway—the mother of the startlingly
Fair One—looked picturesque enough in a black
satin Romney hat and embroidered white silk
shawl, and other uncommon etceteras.
One of our richest squatters is in Sydney,
waiting for the carnival that follows this Ashen
Time. But he got tired of waiting, and the other
day said he was going to start his Easter
manoeuvres at once. So he engaged 11 hansoms
that were on the stand opposite his hotel, got
into one himself—he couldn’t occupy all-, and
solemnly drove about the city. Every row and
then he put his sheep’s head out to see „hat the
other ten vehicles were following. He paid them
all well. But isn’t he a humorist ? Now, an old
Bulletin contributor I wot of had more method
in his madness. He was a terrible Radical when
sober, but when the wine that was red got into
his veins it turned his blood blue, offensively blue.
He wouldn’t travel in the same cab with his own
coat and stick; so they, followed him about in
another hansom.
From an account of a marriage at a N.S.W.
country town : “Mr. Blank, as groomsman, sup-
ported the bridegroom, who beamed exultingly.”
Don’t you conjure up a picture of a man leaning
upon his friend at an angle of sixty-five degrees
and smiling fatuously ?
Miss Fanny Bauer, the Sydney song-bird, has
been warbling at Dublin Castle, and Miss Nora
Long has been engaged for Chappel’s concerts.
So Australia isn’t quite dead yet.
A French paper has discovered that King
Edward has, of all living monarchs, the largest
choice of headgear.
Among them is the round grey hat, known in Paris as
a “ melon,” and in London as a “ bowler.” This is a
style of which the King is particularly fond. He wears
it encircled with a thin black riband, and always travels
in it.
Then there is the Tyrolean, which may be of
brown velvet or greenish felt, and has a grouse or
partridge feather stuck in the band. Two kinds
of Scotch cap with a silver medal on one side are
used by the king, and his silk hats are white in
summer and black in winter. He has soft hats of
many shapes, and 30 varieties of cap. And yet
he isn’t a, hydra.
His Excellency Northcote’s progress through
the widely-smiling regions of Monaro and Mait-
land is tapering to a finish, and will end this week
at his quarters (with the sentry at the gate) over-
looking Farm Cove. The Governor of Tasmania,
Mr. Share, and a brace of Ministers formed the
vice-regal comet’s useful, if not glittering, tail.
He really travelled fast and far, and in every
township he was told, in speeches, of the resources
of the district; he was anthemed by hundreds
of school children, and always, for fear his spirits
would droop, he was shown over the local hospital.
The crowded day closed ever with a banquet, and
the weary representative of the King whohas more
hats than any living monarch laid his head, stuffed
with maize, sheep and wool statistics, on his rail-
way carriage pillow, and railed towards more
anthem and hospital and banquet and boundless
local resources.
“The Marble Dinner” is the latest freak
of the smart hostess in dear old England.
The guest's hair and face must be made to
look as much like marble as possible. Pow-
dered hair and “ pearl cream ” give the
requisite ghostly, classic effect. A Greek
or Roman head on a body dressed in swallow-
tails and black tubular things like crape drain-
pipes, or robed in an empire frock, is suggestive of
the dead and the quick mixed, the same person
being partly quick, and the rest dead. It only
requires that each person shall bring along half a
tombstone to make the half-and-half arrangement
complete. Now that society has got to the point
of thus frankly painting itself for dinner, the
aboriginal Briton meal, at which all the guests
will be stained blue, can’t be far off. Various
Venuses, who are showing opulent proportions in
divers music halls, have probably suggested the
idea of the Whitewashed Guest.
From London, Feb. 15 :
Tuesday’s ceremony of the opening of Parliament was-
a gorgeous affair. Those lucky enough to get admis-
sion to the Royal Gallery saw the show best. The Prince
and Princess of Wales entered very quietly. One caught
a glimpse of the latter—tall, very slim—gliding along in
wonderful robe of silvery tissue that reflected every
color of the rainbow. When the procession entered the
Gallery, every eye was turned on the Queen. She is
wonderfully youthful-looking, still beautiful, eyes of very
pale blue, with a tired expression ; her hair is between
light brown and auburn, complexion pale. The Royal
crimson mantle was worn over the glittering black
gown, a wealth of diamonds covered the corsage, a very
deep cafi au lait veil of priceless lace was worn draped
under the crown. A previous flutterer through the Gal-
lery was Lady Beauchamp, who was escorted by her hus-
band—stouter than of yore, but most important. Her
diamonds and emeralds were magnificent; her veil was
of silvered tulle, matching her Empire robe of silver
tissue.
We have been farewelling Eva Myloft, who sails
America-wards to-morrow, for musical engagements.
Later, Canada is her destination ; this time, for a matri-
monial engagement. The Austral Club farewelled one of
its favorite singers by a tea and presentation autograph
book, containing an address and the signatures of her
club friends. Lady Cockburn and Miss Baxter were the-
principal movers in this farewell.
Our sometime visitor, Miss Peggy Pryde, has
been prosecuting someone for stealing a silver-
foxskin rug from her motor-car. That kind of
rug and that sort of vehicle bespeak prosperity.
A Charters Towers pair, alliteratively and
respectively named Mills and Millican, were mar-
ried, with many rose-point frills, in the drawing-
room of the Australia Hotel, on Thursday. There
was a huge bunch of presents from Queensland,
and that means valuable ones. When the North
starts out to give presents, it does so lavishly.
Miss Pole-Chandos, a near connection of the
Earl of Harrington (whoever he may be), has been
in Sydney for a few days, and during the festive
week will be a guest of the Northcotes. Pole-
Chandos has a great sound. Maybe the P.Cs.
were Crusaders or Inspectors of the Royal Har-
ness-room. And maybe they pronounce them-
selves Pool-Shandy.
Florence Young and “the largest emerald in
Australia ” blazed in the manager’s box at “Robin
Hood” on Friday. The star was all in emeraldl
green, either in honor of the saint who threw
pythons out of Ireland, or to do honor to Sher-
wood Forest and Knight’s verdant attire.
The prettiest little coffee-bean of a girl blew
over from Melbourne and into the Australia to-
lunch on Saturday. She was Charlene, the new
Xylophon and Society Sketch artist for the
Tivoli. Has a 16-inch waist, but its quite in pro-
portion to the rest of her slender symmetrical
proportions. A coffee-brown Charlotte Corday
hat, same shade of a tailor-made coat and skirt,
brown hair and brown eyes. And she’ll probably
have Sydney on nice, brown toast.
Talking about widows. A Sydney personage
who recently married one has painted his house
Mack. This is a fact. And wayfarers on his
road look up at the building, mistake it for a
morgue, and sprint on their way.
A correspondent from a country centre : “ Out-
back school kiddies have their troubles. Recently,,
the needlework of a sewing class was entered for 1
competition by the teacher. To the joy of the'
school, the work of little Polly Jones took first-
prize. Garment was a grown-up’s night-
gown. The Show followed soon, and Mrs. Teacher
suggested that Polly should send her achieve-
ment to compete. Polly was delighted. But
next day a miserable and tearful little Polly
came along. ‘ What is the matter ? ’ asked 1
the teacher. ‘ls anyone at home ill, or been
bitten by a snake, or anything?’ ‘No, mum,’
answered the girl between her sobs, ‘ it’s worse’
nor that. Mother was that proud of the night-
gownd that she showed it to everybody, with its*
blue ticket and ribbons, and everyone said how
nice it was—too good to wear. And grand-
mother heard them, and said “Exackerly. It’s
just fine enough to be buried in,” and that she
would have the blue ribbon taken orf, and black
put on instead, and it’d do lovely fer a shroud.
She never even arsked fer it—she jest took it, and
mother couldn’t say nothink, because grand-
mother’s dad’s mother, and I can’t get it back
from her ’cause she’s got it folded up in silv’ry tea-
paper and put away fer the layin’-out—it and ten
pounds fer her berryin’.’ ”
Employers of female domestic ltybor in Y elling-
ton (M.L-) are being circularised by the Domestic
Workers’ Industrial Union, and Mary Ann as-
sumes a haughty Ermyntrude air as she ukases ;
Eight classes of workers shall be recognised— i.e.,
housekeepers, lady helps, generals, housemaids, nurse-
maids, kitebenmaids, laundrymaids and cooks.
The week’s work shall consist of 68 hours, to be divided
as follows: . . ..
Work to commence on every morning, except on holi-
days mentioned in clause 3, at 6.30 a.m., and cease on
Mondays, Tuesdays, Fridays and Saturdays at 7.30 p.m.,
with three intervals of half-an hour each for meals, and
one hour’s interval in the afternoon of each day.
On Thursdays work shall cease at 2 p.m., with two
intervals of half-an-hour each for meals.
On Sundays work shall cease at 2 p.m., with two inter-
vals of half-an-hour each for meals, but domestics shall,
if required, prepare tea between the hours of 5.30 p.m.
and 6.30 p.m. on alternate Sundays.
On Wednesdays work shall cease at 10 p.m., with three
intervals of half-an-hour each for meals, and one hour
interval in the afternoon.
On Sundays two hours shall be allowed to attend church
in the morning.
Holidays.—Christmas Day, Boxing Day, New Year’s
Day, King’s Birthday, Prince of Wales’ Birthday 7, Anni-
versary Day, Easter Monday, Labor Day, and all statutory
holidays shall be deemed to be holidays, and work done
on those days shall be paid for at the rate of one shilling
(Is.) per hour.
Domestics shall be in every evening at 10 p.m., except
on Thursday evenings, and on that night 12 p.m.
Accompanying the “Claims of the Domestic
Workers’ Union ” is an agreement for the Missus
to sign. “By doing so she will obviate the un-
pleasantness of having to appear personally, or by
agent, before the Conciliation Board or Arbitra-
tion Court.” There’s a really “ fresh ” time ahead
of Mary Merely Maori Ann and her old bag o’
chips. The sixty-eight hours a week claim seems
reasonable enough, but it’s surely impossible to
dictate, in an arbitrary fashion, how those hours
are to be divided in every household. Any fixed
rule would be unworkable without annoyance and
inconvenience to each. Servants with kind, con-
siderate employers will probably not join the
union. But if it does get all it wants, M.L.
Missuses will be forced into the simple life, even
to dwelling in tents, like Jacob.
“ This ghastly thin-faced time ” of Lent, as
some poet has it (only he didn’t mean any time
Lenten), turned quite rosy and fat on Saturday
afternoon when the Waldron-King and Keegan-
King weddings were celebrated. What novelty
there was in the dressing was on the part of the
bridesmaids. They wore half-wreaths of mauve
jessamine instead of the customary hats. The
wreaths were flat on the head, finished with
rosettes of pastel blue and mauve ribbon,
from which flowed long spindrifty veils. That
was for one of the brides’ following. The other
was attended by pink bridesmaids. Mrs. Waldron,
who is the queenlier of the King sisters, was a
picturesque departer-for-the-honeymoon in an
amethyst cripe-de-chine gown and a “Louise”
hat of shaded amethyst massed with purple
grapes and knobs of cerise velvet. There was a
host of guests, and among them the dressing of
Mrs. J. C. Williamson and Miss Ola Jane
Humphrey took the comet. First wore a
sheath-like and long-tailed gown of ivory
lace (Irish point) over a peach silk, and had
a wine-colored head-gear. Brilliant Ola Jane,
who is responsible as far as introductions go for
the double wedding, was in a crazily intricate
frock of sky-blue silk and white lace, and on one
ear and one-half of her nutty coiffure she posed a
blue velvet sailor hat with a blue ostrich
in it. Pink coral and big pear pearls (a lot of
them) encircled her neck. She was presented
with them the night before, but declined to state
the name of the giver. So there’s a lot of guess-
ing going on. Sydney has not much else to do this
week but guess things.
A letter from Christchurch (M.L.): —
Our Exhibition is fast drawing to a close. On the 14th
the one-and-a-half-inillionth person passed through the
turnstile. Again it was a girl. She, like the millionth
■girl, gets a watch for her trouble in being there at the
right time. The Ex. is now adorned with a variety of
side-shows. Last week it was a baby display, with over
350 more or less hot and squalling infants,' and about
25,000 spectators. This u 7 eek the management has adver-
tised a “Beauty Show ” —best lady’s face, neck, shoulders
and arms, ankle and so forth. Ladies can wear masks if
wished, and doubtless many will wish. Of course there
is the usual newspaper bark by the W.C.T.U. and other
skinny-necked females about the propriety of the Beauty
Exhibition. The W.C.T.U. has a firm conviction that no
woman should be seen except the woman who isn’t worth
seeing, and it has doubts as to whether she should be
visible.
Mrs. Lachlan Beaton and some of her house-
hold have gone to Bowral for a month or so.
Small boy Beaton has a cough in the key of B
minor, and the change from sea to hill air is
compulsory in his case.
Sydney is losing for a while the man who is a
director of more companies perhaps than anyone
else hereabouts. F. T. Humphery, with his wife
and daughter, is leaving on a trip to China and
Japan by Wednesday’s liner. His place on the
Australia Hotel directorate will be taken by Mr.
Smith, of Norton and Smith.
My Brisbane correspondent writes;—
Senator Chataway, his better half (I speak advisedly)
and family passed through this week en route for the
South. Mrs. Chataway belonged to every organisation
in Mackay, and received a few pretty jewels at the hands
of her fellow organisers ere she drifted down the muddy
Pioneer.
The Mayoress of Brisbane held her first at home last
Thursday. As she lives somewhere in the wilds of
Mayne Junction, and no cabs were provided to convey the
multitude to and from “Hillside,” the said multitude
mostly stayed away. Those who did struggle out were
well fed, and their thirst was carefully attended to.
The second day of the Patrick’s meet at Ascot, on
Monday, was chiefly notable for the greenness of the
millinery, the lawn, and the punters, who mostly
returned to town with footmarks on their hats (they
had jumped on » them). In the frocking line,
Mrs. Cecil Palmer, Mrs. H. Cohen, the smart
little proprietor of a south-side private hospital, a
draper’s wife from Rockhampton, and a pretty-girl who
assists a Queen-street dentist to haul the public tooth,
were the exceptions. A lot of regular goers are yet at
the seaside, and the rest Were dressed any old way.*

Maoriland’s discarded legislator, Wood
Green, who put up for Collingwood at the recent
Victorian elections, went to the trouble of getting
his license-to-marry cancelled—parsons being in-
capable of sitting in the Bent-country Parliament;
and then was badly defeated by Wilkins. Now
he hasn’t even the scarce and casual marriage-fee
to depend on. It’s a thankless world !
One more N.S.W. concern, the Illawarra
Steamship Co., is beginning to have its doubts
about the beauties of the Foreign-trade gospel of
cheapness. To save a few pounds, it accepted a
foreign tender as against an Australian offer for
building the Moruya. The boat was handed over
in Great Britain, and the captain set out supplied
with a general chart of the world—very service-
able for navigation round the Isle of Dogs, but
published too far away, apparently, to keep track
of changes of lights on the Australian coast. So
the Moruya piled itself up on a Victorian beach,
and now it will cost, one way and another, over
£3OOO before it is patched up and as good as new,
and the co.’s favorite captain, who had the job of
bringing it out, is in hospital, worse damaged
than even the boat. And all because the co.
wouldn’t pay the extra few pounds to have the
ship built in the country that supplied the money.
The fact that rats are swarming just at
present at Circular Quay, Sydney, and that several
plague-stricken rats have been found in that
locality, woke the papers up to say several nasty
things about the Sydney Harbor Trust. And
when two plague-infected animals were found in
the Trust’s own office, the Trust deemed the
situation grave enough for an official explanation.
The explanation explained everything but the
presence of those two rats.
A man was recently brought before the
Sydney Arbitration Court for a breach of the
tailors’ award, and was fined ss. for accepting
work for a less wage than that fixed by the Court.
J. P. Wright, who is the employers’ representa-
tive on the Arbitration Court, arose in indignation
at what he called the scandalous behavior of the
Cutters’ Union in instituting the prosecution,
and dramatically forked out 55., and paid the
fine on the spot. Wright did not ask how the
Cutters’ Union could manage to keep up its end
against the sweating employer if its members
were allowed to cut the wages-rate with impunity;
and, in the case of the man under notice, it was
proved that he is now being paid the union rate of
wages, so that his lapse was not due to i ncompetence
on his part. The Bulletin has often heard, and
it dares Mr. J. P. Wright, as a large employer,
to say he has not also heard, of a union of em-
ployers, who have mutually agreed upon the sell-
ing price of an article. And, should any member
of the union be detected under-selling the rest, he
wouldn’t get off with a fine of 55., nor would an
arbitrator be found to pay his fine with a melo-
dramatic speech.

new books arrive per every English and American mail.
Many are worthy of a place on the shelves of any well-
selected library, the majority are written and designed
to while away a few hours of leisure. Every book by any
author of note is available to subscribers of the above
library. All copies are clean and fresh, and any book, no
matter how expensive or how keen the demand, may be
had for the asking. The country subscription, 8 volumes
at a time, ranges from £1 11s. 6d. for 6 months, and
£2 12s. 6d. for 12 months; the city and suburban rates
from £1 Is. and £2 2s. per annum, shorter periods or
larger parcels at proportionate rates. The most popular
books at the moment are : R. W. Chamber’s “ Fighting
Chance,” which reveals life in New York as seen in the
Thaw trial; Spearman’s “Whispering Smith,” and Parish’s
“Bob Hampton Placer.” Do not leave town without
joining Dymock’s Circulating Library, 428 George Street,
Sydney.*

MELBOURNE CHATTER.
Melbourne, March 25, 1907.
My Dear Yarrie,—
Melbourne people of all sorts are rather glad to
have the Talbots out of the way in order to have
the Maddens on the vice-regal job again. John
Madden is probably as satisfactory a vice-royalty
as the royal ghost need be, and his family, fixings
and surroundings generally fill the gubernatorial
bill according to the popular estimation of the
manner in which the document should be filled.
John’s ruddy, shining, wide-smiling delight in the
position.is a source of vast satisfaction to the
mass, since it implies that he is grateful
for the honors and glories conferred and the privi-
lege of shaking things up in a yellow-wheeled
equipage, with a rattling warrior cantering ahead
to prepare the way and excite interest. Imported
Governors, on the other hand, do not smile far
and wide, and look rubicund and glad ; they look
rather bored as a rule, and go to functions like
victims to the sacrifice. This makes the onlookers
tired. The happiness with which the Maddens,
dad and family, attend a theatre vice-regally, or
open a suburban hall or a sardine tin as represen-
tatives of the King, is a refreshing contrast. Cer-
tainly Victorians would rather have John Madden
a vice-regal fixture than any importation for years
past, but they share Australia’s lamentable habit
of preferring the thing they don’t want if it’s
“sent out.”
The “Old Folks” had their annual Tea on
Wednesday. There is no doubt about the joy of
that annual Tea, when over 1000 ancients collect
in the Exhibition Building, and fill themselves
with cheap refreshment. Big Parson Edgar was
originator and moving spirit. He gradually per-
suaded many of the charitably-inclined Smart
Ones to personally pour out the cup of steaming
drink that furnishes the festive guests with dys-
pepsia, though some of them still stand on their
unchristian dignity and send an odd, spare house-
maid to represent them, and shoot the contents of
the pot into the cup. In these cases, it is said,
the festival has not the same pleasant aspect to
the Toothless Ones as when Lady Sorneone-or-
other personally hands them their hot brew with
jtwo lumps of sugar and a smile. All over
60 are invited, the alleged ages soaring as
high as 100 odd. Incidental to the Tea,
a census is taken, and the eldest consumers
are made to feel proud that they’re still able to
sink a cup with the strongest of them. Some
Samaritans send eider-down quilts and such
luxuries, which go as prizes to the very venerable
and tend to foster the art of longevity. An early
concert —with sweet ancient' ballads and a
cinematograph—usually closes the wild revel, and
then the revellers go wheezing on their way, in the
blessed hope of another corroboree next year.
Heigho ! From the Sunday School tea-fight of
jam-bespattered childhood to the gathering, of
Old Folks is along, long journey, and theredoesn’t
seem to be much money at the end of it.
Packed outgoing boats have been the order of
the day lately, but the Mooltan took the
loaf for crowded farewells. Overhead the
occasion didn’t carry an auspicious appear-
ance, and, as a matter of fact, the trip
to Adelaide realised the worst expectations.
Reports to hand state that the voice of a thousand
Little Marys was heard wailing above the tempest.
The Farewellers were much more cheerful than
the Farewelled —who showed as a mass of some-
what white worried faces in anticipation of the
trouble to come. With such a background the
Talbottle dame was conspicuously cheerful. She
chatted, and laughed, and shook hands enthusias-
tically.
Florists had the day of their lives, and were
nearly swamped with orders. Why, 0 why,
do people bear bulky baskets of blossoms to
boats, and make the recipients cross by reason
of a deadweight of vegetables? I saw one
lot dumped down unceremoniously aft, with
a word that could only be written out and handed
up to the magistrate, and an armful of posies fol-
lowed with much more force than sentiment.
And that was before the boat moved ! Later on
they were probably dashed overboard with much
dash.
The Mooltan took so many people away that
there wasn’t a Governor, or Premier, or anything
of size, left to open the Dahlia Show, so it just
had to blossom out on its own merits. As far as
dahlias were concerned the merits were not con-
spicuous, but the weather was responsible for
that. Begonias (which grow under glass) took
this weather-marred display into their warm
embrace and made it a success. Color in these
blooms runs up and down almost impossible scales.
Another corner that was not dahlia was Aus-
tralian shrubs—glorious, blazing, shouting color —
native shrubs from all the States, but grown at
Melbourne Botanical Gardens. An orange-
flowered pine, a flaming robin redbreast, a
Westralian gum with blossoms of hunter’s pink,
a white and gold, lemon-scented shrub —these
were only a few items of the dazzle. Don’t folk
want more of these in their grounds? If they
think they don’t, let them make a special pilgrim-
age to the Botanical Gardens and be converted.
Smart people really ought to take advantage of
those beautifully-kept Gardens for other purposes
than meeting a friend by appointment in a shady
spot.
For some months before he left, Governor Tal-
bottle whizzed round the uttermost parts, of the
State seeing things, and shaking hands with the
oldest inhabitant. He went down mines and up
mountains. He motored and horsebacked and
Taited (which means that he went on slow,
perilous journeys over Victoria’s railway lines).
In six months he did as much travelling as most
Governors do in six years. His Ex. was deeply
impressed with the greatness of Victoria, while
Victoria was everywhere favorably impressed
with the length of his Ex. The departed Gover-
nor promises to say kind things about Australia
when he gets to England.
Surgeon Fitzgerald is off for a trip to Europe.
Miss Burrows, so long the popular and capable
matron of the old doctor’s private hospital, wings
her flight to America. A long course of petted,
pursy invalids is twice as wearing as the same
period of attendance upon less well-to-do sufferers.
Sick nurses all say they would rather nurse a case
of consumption or cancer any day than the fine
lady, whose imaginary complaint . goes . with
swelled head, fractured temper, and intermittent
civility. One of our most decorative society
leaders engaged a nurse to tend her dying infant.
The nurse was well-born, well-educated, pretty,
and devoted by night and day to her tiny charge.
Nevertheless, baby’s mamma reproved her child’s
best friend for not standing up whenever Madame
swept into the nursery.
By the way, private hospital never sheltered a
less exacting patient than the then Countess of
Hopetoun what time her nose was broken in the
hunting-field. Whenever Hopey visited his dis-
abled mate, he would draw up a chair and say to
the matron, “Now Miss Z, sit here and make us
laugh.” And the flattered lady, as she cracked her
best jokes in response, thought of other patients
“Mrs. This” and “Miss That”—local Some-
bodies given to rewarding her conversational
attempts at cheering them up with such apprecia-
tive remarks as “ Hoe funny, but would you maind
being quaiet for a taime ? ”
“Tryphena,” from the rolling wave
My sex isn’t a fast color at sea, and it can’t find a com-
plexion to stick on. As soon as the liner gets agitated,
all poetic feeling for a turquoise sky and a dimpling sea
passes right out of mind. In its place a jaundiced
melancholy takes root, and the countenance assumes
either a drab or a Stilton-green or a pale lilac hue against
which no part is taken. The owner blinks miserably
from a lounge where all but the jaundiced com-
plexion is buried in a rug. All day long and through
the sapphire hours of the evening, the sea-sick
one is prostrated on deck, and the sex looks like a con-
signment of gloomy incurables taking the prescribed
airing on a hospital verandah. It does nothing, knows
nothing, thinks nothing. Nothing matters. Apathy
holds the sea victim in a vice. Its complexion went first
—and everything else it held dear has followed.
For the good-sailor sex (when it does occur) there is a
joy in deck life that floods the veins with the keenest
current of vitality. Eyes dance across the throbbing
blue sea and catch some of its splendid color. The
spirits keep the tongue at an incessant, blithe wag, and
the wit cut and ready. The sense of physical well-being
—the savage instinct of life and laughter—drive the
possessor into vigorous ship capers, mischief, larks that
start at breakfast and last till bunk-oh ! For the rare
flutterer who can polka when the liner inclines to an
angle of seventeen degrees, and can giggle when sea and
shin settle to an active game of pitch-and-toss, board-ship
delights are greater than any land joys. And not the
least of them is the pitying contemplation of her prostrate
sisters. Woman’s noble capacity for pity gets good exer-
cise on ship-board if she is a good sailor.
Elsie Guenett, the pretty little daughter of one-
time critic Guenett, was on the Suevio, going
Londonward to be married. Now that she is
used to the fact that she wasn’t born to be drowned,
the bride-elect is probably weeping salt tears at
the loss of her trousseau—a wonderful collection of
frills stored in the hold where “the tide flows
through,” as the papers calmly report. It must
be terrible to have the sad sea waves washing away
your trousseau and the crabs crawling over your
lingerie.
The University in general junketed on Thurs-
day over a Queen’s College wedding, the bride-
groom being Harry Sugden, brother of the
musical Master of Queen’s. Lawyer Harry was
so impressed with the success of his brothers
matrimonial state that he signed the deed of
partnership with Mrs. Sugden’s sister, and thus
prevented a nice girl going out of the family, so
to speak.
There was a swagger house party last week at
“ Corooroke.” A polo match was played at Colac,
and the Everard Brownes entertained the Sydney
Polo visitors for a week’s shooting. Two of the
men —Mr. Garvan and Mr. Stephens—motored
all the way from Sydney to the scene. Miss
Chandos-Pole and Miss Maggie Turner helped
along the girl part.
Things were done to a turn—not to say Browne.
A florist’s girl from Melbourne was forcibly held
the whole week to look after the floral decorations
of breakfasts, lunches and dinners. She had
“ carty blanchy ” (as “Hopkinson” Hugh Ward
says) in sending to town for flowers (and this in
the country !).
Mrs. Durant, of the big green motor car, gave
a theatre party and supper _to farewell Miss
Florence Young last Monday night. There was
not much time between the last bow on the stage
and the dreary departing midnight train, but
considerable warmth of feeling was concentrated
into the short interval, and Florence supped with
enthusiasm. Whatever the occasion, she is un-
failingly “ all there.” The whole party indulged
in things floral at the theatre. One huge design
held a champagne bottle in its expansive clasp.
Oily Moore—the small sister of Carrie Moore—
is in Melbourne, after an exhaustive tour of the
States. She and sister Eva have been with Pol-
lard’s Lilliputian company (made up altogether of
children), playing “The Belle of New York,”
“The Mikado'” “The Geisha,” and such things.
Oily is a dark, slim, graceful child, very like
Carrie, with her small, dark, expressive face and
great,.luminous, brown ejes, and lank, black
hair. She is a born mimic, and brims over with
impressions gathered in her wanderings. Just
now she has demurely settled down with an aunt
in St. Kilda, and goes regularly to school. The
manager of the company undertook to see to the
children’s bookation, “ but you may be sure we
didn’t do much,” says cute little Oily. “It was
like our day-time sleep; we were supposed to—
that’s all.” Sister Eva, with the advantage of
some extra years, has put her hair up more or less,
and departed with a grown-up Pollard troupe.
Amy Sherwin, back again in Melbourne, was
warmly welcomed by some of her old admirers.
A once-glorious voice carries on its echoes for long
after the fullest note is sounded. But, alas ! the
admirers didn’t roll up in battalions to the two
concerts last week, and if they wish to indulge
their “AuldLang Syne” sentiment they must
get to the Town Hall on Easter Monday night,
when Madame will sing again after a Holy Week’s
rest.
Nellie Stewart is in Melbourne, looking well
in spite of her illness, and full of enthusiastic
predictions of success for the Musgrove Opera
Co. The arm depending from the shoulder
where the trouble was is not exactly an invalid,
but it has to be carefully reckoned with. And the
reckoning must necessarily be rapid. Nellie de-
clares that she has only six weeks to “make
good ” before she returns to her importunate
American managers.
There was an aroma of motor-car incidental to
the marriage, last Thursday, of Miss Steele —of
the beauty family that includes handsome Mrs.
Wallace and Mrs. Vivian Parker. A while after
the heroine of the frilly ceremony had promised
the parson to be for better or for worse to her
Mr. Watt, the motor tooted. It was an impress-
ive and gorgeously-dressed ceremony, and the
family reputation for good looks was well-sus-
tained. As they choo-cliooed off, and the confetti
started raining, the bridegroom showed his fore-
thought by producing a pocket brush and whisk-
ing his coat and his lady’s dress. His backward
glance was triumphant, but the guests smiled
widely as they watched a big row of old boots
dangling jocosely from the back of the car. Truly
a delicate method of “ giving the show away.”
Reverend Evelyn, of the Snodgrass clan, leaves
for England by the Omrah shortly. The family
sometimes speaks of the “attached” Snodgrass
name, contending that Douglas is the real brand,
and that the other was frivolously added some
mere hundred years ago. At that rate, it must
have been a comparatively recent addition—less
than half a century old—when “Janet Lady’s”
highly genteel, but not very moneyed, papa
brought it with him to Australia, and was
vaguely suspected of being the original Mr.
Snodgrass, from “Pickwick Papers.” Howbeit,
the cheerful reverend intends to revert to the
“Douglas,” and drop the Snodgrass before his
return. How or where he does not say—perhaps
overboard. It is the sort of name which should
sink rapidly to the bottom of the ocean —Snod-
grass !
“ Rosna ” writes from Adelaide :
Adelaide’s pet little exhibition of women’s work is to be
put through its paces in August, as a preliminary to fall-
ing on the neck of its big Melbourne brother ; and all the
grand dames of the State are fully determined it shall be
the best little pet ex. that was ever beheld. It will be open
over a week, including two Saturdays, and every after-
noon the Oval will be given over to sportive competitions
of every shape, size and variety, wherein horses, auto-
mobiles and other untamed beasts will cavort. The pro-
gramme of competitions in the local affair is to be identi-
cal with that at Melbourne, but far better arranged, of
course. Also, there will be separate prizes.
A horde of Adelaide folk joined the yearly Big Game
hunt in Britain and the neighboring uncivilised countries
per the Mooltan last week, and the übiquitous tearful
farewell and “token of esteem” loomed largely on the
Lenten horizon. As, for some mysterious reason, the
token invariably takes the form of a travelling bag, one
would imagine the trade in that platitudinous article must
be immense. A few of the esteemed people who got (or
deserved) the bag were Mr. and Mrs. Fred Young, the
T. O’Halloran Giles and all their chicks, Mr. and Mrs.
Leonard Waterhouse, and the Taylors (of Wood, Son and
Co.). The Blue Anchor Wakool captured yet more of the
landed gentry, and carted them off to see Edward and
Alexandra and their Island—among them Surveyor-
General Strawbridge and his wife, son and daughters,
Mr. Pelly, and theC. A. Moody family.
Rumor is rending the hearts of many a sentimental
maid in this Holy City by engaging its sweetest-expres-
sioned and longest-haired piano-smiter to some fair
vandal in London. The musical one is at present making
holiday in England, and the rumor was started by an
Adelaide maiden in the same neighborhood, who knew a
girl whose uncle by a previous marriage saw the musician
conversing with another girl in the Strand.
It is becoming fashionable to deposit any stray son
you may happen to have about in the beautiful British
navy that rules the wave. There is something so beauti-
fully aristocratic and brass-buttoned about the navy.
And what fond mamma wouldn’t part with her boy for the
joy of having him come back Lieutenant Smyth of the
H.S.M; Hippopotamus. Naval Commandant Creswell,
afore-time a denizen of this State, packed off his young
son Colin by the Mooltan the other day. He goes to
Osborne College to become a naval cadet and learn to
tread the bounding quarter-deck.
The little Doubleday maiden, her fiddle, and her
mamma are settled in Vienna, and the maestros are said
to be strewing the atmosphere with Ach Himmels and
other picturesque expressions of wonder at her playing.
It has been decided that the kiddy shall make the fiddle
her first study, but the piano is not to be given up alto-
gether. The violin teacher par excellence in Vienna, one
rejoicing in the comparatively sane name of Rossi, has
accepted Leila as a pupil, and says she will be a great
artist. Also, a fierce authority on the piano, after hear-
ing her hit the teeth and put her foot on the tail of the
large instrument, wrote to the wonderkin’s mother to
express the picturesque conviction that “ she is famous.”
Yours affectionately, JOHANNA.
Evidently a generation is arising that
knows not Charles Dickens. At Adelaide re-
cently a boy was charged with loitering, and the
solicitor told the Bench that the case reminded
him of that of Poor Jo, in “Bleak House,” who
was kept on the incessant move by Inspector
Bucket. One pressman administered a severe
rebuke : First, he wrote, the Adelaide inspector’s
name was Burchett, not Bucket; and the boy’s
name was not Joe, and if the boy was in Bleak
House the inspector could not harry him. The
law only prevented loitering in the street. Un-
fortunately, the sub-editor had read Dickens, and
the howling jest never appeared.
Arrived from America the other day, after an absence
of three years, during which they visited Manila, China,
Jw the United States, and Canada, Pollard’s Lilli-
putian Opera Company, composed entirely of Australian
juveniles. After reorganising in Melbourne, the Lilli-
putians will make a tour of the Commonwealth.

POLITICAL
POINTS
Brisbane Courier has at last definitely
thrown in its lot with Philp and Co., much to the
relief of Premier Kidston, who has hitherto been
considerably handicapped by that paper’s
attempts at patronage. No progressive leader
can afford to carry a paper with the Courier’s
black-labor traditions through an election cam-
paign
There seems to be a rush for the State
seat of Gough (N.S.W.), now occupied by
“ Reformer ” Thomas. The candidate who looks
best is W. E. Vincent, who received the nomina-
tion of the Australian party to contest New Eng-
land, and who stood down to allow Laborite
Foster to oust Lonsdale, that played-out idolator
at the shrine of Anti-Sosh.
The good words of Victorian Premier
Bent, when leaving Melbourne for England :
I find wherever I go that people speak of Canada, and
I intend to use the word “Australia.” In Canada they
have 10 States. We have not so many here, hut unfor-
tunately we use the terms Westralia, South Australia,
Victoria, Queensland, New South Wales, and Tasmania.
I think the time has arrived now when each and every
one of us should speak of Australia.
The cabled news that the new Transvaal
Ministerial salaries have been fixed at fairly'
generous figures has shocked Sydney Herald
The allowance attaching to the office of Prime Minister
in this Commonwealth is £2lOO, while other Ministers
receive £1650 ; yet it is not improbable that Australia fills
quite as large a space in the Imperial scheme of things
as the Transvaal.
But because the Australian Commonwealth has
been mean to its servants, why should the new
S. African State follow the bad example?
Apropos the importation of Italians to
work in the Queensland canefields, on the ground
that Australians can’t stand the climate. Aus-
tralian navvies worked on the Nyngan-Bourke
railway long after the Italians employed thereon
had downed tools, beaten by the heat. The
C.S.R. Co.’s lot may be made of tougher maca-
roni, but there seems a strong eventual pro-
bability of a violent and totally uncalled-for
swamping of the ice-cream and banana-barrow
professions.
A sample of the methods by which Wes-
tralia has drifted into financial chaos has turned
up in connection with the State farm, used as an
agricultural college. Nobody seems to know
what it costs; the Minister can’t find out.
All he can ascertain is that the place is chock-full
of appliances that are no good, that the stock are
mostly weeds, and that the students are getting
hardly any training that they wouldn’t be better
without. Of course reform is promised. But
Westralian Government methods want reforming
with an axe and a broom, and at the most the
present Government is armed with a clothes-
brush to wipe off the specks.
THE ARMY OF LIBERTY.
The N.S.W. State Government is a ruth-
less vandal. It first destroyed Wentworth Falls
—one of the finest falls in the world —by robbing
it of most of its water to fill a railway dam ; and
now it intends to make a drainage ditch of the
remainder of the stream, in order to get in a few
pounds of land sales revenue. Joseph Carruthers
can see nothing in the Blue Mountains but a
handy retreat to meet his pals. His troubles
about waterfalls.
J. P. T. Caulfield, the Boy Politician
(who may be called the Boy Polly-Caulfield for
short), supplied one of the happy episodes of the
recent Vic. election :
In the course of a speech at the Queen’s Hall, Caulfield,
last night, Mr. J. P. T. Caulfield, said that when he de-
cided to contest the seat he went away for a month’s re-
cuperation in the 'Blue Mountains, because he knew that
the fight against the capitalistic and monopolistic forces
which had represented the electorate so long would be
trying for his nervous system. He then heard of the de-
cision of Mr. Bent to have the elections early. Being
friendly with the Premier, he despatched a telegram to him
stating, “ Kindly delay the elections until my nervous
system is recuperated.” Mr. Bent did not see his way to
make the change.”
Bent, being a great hulking character who has no
more use for nerves than a bear has for a fan and
a bottle of scent, was naturally unsympathetic.

“ Caber ” writes :
The unevenness of Queensland politics, with the rapid
fluctuations of opinions, conjure up reminiscences of
Warwick in the sixties. It used then to change its
opinions and its member every election. The fight was
mostly between Democrat Morgan and Squatter Clark,
and they came and went by turns. Other electorates ran
things on much the same principle of now-you-see-it-and-
now-you-don't. The great exception was Drayton and
Toowoomba, and that brings up another reminiscence.
When “Johnny” Watts, of Eton Vale, resigned the Dray-
ton and Toowoomba seat in 1862, it was regarded as a
certainty for J. C. White, of Jondaryan (he went down in
the wreck of that Maoriland boat, the name of which I
can’t spell), but William Henry Groom came forward.
White’s crowd sported rosettes of blue and White, and to
keep the Groom men from indulging in decorative dis-
plays, J.C. bought all the ribbon in the county of Aubigny.
But William Henry rose to the occasion. He got an acre
of Turkey twill, and had rosettes built therefrom. The
red won by 21. votes, and after that it was carried to
victory for 37 years. When the electorate got two mem-
bers, the dark blue was added, and in ’99 the conjoined
colors won their last fight. Groom went over the Great
Range in 1901, when Federal member for Darling Downs;
and “ Johnny ” Fogarty followed his colleague two years
ago, while still representing the old constituency.
The lesson of the Butter Commission is
already lost on the fool farmer of Vic-
toria. The other day George Stewart,
chairman of Colac Dairying Co., told
his suppliers that in listening to the
wiles of the army of travellers can-
vassing for the Melbourne butter
manufacturers and agents, they
were steadily ruining them-
selves. Their co-operative fac-
tory returned them every
penny earned, and though the
agents might offer a trifle
more to-day, they would
more than get it back to-
morrow, when they had
killed the co-operative
companies; moreover,
the wages of all those
travellers would ulti-
mately come out of the
farmers’ pockets. But
the farmer can’t or
won’t see it. He wants
the egg to-day, so he kills the goose.
A recurring feature of the Vic. State
elections is the contest between journalist Thomas
Hunt, of Kilmore, and the regrettable Malcolm
Kenneth McKenzie, farmer and land-acquirer,
for the electorate that has gone under the names
of Kilmore and Dalhousie, Anglesey and Upper
Goulburn. Hunt topped the poll away back in
’B6, and again in ’B9. In ’92 McKenzie ousted
him. Next election he beat him again, but by
only 169 votes in a 2600 poll. In ’97 Hunt lay
low and said nothing, and again in ’OO, when
McK. beat Fenton, and in ’O2 when he was un-
opposed. Then came the exposure of McKenzie’s
land dealings, and he was forced to resign. But
in ’O4 he bobbed up once more— Hunt having won
the bye-election—and got within 32 votes of his
old opponent—B62 to 894. The other day he tried
his luck and the public memory once more. The
public memory was rather too good for him, so he
polled only 1184 to Hunt’s 1201.
Of metropolitan electors 58‘8 per cent,
voted in the recent Victorian election, as against
61'75 per cent, in the country. The worst
examples were the market-gardening and
fruit-growing district of Dandenong and the
haughty constituency represented by Speaker
Madden, in both of which but 39 per cent,
troubled to mark their papers. The best percent-
age was put up by Dalhousie, where the perennial
Duffy and farmer Argyle battled to a finish that
left the former but 26 votes behind in a poll of
2604 out of 3336 electors—7B per cent. The
Rodney farmers took such an interest in the con-
test that three out of every four turned up to help
decide the merits of Hugh M'Kenzie and Sammy
Lancaster. As might be expected, five Tory
electorates —St. Kilda, Toorak, Melbourne, East
Melbourne, and Boroondara—show up worst in
the percentage of city folk who did their duty,
and. five Labor constituencies stand best.
THE OFFERTORY.
SOMNOLENT DEALER : “ Discount for cash, please.”
Victoria’s new Assembly is fairly repre-
sentative, though some callings have an extra
large finger in the pie. There are nine farmers,
six auctioneers, five lawyers and as many store-
keepers, four agents of sorts, four graziers, three
“ gentlemen,” two journalists, a couple of boot-
makers. and one each stone-cutter, tailor’s ditto,
laborer, artist, butcher, photographer, hay and
corn dealer, turner, engineer, rate collector, ware-
houseman, traveller, engine-driver, cordial manu-
facturer, printer, cutter, legal manager, vigneron,
surveyor, blacksmith, watchmaker, valuer (Bent
himself), accountant, managing director and
publican. Also, one member states his occu-
pation as “landowner,” which, like that of
“ bishop,” seems a nice, clean, easy profession.
Fathers casting about for professions for their sons
are hereby recommended to apprentice them to the
trade of landowner. Jack Murray used once to
be the only “gentleman” in Victoria’s Assembly,
but he has now got Bent’s discarded Minister,
Langdon, and Hubert Patrick Keogh, Allan
McLean’s successor for North Gippsland, as mates.
“Crimson Wing” on the Queensland
political situation :
I notice that The Bulletin has published notes from
some pessimistic democrat as to the outcome of the next
general elections. On the highways and byways of this
State I have met and talked with men and things, and
the men are Kidstonite every time, and many of the
women too, and the opinion of the things doesn’t
matter. Queensland is not the fool country some of your
scribes think it is. Good government during good seasons
has given Queenslanders time to look at affairs, and, if my
chance of living were as small as Philp’s chance of getting
a majority, I’d order my wooden frock-coat to-morrow.
In spite of the utterances of office-hungry Mat Reid and
chief puppet-worker Hinchcliffe, the working men are
thinking for themselves, and the more they think the
more determined they are to lend a helping hand
to Wullie Kidston. I’m a worker myself, and I
know. Mat Reid tried to widen the “little rift ” the
other day at the O.P. Convention, and spoke about “ the
parting of the ways.” About the best “ parting of ways ”
for Queensland democrats is to put Reid and Hinchcliffe
on board a special train (not prepaid) and detain the mail
for oblivion till the special arrives to connect.
It is generally understood that all the old-
world lines of steamers running
to Australia do so at a great
annual loss, burning coal out of
sheer good nature ; and that
the opposition to the pro-
posed new mail line is
entirely a matter of
dispassionate kindness.
Particularly is the case
of the Messageries Mari-
times a hard one, the ter-
rible financial disasters
it puts up with in the
cause of humanity being
painful to think of. Now
we learn that a powerful
combination named the
Lloyd Francais has undertaken to run still
another regularly losing line from Marseilles to
Australian ports. This philanthropy grows
monotonous.
The irony of it. While Premier Moore,
of Westralia, is negotiating for the importation
into that State of 30 Italians, on the very com-
fortable basis of paying their fares, giving them
land, and lending them something to start with,
comes the public statement in a baby-farming in-
quest that 33 young Australians (out of 37 born)
died —primarily of starvation. If Westralia
possessed State foundling hospitals where no
questions were asked when the white baby
arrived, it might have saved, in this one case,
which is probably only one case among many,
three more immigrants than all the heap Of pro-
spective Dagos about whom Premier Moore
makes so much fuss and feathers. And these
would be people of our own race, with Aus-
tralian sympathies, and they wouldn’t ask
for the present of a farm apiece. Of course it
may be that many of them were illegitimate, and
Snuffle rises in its top hat, and flourishing its
midnight gamp, to say that any State effort to
save them would be “encouraging immorality.”
Well, even if Snuffle waved two gamps instead of
one, how is it going to guarantee that quite a
proportion of its assisted immigrants are not
illegitimate also ? And what would it matter if
it did ?
This from “ Squidge ”
Nothing about the Victorian Labor party is more dig
appointing than its headquarters—the Melbourne Trades
Hall, It is a mouldy cavern of neglect and dirt. The
building itself is a fine one that anyone ought to be
proud of; but apparently it has never occurred to anyone
since the day of its opening to brush off the accumulated
dust, to dean the columns and pilasters (if that is what
they are called) of the walls, to polish up or paint the
woodwork, or to do anything that careful men, and men
proud of their building, would be ashamed to leave
undone. Great hideous spikes driven into the facade
once supported ugly advertisement hoardings with
glaring statements about dead and gone Eighi Hours
fetes, and from them there now hang dusty and long, cot.
web covered ribbons of kinked wire. The hoarding!
were torn down or blew down, and it was nobody's
business to remove the spikes and wires. Such an
exterior prepares a visitor for the inside. Muddy
boots and a long-continued lack of brooms anil
scrubbing-brushes have combined to make the search
for any particular room in the big place a lonj
horror. The reading-room is haunted by old paperi,
dog’s-eared, torn, and brown with venerableness. There
is no sign of intelligent attempt to make the place
respectable. A decent horse would object to be stabled
in parts of it. Somebody is needed who will s' and up in
their council and tell the members that men who are
content to sit on dirty, greasy seats in a half-cleaned room,
approached by rank-smelling corridors and musty, be-
grimed staircases, are not the sort who will ever do much
good in politics or in anything else. Until they can
properly govern and conduct their own place (and ol
such government order and cleanliness are the visible
signs), they cannot expect to make a success of governing
the country.
One noble feature of the recent Victorian
elections is that the insectarian crowd, the
Bible-in-Schools push, the faddiscs and goody-
goodies generally sustain a heavy set-hack.
Judkins, about the best-advertised man in the
State, except perhaps Bent, after choosing the
softest —because yellowest—district open, is sent
about his business—22Bo votes to 1526. The rest
of the Protestant Electors’ nominees—Carter,
Wood Green, O’Neill, Barrett, Wishart, Robert-
son, Ramsay—all failed. One or two very mild
Insectarians crawled back—J. W. Mason and
Gurr of Geelong— but generally the State spoke
out as straightly as the circumstances allowed.
An old military man writes
A recent Bulletin par. talks of the way people are
allowed to sketch and photograph the “ forts,” or alleged
forts, about Botany Bay. What else do the authorities
want ? On the side of a road leading from Bondi towards
South Head is a wooden cottage close to a wide gate.
There is also a conspicuous notice board with “ Bondi
Fort” on it. Why the curiosity of the passing stranger
should be excited in that way is nob clear. There is
nothing that seems at all like a fort-. The wooden cottage
is quite harmless-looking. There is a very ordinary wire-
fence, and inside it a hill of rock and sand and scrub. In
other parts of the world forts generally show a retiring
disposition. They never hang out notice-boards with
their names, and all that the casual stranger can see
when he comes near them is a series of gentle grassy
slopes, with their tops rising one above the other.
At the top of the first slope he finds himself stopped by
a sudden drop of the ground and a stout palisade.
Beyond these are a ditch, 30ft. or 40ft. deep, with strong
retaining walls on each side, and a much higher and
steeper grassy slope beyond. Before he has had time to
note details, he will most likely have been asked by a
gruff person to give an account of himself. The cost of
fortifying some seaports is enormous. Antwerp (Bel-
gium) is defended by an enceinte and two citadels, and
also by no less than 23 enclosed, detached forts, each
with its own garrison, its own magazine, and stores
enough for a six months’ siege. These forts are far enough
from the city to prevent the possibility of bombardment
till at least three or four of them are taken, and they can
only be attacked one at a time. When Sydney and Mel-
bourne are similarly girdled with strong, independent
forts they will be fairly safe, and not before.
While supine local ineflicients —mostly of
the importing class —who judge the country by
themselves, wail about the impossibility of build-
ing an Australian navy locally, the English ex-
perts, Clarke and Esplen, not only think the
proposal feasible, but reckon our present facilities
ample. Clarke says (Esplen agreeing) that he
“would willingly undertake to build a first-class
torpedo boat at Mort’s Dock, with the plant and
conveniences already there.” Perhaps now the
local worm will begin to look up.
Melbourne Argus estimates that Bent’s
surpluses, for four years, have totalled £2,200,000,
and gasps with horror because nearly £1,000,000
of revenue has been spent on public works.
Consequently it yells for a reduction of taxa-
tion. The Argus objection is to the principle
of paying for your public works with your
own money. In the Argus view, all the
work of development
should be done by
Cohen per medium
of the public lOU.
AT THE CANNON'S MOUTH.
The Liberals, in the approaching contest, would have
to face the same forces as the Reid party had to contend
with at the Federal election. Substantially the parties
supporting the liquor and gambling interests ranged up
behind the Labor party.— Job Cook.
Westralia has now started monkeying with
the Sinking Fund, which it some time ago decided
should be made a feature of every loan. In con-
nection with its last visit to the pawnshop, a
resolved that the Sinking Fund provision should
be suspended for four years. But it didn’t resolve
that the foreign mining and timber cos. should
suspend exploiting the country for four years.
It just pushed the repayment of the debt four
years further towards eternity, so as to give the
absentees more time to get the cream off, and
throw more of the burden upon the local people
who will be left with the skimmed milk.
