SPORTING
NOTIONS
Owing to the fact that he was shelved in a
hospital for some weeks, many people had forgotten that
crack English swimmer Dave Billington is still in
Australia. He is now at large again, and in
Sydney. His manager reports that he has made
an excellent recovery, and hopes to shatter a few records
before leaving Australia. The fact that the Australian
crack swimmers cannot meet the man who is regarded as
practically champion of the world in his own line has
aroused a certain languid local interest in a proposal
recently put forward by a British newspaper, 'that in
championship races professionals and amateurs should be
allowed to race together, as long as the pro. re-
ceives no remuneration or share of the takings. While
admitting that it would be very interesting to be able to
decide definitely who is champion of the world in every
branch of sport, The Bulletin is of opinion that the inno-
vation would be a had one generally. It would mean
that, in the great majority of cases, the championships
would be held by men who made a trade of their sport;
and we don’t want to encourage anyone to do that. The
object of sport is to improve the physical development of
the people, and it is unfair to call upon amateurs, who
devote only their spare time to athletics, to compete with
those who live by them. It is far better for a man to
distinctly understand that, when he decides to make his
living by athletics he is of a class apart, and cannot
compete for honors and prizes given to encourage the
people in the mass to take healthy exercise.
ft- * * *
Reported from Maoriland that Dick Cavill has
been showing flashes of his old form, and also that
Richard has given up ginger-beer, signed the pledge to
take no more pea-nuts, and settled down to hard train-
ing. Wild horses and steam tugs have not been able, so
far, to drag from him what he is after. Presumably it
is a match with Billington.
•* *• * •
Although they may not meet him in the water,
the swimmers of Sydney rolled up and gave David Bil-
lington a real bright welcome at the Sports Club when
he arrived in the Harbor city. Referring to his disquali-
fication, Billington made some caustic remarks. He
declares that the action of the Swimming Association
was taken owing to a mix-up in the telegraphed reports
of the Paris swim. He said the officials would not allow
him to give evidence on his own behalf, and refused to
read letters from the officers and president of the
French Association denying the accusation. Bil-
lington concluded his remarks with what was
probably solemn truth : “Being merely the son of
the manager of the municipal baths at Bacup, and
not of an aristocratic family, I suppose, went against
me.” There is but little doubt had his name been the
Honorable David Blueblood Billington, he would have
Still been an unsullied amateur. Billington, by the way,
although only 22 years of age, has 400 prizes to his credit,
including the King’s Gold Cup for Amateurs, which he
won against all cpmers and all nationalities in 1902.
When Reg. Healy started at Drummoyne Bath
on Saturday to win the 1320yds. championship of N.S.W.
he had no idea that he would come out of the water
covered with the undying fame of having broken a world’s
record. Including himself, only four started, and one of
these, Jacobs, left the water after travelling 630yds. The
course was 39 laps and 20yds., and at the end of the 10th
lap Healy overtook Hardwick, who had led him till then,
and passed him. Young Dickman came gliding up and
tackled Hardwick for second place, while Healy streaked
away to win by 28yds. from Dickman, who was full 17yds.
in front of the exhaused Hardwick. Healy had covered
the distance in 18min. 7 2-5 sec. , which was notable time,
having not only broken Dave Billington’s world’s record,
but absolutely smashed it, by chipping off 32 3-5 sec.
David Billington established his record of ISmin. 40sec. at
Leicester on August 4,1903.
• » * »
T. Henderson is one of the crack swimmers
who goes out to Coogee to disport in the breakers, and,
true to his custom, he went out on Sunday last and
struck the hardest day’s work of his life. A fearsome
current was running, and the people who knew nothing
about it went in gaily. No less than 19 of them had to
be hauled out by main force. To return to Henderson.
Two girls were carried out, and Roy Allan gallantly went
to their assistance, only to find himself blinded by the sheen
of the pearly gates. Then T. Henderson jumped into the
life-belt and swam out, but was in the last stage of
exhaustion by the time he got the original three on to
the line, and, although he bad the assistance of the belt,
he was nearly washed out to sea himself. However, he
got back, and took a few seconds on the floor to get his
wind. Just as he was getting a grip of it, two more
flats, men this time, raised a yell for help, and
Henderson and Allan, although not recovered from the
previous excitement, plunged in again and did more
rescue work. Then these two heroes, after a hurried
consultation, decided to leave before they got into
difficulties themselves. All day long boys and girls were
being hauled out, and, immediately they were rescued,
fools who had been looking on plunged gaily in to see
if they could not create a sensation by requiring the life-
line. This sort of thing ought to lead to the prohibition
of bathing on such a beach. When 19 people get into
difficulties in one Sunday, it is time to quarantine the
place.
* » • •
The Melbourne football clubs are publishing
their balance-sheets, as per usual, and as it was in the
past, so it is again in the present. In Collingwood club’s
statement of expenditure, travelling expenses absorb
£133 16s. Over and above those ordinary travelling
exes., the Tasmanian tour ran away with £463 165., and a
special train to Geelong cost £l5 Bs. Total of £613 on
travelling. The total expenditure of the club was
£1368 11s. 4d. Other interesting items are : refreshment
account, £67 Is. 5d.; medicines, bandages and baths,
£3B 35.; uniforms, hoots, etc., £52 65.; and general
expenses, wreaths, washing, boot-studs, flags, etc.,
£3l 9s. 3d. In fact, the only thing that is not set out in
careful detail is the amount paid to professional toe-
ballers for services rendered; but, as the commercial
traveller told the boss, it’s there, all the same, although
you can’t see it.
* * * »
The only difference between Collingwood’s
balance-sheet and that of South Melbourne is that in the
latter’s document the trail of the cloven hoof is more
noticeable. South Melbourne lumps the most incon-
gruous items together in a bewildering sort of way, which
suggests that balance-sheets were invented to hide the
real sources of expenditure. For instance, fire and acci-
dent insurance are lumped together with assistance to
injured players (£91105.). Then the medical fees, which
might reasonably have been included with the assistance
to injured players, are thrown in with the expenses of the
audit, and stand at £45 14s. Travelling expenses are put
in on their own, and amouht to £163 Bs. 9d., and im-
mediately afterwards there are items such as cab hire
to matches, baths, etc., £2O Is. 6d., and a special train to
Geelong totalled £l9. And yet all these are, more or
less, travelling expenses. The supply of uniforms and
boots cost £B7 6s. 2d., and material and training exes,
ran to £56 14s. 4d. Refreshments came to £B3, and
players’ teas swallowed £47 10s. Theatres ate up another
£44 17s. 9d. The most curious were hot sea and Turkish
baths, £25 Bs. 6d., and wedding presents £l5 2s. Band-
ages and embrocation cost £37 Bs. 2d. Apparently there
are some pampered pups in the South crowd. The ques-
tion is, should this sort of thing, which is obviously a
roundabout way of paying crack players for their ser-
vices, be allowed in an amateur organisation ? It would
he interesting to know how many of the gentlemen who
were given money for hot sea baths, for instance, ever
took them.
* * * *
The levelling hand of Sport again — only this
time the hand was a toe. Of the S’Africans who last
season swept the British football fields, 20 fought in the
war—nine on the Jew-Chow side, 11 on the side of their
oountry. In healing wounds the scrum is mightier than
the sword.
* * * *
The competition for the Governor’s Cup, the
trophy for which the Sydney Australian-rules footballers
spoil their clothes and harden their legs, will start on
May 4, when the Y.M.O.A. team will set the ball rolling
against Paddington.
* * * *
Prince Alfred Yacht Club held its final race
for the season on Saturday afternoon under ideal con-
ditions. The handicaps being given at the start of the
race robbed the fixture of the picturesqueness that makes
the sudden unloosing of the whole squadron a sight to be
remembered. Subsequent to the starting of the handicap,
Rawhiti and Awanui were sent off on their second test.
The light wind and the fact that on her approximate
rating Awanui started with a handicap of smin. 32sec.
made the race a hopeless-looking one from the start.
• * • *
True to his custom, owner Brockhoff was behind
time at the starting line, and the rest of the club’s fleet
had been dispatched on the handicap before he came
along. It was the faintest of E.S.E. breezes, and utterly
devoid of weight, so both yachts got away with their top-
sails etched against the turquoise sky. The time allow-
ance was to be adjusted at the finish. Rawhiti had no
hope against the lighter boat, which gained Imin. 46sec.
on the work to Bradley’s. In fact at first it looked as
though Rawhiti was in for a ferocious doing. The
ballooners blew out, and both boats stood for
Manly, where Rawhiti caught a slant of wind and
began to move. But Awanui rounded ahead, although
the lead had dropped to 56sec. Across the heads
Awanui broke away again, and at Shark Island had gained
78ec. ; another Bsecs. were gained on the run to Fort
Denison, where both vessels sailed into a dead calm. On
the run to the winning line Sydney got the first chance
to see what Awanui looks like with them all on. A huge
jib-topsail, balloon foresail and spinnaker hid it from the
vulgar gaze, and it moved through the almost motion-
less atmosphere like a a shadow, reminding one of
an etching on a large emu’s egg. Rawhiti was beaten
by Bmin. 18sec., or 2min. 46sec. more than its rating
allowance. Unless these wins are upset by the measure-
ment, Awanui will he N.S.W. respresentative in the next
contest for the Sayonara Oup.
* ■» * *
In the P.A.Y.O.’s last handicap in Sydney
Harbor Herreshoff and Cooya made a long duel right
away to Manly, where the boat with the Teutonic sound-
ing name was in the van. Kukuburra had closed on
both of them. Before reaching Shark Island Herreshoff
fell away to leeward, and Cooya went to the front, and
Kukuburra drew a little closer. Caught between Rawhiti
and Awanui, Kukuburra fell away, but on the second
round made up the lost water, and catching Cooya on
the run home, beat it by 49sec. This is the Alfred Club’s
last appearance this season.
* * » *
In Sydney regatta and club racing, Brockhoff’s
Rawhiti stands easily first, having scored four wins, one
second, and one third, and prize money to the value of
£47 17s. Scotia and Kukuburra have both scored three
wins each and one second, and Kukuburra has scored
two thirds, a fact which gives her £37 16s. to Scotia’s
£35 7s. Next to Rawhiti, Culwulla was the money
earner, taking £42 6s. for one first, five seconds, and five
thirds—a wonderfully good record.
* « * *
Horses are not the only racing machines that
show sudden and startling reversals of form. In the
open boat racing on Saturday, in the Sydney Sailing
Club’s Handicap, Keriki reversed in a fearsome manner.
Leading by eight seconds at the last turn round Shark
Island, Famous was allowed to overhaul and beat Taylor’s
charge by 17sec. Which looks like darned bad naviga-
tion, to say the least.
’* * * •
On Easter Monday morning Charles Relph’s
Invincible will make n effort to enter Sydney Heads nd
reach the military wharf at Cobbler’s Beach without
being detected by the searchlights. The Invincible is
painted a bright green, and is almost invisible on a gray
day when travelling at high speed. At the fag end of a
faded gray night it will perhaps be quite invisible.
» * * *
Manager Sherwood, of the Sydney branch of
the big Dunlop Rubber Co., announces that his company
will supply a third Dunlop Shield for the cyclists’ Inter-
Club competition. The first shield hangs in the rooms of
the Balmain Club, and the second is the property of the
Enfield body. The Union proposes to make some slight
alteration in the conditions of the competition for the
third shield.
* * * *
Newcastle (N.S.W.) is the only branch of the
League of Wheelmen which keeps on catering for racing
cyclists near Sydney, and rakes in the shekels in conse-
quence. A two-nights’ meeting last week was a success
financially ; and brought out some fine racing-. The chief
item was a challenge match between English cyclist
Gascoigne (who recently plugged under the assumed
name of “ Mills”) and O. H. Brook ; but Brook put his
opponent down rather easily, and also won the five-mile
scratch race, and the ten-mile State championship.
Gascoigne, however, is a better handicap cyclist, and
won two open handicaps from scratch.
* ■* * »
The Dunlop Rubber Co. is now making pre-
liminary arrangements for the running of the Warrnam-
bool to Melbourne road race inter-State tests. The big
event itself will probably transpire some time in Sep-
tember, but in the meantime each State has to hold its
own test race to decide who is competent to represent it
in the biggest road race in Australasia—if not the world.
There’s a sure fortune awaiting that substi-
tute for rubber, recently paragraphed in The Bulle-
tin, if it proves effective. During the last 15 years
the demand for rubber has more than doubled, and
is still increasing by leaps and bounds. In South
America whole rubber plantations have been ruth-
lessly destroyed in the endeavor to keep pace with
an increasing market, and as the African (Congo)
material is of practically no use for tyres the outlook for
the cyclist and the motorist is disquieting. Big groves
of rubber trees have been planted in Ceylon and the
Straits Settlements, but it will be some years before they
are in full blast, so to speak. Under these circumstances
it is very probable that wheel and car men will be shortly
faced with the alternative of paying more for their tyres
or having less rubber in them.
* * * *
Approval has been given for the construction
of a broad avenue from the new Mitchell Library, across
the Domain, leading up to the National Art Gallery.
This will mean a knock-out blow to the old cricket pitch
on the Domain, where the inter-State matches used to be
played by persons in top-hats. The same green, later on,
was the scene of the important inter-club matches, when
the Carlton, Albert, and Belvidere clubs struggled for
supremacy. The decision illustrates the usual tendency
in Australia to steal a bit of park at every opportunity
for a public building, or a road, or a Governor’s residence.
Manly (Syd.) has now got its breaker-riding
boat launched, and henceforth surf-bathing will be a
much safer pastime than it was in the days arear.
Attorney-General Wade performed the ceremony of
cutting the ribbon, and ,the boat, which was manned
mostly by the famous Sly life-saving family, shot away
to where the dazzling green turned to rearing white
horses, and rode them easily. It will be now almost
impossible 'to commit suicide at the ocean beach, let alone
get drowned against one’s will. Out at Bondi, practi-
cally nothing has been done. Some of the more
regular habitues have formed a life-saving club, which
works hard instructing swimmers how to go about saving
a person in difficulties ; but the Council has not lifted a
finger. Although Manly Council was quite prepared to
pay for the construction of its boat out of the municipal
funds, it was, fortunately, unnecessary. The people
shelled out so liberally that the boat and the life-saving
plant have all been paid for by public subscription.
Manly is now the most popular and safest place—the only
safe place, in fact—in which to indulge in the luxury
of surf bathing.
* * * *
Cabled that Lord Linlithgow, alias Hopetoun,
has again fallen off his neddy in the hunting field, and
fractured some part of himself. The foolishness of
Linlithgow, alias Hopetoun, is saddening. Like most of
the British Governors with whom Australia has been
burdened, Hopey was absolutely dangerous on horseback
unless tied on. The bright, loveable exception to the
rule was young Duff, whose father died while running
N.S.W. On one occasion when riding in an amateur
event Duff hugged the brute underneath him as though
it were a long-lost brother, or his best girl, or something
of that sort. There wasn’t an inch of that horse’s surface
he didn’t touch, and the animal’s coat shone like satin
from the polishing Duff’s riding pants gave it. And as
for the range of the spurs—well, there are things that
are not nice to write about.
* * # *
Tasmania, of which so little has been heard
lately that its presence on the map was beginning to be
a matter of doubt, had its annual sculling championship
tussle lately. W. Robinson, of Esk, was the winner, and
in the fierce struggle for the championship of Sleepy
Hollow he easily outcrawled Jack, of Tamar. It was a
bloodless victory. Sport, like most other things, save
sleep, is very anaemic in the Flyspeck.
Something like a sensation was made in tennis
circles in Sydney last week when N.S. Waler Horace
Rice beat the hitherto invincible Maorilander, Parker, in
the Singles Championship of N.S.W. tournament. Rice’s
three sets were won by 6—4, 6—3, 6—l; and Parker got
a look in twice with 6—3 and 7—5. Tennis players nearly
smothered Rice when the game was over, and one enthu-
siastic lady player hugged him boldly and unashamedly.
Rice will hold the Cup for the next twelve mouths.
* * * *
“ Vulcan ” : Accounts of a weird motor happen-
ing come from France. A Montpellier doctor purchased
a car at Lyons, and engaged a chauffeur to drive It home,
the owner and two friends accompanying. At a steep
incline, the car began to fly down without brakes. The
doctor shouted for them to be applied, but no notice was
taken, and, bending forward to enforce his commands, he
was somewhat surprised to find the wheel in the hands of
a dead man ! The car crashed into a house a t the bottom
of the hill, killing the doctor’s two friends, and fracturing
the medical gentleman’s leg.
* * * *
John Wren, law-breaker, hauled down his
colors last week and undertook to abandon bo;h the
Collingwood Tote and the City Tattersall’s Club. An
application had been made to have the premises
placed in “ quarantine ” according to the provisions of
Bent’s new Anti-Gambling Act; and then the tote-man
seems to have realised that the game was up. So one of
his friends waited on the superintendent of police and
requested that the extreme step should not be taken,
as it would not injure Mr. Wren, but the owners of the
properties, who were quite innocent. The superintendent
made no promise, but hied him out to Studley House,
where Wren lies a-hed from the results of the knock he
got in the row at Swinburne’s meeting. There
Wren personally undertook to close up and retire from
both premises and have no more to do with the running
of illegal gambling enterprises. He is going to settle
down and be a respectable racecourse proprietor.
This sympathy for the owners of the property is very
touching. If they allowed their premises to be used for
the purpose of defeating the law, they are richly entitled
to all the injury the law can do them.
* • •
One of the curiosities of the A. J.O. legislation
is that, although no horse-owner is allowed to either race
horses or lay odds at a pony meeting, a man who operates
as a bookmaker at pony meetings can race horses at
meetings under the A. J.C. suzerainty. Why the A.J.C.
has thus greased the pony bookmaker has never been
explained.
* * * *
o The Wade Anti-Gambling Act created a strange
P, sifcion with regard to the hitherto “ unregistered
•Yuba.” In the old days, when the A.J.C. was a law unto
■self, the pony branch of the sport was utterly accursed,
a nd no one who touched the unclean thing could enter
%e kingdom of the A.J.C. But the Wade Act altered all
c hat. The A.J.C. is no longer the panjandrum of the
business, and the pony-racing section is as legal as the
A.J.C. itself. Moreover, as the business is now all fixed
by law, the reasons which made the A.J.C. attitude
reasonable and justifiable in the past no longer operate.
Therefore, the A.J.C. should take time by the eyebrow
and do gracefully the thing that it will be compelled to
do in any case before very long. It is a wise A.J.C. that
knows when to get out. Instead of facing the matter in
a straightforward manner and dealing with the whole
question, the A.J.C. has so far just shuffled round it, and
intimated in a surreptitious sort of manner that individual
cases will he dealt with on their merits. Now that the
Kensington, Rosebery Park and Ascot clubs have
amalgamated, and have the time they originally spent
fighting one another idle on their hands, they will
probably devote some of their leisure to forcing the hand
of the erstwhile governing club.
• • • •
The country people who are pouring to Sydney
are deeply interested in the high-jumping at Wirth’s
Circus. Just now the crack jumper is Lady Morton,
who last Saturday night, spite of a strained shoulder,
cleared 6ft. Bin. This mare, which was bred by H. D.
Morton, of Coolangatta (N.S.W.), beat all Australasian
records at Christchurch when she cleared, with several
inches to spare, a 7ft. OJin. jump in the presence of the
press and a judging committee. The only performances
to beat this are those of two Amurkan horses, Heather-
bloom and Pearl. Pearl cleared 7ft. 6}in. at Des Moines,
lowa, on September 20, 1902. This was regarded as a
world’s record, but in 1903, at Philadelphia Horse Show,
Heatherbloom cleared 7ft. 9in., and, two years later,
eclipsed her own, and all other records, by
clearing 7ft. lOin. at Norfolk, Virginia. It is now
explained that at Amurkan horse-shows the neddies
jump from turf-covered spring-hoards, and for that
reason their records are not taken seriously in other
parts of this ball of mud. If this is correct, then Lady
Morton holds the championship of the world for high
jumping under natural conditions. Lady Morton is not
the only jumper the show possesses, however ; another
horse, Maori, is a fairly-good flyer, and the little 13'2
hands pony, Playmate, is a wonder. At Sydney Show in
April, 1905, he broke all records of his class by clearing
6ft. 4in., and at Moree, last year, he jumped 6ft. Bin.—a
great perfoimance. It would be worth while fixing up a
turf spring-board, and trying Lady Morton and Playmate
from it. More than likely the Yankee faked records
would be badly beaten. The Yankee is not a sport; he is
a mechanical fake all the time.

has received 47 entries for the Adelaide Cup, and the
same number for the Goodwood Handicap, the total
being the best since 1903. The Onkapavinga Club, which
races on Easter Monday only, has sold by auction its

* * * *
Owing to the fact that the previous Saturday
was wet, the latest round of Sydney Grade cricket had to
pe played in one afternoon, and the result was that of
the six games played no less than four were drawn. The
victorious teams were Waverley, which beat Balmain bv
nve wickets and five runs on the first innings, and Cen-
tral Cumberland, which downed Glebe by four runs on
the first innings. The position at the top of the
competition list is.unaltered. Glebe has receded from
eight points to six, and Balmain has progressed the
wrong way, and is now five less than nothing. Central
Cumberland’s win leaves it still fifth on the list with four
points, and Waverley is now one whole point instead of
being one less than nothing. Paddington, as before, is
at the top with 11 points, and Gordon second with 10.
Burwood and Glebe follow with seven and six points re-
spectively. Among individual players Alphabetical
White, of North Sydney, did far and away the
best with the bat. He carried his weapon against
Paddington for 125. Next on the roll of honor was Victor
Trumper, who had made 98 for Paddington when he was
stumped by Coltnian off Clarke. A. J. Nicholls, of Red-
fern, made 91 against University before Harvey boa led
him. The most successful bowler of the day was Bowden,
of Glebe, who took five wickets for 33 runs. Payne, of
Central Cumberland, took five of Glebe’s for 41 ; Connelly,
of the same club, backed up Payne with three for 30.
But the most consistent bowler of the afternoon was Pitt,
of Gordon, who was responsible for the taking of seven of
Sydney’s wickets for 49. Gregg and Docker, of Burwood,
took three for 28 and four for 48 respectively against
Middle Harbor, and Cottier, of Glebe, knocked down
three of Central Cumberland’s stumps for 29.
* * * .
The latest development in the McLean-Johnson
upheaval is that McLean (now out of bed) will not
allow Johnson to fight Bill Smith, as the colored bruiser
intimated he would do. Johnson came to Australia
pledged to fight under McLean’s management, and
McLean has an agreement with Mr. Brennan, so he
says ; and he “ reckons ” that, under these circumstances,
Johnson’s name, as far as any fighting without his
consent is concerned, is just plain slush. Johnson’s view
of the position is not available at time of writing. Any-
how. he seems to be a whale for fighting of one sort and
another.
* * * *
“Suss”: I have seen many fights, and many
referees, but I have never seen a referee yet who did not
take notes at the end of each round, and I have never
seen one yet who didn’t do it badly. He never has a pro-
per notebook or a decent pencil. The paper most times
looks as though he had rushed to the nearest hoarding
and torn a hit off the corner of a poster. It is a ragged,
crumpled, dirty fragment about 25 inches by 1 5-lCth.
This his referee-ship carries hidden about him somewhere,
and at the end of the round he occupies 20 seconds
looking for it. Having found it, he takes 20 more
seconds to look for a chewed, deformed hunk of pencil.
Having found that, he furtively lurches into a corner of
the ring- where nobody can see, and scratches into the
palm of his left hand with the stub of pencil. This is
repeated at the end of every round. When the 20th is
reached, the 25 x 1 5-16 square inches of papers ought to
be covered on both sides with notes, and I’ve wondered
again and again how the referee was going to read them,
and how long he was going to take. But the curious
thing is that he never looks at them. He walks over and
declares someone the winner without consulting the
notes at all.
Apropos of that guff about the frozen
eminence on which judicial persons sit, the com-
plaint about one N.S.W. Judge is the warm
proximity he favors when sitting down in a tram.
He always makes a point of sitting as close as he
can to any pleasing girl, whether he knows her or
not.
Goldsbrough, Mort and Co. report:—ln the
absence of auction sales there is little to chronicle in
wool. As usual at this period of the year, wool is coming
forward in very limited quantities, the hulk of the
supplies consisting of local scoureds and a few Queens-
land parcels, which are readily placed at values fully
equivalent to those ruling at the last auctions. The
next sales in this centre will be held during the week
commencing April 16th.*
The most interesting man in Australia
last week was Yarischetti, an Italian miner, who
was leading a cave-dwelling existence down
a mine at Bonnie Vale (W.A.). The mine
became flooded, and everybody escaped save
Yarischetti. He fled to the top of a stope,
and the compressed air kept the water back
and left him a fragment of condensed atmosphere
and a patch of unutterable blackness to live in,
By banging on the thin wall behind him, he
managed to draw the attention of explorers in
adjoining workings, and the management set
to work hard to pump out the mine. Also,
two immensely plucky professional divers
went down, and after a long, dangerous journey
under water reached the Italian with food, lights,
tobacco, and information. Likewise, fresh air
was pumped in to him. It is expected the
blackened hermit will be out about the time this
issue appears—unless the wall behind him starts
to leak and his precious little stock of compressed
air escapes. In that case, it is all up with Varis-
clietti. ■ '
HEADACHES.
One of the chief causes of headaches is the cir-
culation of poisons in the blood. Poisons are
constantly being formed in the body in the diges-
tion and absorption of food, but they produce no
ill effects, unless formed in excess. In indigestion,
the formation of these poisons takes place at an
enormous rate ; they pass through the system in
the circulating blood, and produce high blood
pressure and a sense of general ill-health, iirita-
bility of,temper, and headache, which only the
sufferer can realise the extent of. To overcome
headaches it is absolutely necessary to get at the
cause. Drugs which act on the heart and reduce
the pulsations only have a temporary effect. If
you suffer from indigestion, biliousness, constipa-
tion, any disorder of the liver and kidneys, or
from any weakness peculiar to your sex, there is
no better remedy than Dr. Morse’s Indian Root
Pills.
Mrs. Johnson, of 397 Pyrmont-street, Pyrmont,
Sydney, states I wish to add my testimony
to the valuable properties of your pills. I have
always found them a most efficacious remedy for
headache and dizziness, and a most valuable
medicine for married women. lam convinced if
your pills were more universally adopted as a
family medicine there would be far less suffering.
I always recommend them, and use no other medi-
cine in my family.”*

The Piano of the Future
The New Pianola Piano is an upright piano of the highest
grade, with a Metrostyie Pianola inside its case „ It is in every
particular a perfect piano, and, in addition, contains within its
case a complete Metrostyie Pianola, the only Piano-playing
device endorsed by the greatest living musicians.
It can be played by the fingers on the keyboard in the
usual way.
It can be played by means of a Pianola action within its case.
There is nothing to move up in front of the keyboard. To change the
method from hand-playing to Pianola-playing it is only necessary to slide
back a panel in the front of the case, and insert a perforated roll of music.
There is no outward indication which catches the eye to distinguish the
Pianola Piano from the heretofore type of Pianoforte.

Public Notice.
AN APOLOGY.
20th March, 1907.
Dear Madam, —
I hereby desire to express my regret that I
should have inadvertently reflected upon you in
any way whatever with reference to your having
travelled in the same steamer with the Sultan of
Jahore.
I disclaim all intention of reflecting on your
reputation in any way, and I unreservedly apolo-
gise for any statements I have made reflecting on
your reputation, and I desire that this retraction
and apology be published.
Yours truly,
JAS. C. BARTLETT,
Spring Street, Port Melbourne.
Mrs. S. A. Serebrenikow,
Formerly Miss Queenie Strachan.
Witness —
J. Woolf, Solicitor,
Melbourne.

trial. The first package I had I felt the benefit of, and since, needless
to say, I have never been without it, using it in many of the delightful
little receipts given with each package. These wonderful little receipts
prevent the monotony felt so often with other foods.
I sleep better, feeling refreshed in the morning, have gained

feel in the best of health. I feed my little girl with it also ; she, like
myself, suffered from anaemia, and was a constant worry and trouble to

I strongly recommend all mothers to use Grape-Nuts, and they can-
not fail m getting the happiest results, for it is certainly the most

children, as well as those in the prime of health.

WHAT IS CATARRH?
CATARRH is inflammation of the lining
membrane of the nose and adjoining
passages. If this inflammation is not ar-
rested it invades the passages which lead
from the nose to the head, ears, throat and
lungs. It injures the sight and hearing,
destroys the sense of taste and smell, ren-
ders the breath offensive, breaks down
the affected tissues, and consumes the
nasal cartilages. The discharge, passing
through the lungs and stomach, causes
dyspepsia, also "consumption. Do you
want relief and cure? If so, try our
great remedy.

POLICY for the COMMONWEALTH.
Price 6d, from The Bulletin Office.
There was a tremendous exodus of public
men from Victoria last week. Talbot and his
lady. Bent and his daughter, and an Under-
Treasurer and a Secretary went on the Tuesday,
amid speeches, and band-playing, and waving of
the public hanky. Next day Tait and his cigar
vanished - in a cloud of smoke to pick up the boat
at Adelaide. Tait did not go unsung. All
along the route the railway men knew that he was
going, and as the inspection car went past they
lined up and cheered themselves hoarse. No
man’s departure from Victoria has ever been more
lustily cheered. No men ever seemed as glad to
see the last of any other man as the railway men
seemed when they saw the tail end of Tait fly
round the corner.
While the departed Governor Talbottle
was touring Victoria he had some experiences.
One of the best of them was up in the Deep Lead
district. He arrived late at night, but the mayor
and councillors and the upper 48 of the towns-
people were waiting, and the festive Corned beef
was laid in the town hall. The mayor stood him-
self on end and spoke briefly :
Your Excellency and Gentlemen, —We have 16 toasts
to get through, so I won’t trouble you with a speech
about the first. It is ’‘The King,’’ and I will contente
myself with remarking that he is a good king—one of th -
best—and if we were to dispense with his services to
morrow morning, he’d have another job before tea-time,
because the French would engage him to be their fresi-
sident. They know him, and are just waiting to snap
him up the first chance. So I ask you to be upstanding,
and drink the King, God bless him.
They drank it all right, and Reggie Talbot was
noticed to wipe tears from his eyes, he was so
much affected. Talbottle has an iron mouth that
never laughs unless he wants it to, but his eyes
are only meat.
!Lord William Nevill, charged with prac-
tising legerdemain in connection with jewellery,
has been committed to take his trial in the
beastly common, vulgar way ; he is said to be
directly descended from Warwick, the King-
Maker. Perhaps his youngsters, if he has any,
will be forced to admit that they are descended
from William the Stone-Breaker.
W. T. Carter, ex-Victorian M.L.A., who
died a few weeks ago in England, possessed a
faculty for thriving on his mistakes. About 25
years ago he was the Rev. W. T. Carter, Methodist
parson, who preached to the hairy and profane
sawyers and splitters of Wirrabarra Forest (S. A.).
There he made the mistake of interweaving with
his religious exhortations much of the local pro-
fanity of expression. He was summoned to
Adelaide to make a personal explanation,
hut he explained in such splendid sawing
and wood-chopping language that the board-
room smelled of sulphur for over a week
thereafter. Methodism then knew him no more
as a parson. Later he struck a Williamstown
(Vie.) audience of stevedores, lumpers, crimps,
sailors, fishermen, &c., with his sirocco of lan-
guage, and was enthusiastically elected their
M.L.A.
“Snell”: The Rev. G. A. Judkins, of
Bendigo (Vie.), little brother of W. H. Judkins,
of Melbourne, is a parson with an anxious eye
and a nervous manner, who is continually making
bids for notoriety. But he hasn’t the luck of
Brother Rill, and his rockets won’t soar. His
latest effort is a fervent denunciation of public
marriages for show purposes, as calculated to
degrade the bond and to make a sort of serio-
comic turn of the sacred ceremony. This out-
burst is inspired by an exhibition marriage, to
take place in connection with the Bendigo Easter
Fair. However, marriage is a purely secular
affair here, and nobody need put religious
frills on unless so disposed ; consequently the
impression is that Pastor Judkins would be
better employed lifting up his howl against the
expensive, be-spangled swagger wedding, stage-
managed in a decorated cathedral, that draws a
fighting, tearing crowd into the alleged sacred
edifice, and is worked off like a sensation act in
a bad melodrama. The latter excitement is sup-
posed to be a holy function, while the wedding, as a
side show at a fair, is a mere let-us-be-joyful pagan
kick-up, but the reverend sky-terrier overlooks
the flash, expensive irreverence, and barks his
small head off at the little brown incident. The
Easter Fair wedding may be a vulgar show, hut
I’ve seen weddings at the Cathedral in Melbourne
that were a whole circus, clown, bounders, mena-
gerie, and all.

“Relay”: T’other day a sun-baked
citizen, straight from Jewburg (Africa), entered
my railway carriage on his way from the boat.
He had arrived that morning, together with 58 car-
penters and bricklayers, all bound for Australia.
He expressed, in no measured terms, a general
despair of S’Africa under the present system.
Property depreciated about 60 per cent, during
last year, and citizens are flocking to the coast.
Outgoing steamers get what labor they need
free if they will take the workers away ; anybody
will work free to get away. Many families
can’t even get away. My stranger remarked on
the prosperous look of Melbourne, and was
pleasantly amazed at the beautiful lack of dark
skins. He said, “ I’m blanky well sure no one
knows what a good blanky wicket they’re on
here till they get a taste of some other blanky
place,” and alluded flatteringly to Australia’s
horse-sense, as evidenced in the White Australia
and contract-labor policies. Australians of the S.
Fish variety would do well to come personally
into contact with a few of this kind of person.
Dear Bulletin. —l am not a church-going
man—one has so little time to spare when mooch-
ing about. But once was compelled, while in a
Victorian Benevolent Asylum’s hospital-ward, to
listen, five times per week, to various clerics:
Sunday, Baptists; Monday night. Salvation
Army; Wednesday, Oongregationalists; Thurs-
day, C.E. Missionaries, &c. All these good
people begged God, in their prayers, to give us
POOR PEOPLE STRENGTH TO BEAR OUR TROUBLES
AND STAND OUR PAINS, AND PREPARE US FOR
Eternity. Not once during the whole seven
weeks I was there did one pray to God to ease our
pain, relieve our sufferings, or cure us, and then
alleviate our poverty with the solid cash that is
of no use to Him, but of so much use to us. Oh,
religion, what sins are committed in thy name !
Mooch About.

As a matter of fact, the loss on the undertaking in
1 1)04 was £3688 ; hut, as the City Fund had to make
good the required contribution to the Sinking Fund,
and as the 1904 balance-sheet showed no liability
to the City Fund on that account, it isn’t easy
to represent matters in any other way than the
foregoing. Actually, then, the amounts shown as written
off during the last two years are amounts advanced in
the early period. So far three statements have been
issued by the department, and the form of each has been
different. This makes it difficult to follow the
transactions. The latest accounts are certainly in
the, best form. But even they are capable of improve
ment. In 1905, for instance, certain goodwill items—
loan flotation expenses, preliminary expenses, &c.—
totalled £5OlO. This year the items are not the Same ;
but, apparently, while a.profit of £lOOO has been made on
loan flotations, a further £643 has gone' in expenses.
These things, however, are not clear, but are a matter
only of computation. Of the profit, £8485 has come from
tne undertaking itself, and £163 from interest on thfe
sinking fund, which now amounts to £7160. The whole
position may be put as follows :
Deficit, profit and loss account .. £9552
Goodwill, paper assets .. .. ;. 4340
Total deficit £13,892
LeSs Sinking fund .. .. £<l69
Depreciation account .. .. 4770
11,930
Net Deficit*.' 5 £1,962
• l 4
The actual results of the business are perhaps
best shown this way :
Woiking Net
Revefuje. Expenses. Revenue. Interest. Loss.
1904 .. £4,061 £6,313 £2,436 £3688’
1905 .. 22,176 15,005 £7,171 7,874 703
Profit.
1906 .. 40,983 21,567 19,416 10,931 8,485
Compare the last column here with the loss or profit
shown in the first table. In the first year the difference
is accounted for by the contribution from the city
treasury, now being repaid ; in the following years the
difference is represented by the interest earned by the
sinking fund investments.
Of the reserves shown above, along with
the sinking fund, £4770 is depreciation account. As
nothing else is provided out of revenue to cover
depreciation, this is not a genuine reserve, but a mere
set-off against actual wear and tear. This £477o—the
first amount ever provided—is at the rate of about 2 p.c.
on the average amount invested during the year. The
question is whether that is enough. The Melbourne
undertaking, which used to proyide 2 p.c., finds it
necessary from time to tiriie to make special
provision ; last year it brought the total written
off for the 12 months to p.c. ThCre seems
to be an idea that when an undertaking has
a sinking fund it needn’t worry much about anything
else. But a sinking fund will simply pay Cohen off ; it
won’t replace the works when they are worn out. The
capital in the fixed assets of the undertaking is now
£280,464, and in stores, &c., about £20,000; the out-
standing loans are £310,000. Taking the average capital
for the year at £265,000, the profit, after paying interest,
was at the rate of £3 ss. 3d. percent., available to provide
for sinking fund and depreciation. It is none too much ;
but the improvement is most marked, and, anyway, for a
great concern which has not yet completed its third
year, the result can only be regarded as satisfactory. The
value of future reports would be added to if the charge
for light and power were mentioned, along with any
variations since the business started.
The sooner two or three items are wiped out
of the Sydney Electric Light accounts the more
respectable they will look. Some £lOOO or more has been
paid as damages in connection with installations; and
“Damages” appears as an asset, to be wiped off in 1C
years ; and “preliminary expenses” are to be wiped off
in 25 years. These and others are such hilarious “ assets ”
that the City Council can’t wipe them out too soon.
The joke will be much too venerable 23 years from now.

This society has not yet presented its accounts in an
intelligible form. You never know how one half-year
really turned out till the next half-year’s accounts are
issued, for “ fees to committee and auditors " are not
charged to profit and loss, but hung up«for six months
and then shown in the appropriations. The society has
a capital account and a loan account. It is stated that
the interest on members’ capital is at the rate,of 4 per
cent, per annum ; also, it is understood that, so far, loans
have carried 4 per cent. Yet the figures in the appro
priation account can’t be made to correspond With any
published figures as to either capital or loans, and what
the “ dividend ” means in the appropriation account it is
impossible to tell. It may even be some sort of bonus oi
discount on members’ cash purchases. The sqdiety has
got into an absurd style of boolf-keeping and tpe use of
expressions that are confusing. No doubt it is all per-
fectly clear to the men, mostly genuine philanthropists,
who run the society, and have been connected with it for
more years than some of them care to remember. But to
the outsider the thing involves guesswork. The society
is r'w entirely free from debt except to its own mem-
ber., who can “ pull out” at any time ; and it is at last
making a pretty good showing in regard to a reserve
fund, of which it was so inexplicably shy for years. In
fact, the only thing about it at which the word of reproach
can be thrown is the antediluvian form of the published
accounts.
* * • 0
Murchison Associated, Day Dawn (W.A.), after
working 12 years, and suffering 11 reconstructions of a
more or less virulent character, is still hopefully viewed
by the Board as a twin of the Great Fingall. Already
over £120,000 of Bull’s money has been spent on the
mine, or in the office, but nothing except an occasional
small crushing has resulted. The lowest level is at 800 ft.,
but no gold shoot has been got below 300 ft., although
nearly 45Cft. have been driven north at the bottom level.
Shareholders are assured that the reef which is being
worked is the Great Fingall reef, but are not told that
the Fingall reef splits coming north, and is practically
worthless beyond the split, which is 600 ft. north of the
Fingall shaft, and 1£ mile south of the Murchison
Associated.
• * * •
The directors of Queensland Exploration, Ltd.,
of Cloncurry, are F. O. D. Haggard (brother of Rider
Hago-ard—there are tremendous numbers of people in
England who have brothers), Lord Ernest Hamilton, and
A B Dealtry, with a Queensland advisory board, and
the everlasting Burns, Philp and Co. are the managers
The co is entitled, says the prospectus, to half the pro
ceeds of the freehol surface rights. Who gets the other
half? Also, a royalty payable to the British Colonial
Railways Co. is mentioned and notspecified. The English
Companies Acts are very stringent as to disclosures of
agreements ; how comes it that agreements as to “ sur-
face rights” and “ royalties ’’are merely hinted at?

AND! DATES FOR HEALTH must first of all recognise the paramount
influence of the liver ; it is the organ which holds the key of life and
death. It regulates the whole mechanism of the human frame ; it is

only when the liver acts properly that all the other
organs—the kidneys, bladder, stomach, and intes-
tines—work efficiently, and there is general good
health. The liver, furthermore, exercises a still
higher function than that of mere mechanism. It
produces great moral effects in the influence it
exercises on the temperament; an active liver
creates brilliancy, energy, shrewdness, keen intel-
lect, and many other excellent qualities. Reverse
the picture and see the irritable, nervous, fidgety,
and morbidly despondent individual. In despair,
the victim of liver torpor will sometimes endeavor
to thfasli it into temporary activity by drastic
medicines. This is useless. The proper remedy
for a disordered liver is a course of Kutnow’s

How to Avoid Fraudulent Imitations.
The Gemline and Original Kutnow’s Powder can be had of all conscientious
Chemists throughout the world. See that the-fac-simile signature, “S.
Kutnow and Co., Ld.,” and the registered trade-mark, “ Hirschensprung, or
Deer Leap,” are on the carton and bottle. You will then avoid imitations,

A Child's Delicate SKin.
Its Care should be the First Consideration
of riothers to Ensure a More
Healthy Constitution.
FROM the very beginning of its life a child should be made accustomed to the use
of the purest ingredients in its daily bath to ensure proper nourishment of the
skin, and the building up of a more active and vigorous constitution.
Lack of early attention in this particular very often leads to serious
skin and internal disorders, owing to its weakened condition due to
improper and injurious compounds, principally common soaps, robbing
the immatured skin of the child of the nourishment it needs.
Every mother knows that the daily bath is as necessary for the
babe as is its food, and is largely responsible for its good health. The
very tender skin is so apt to get raw, chafed, and inflamed, and the use
of inferior soaps irritate the parts, causing restlessness, pain, and very
often skin troubles in a malignant form.
Obtain peace of mind for yourself, and comfort and skin health
for your child for all time by obtaining Zam-Buk Medicinal
Toilet tSoap. Use it exclusively in the bath each day, and you
will find it contains the best medicines possible to soothe and comfort
the child and ensure its future good skin health.
Zam-Buk Soap is of special value for all requirements or the bath and toilet where a
softness and refinement of the skin and complexion is necessary. It will remove the
coarse,dry feeling caused by dust, wind, sun, and acids contained in gritty powders and com-
mon toilet soaps, and will, with constant use, continue to exert its powerful influence in the
prevention of disease and the maintenance of good skin health. Zam-Buk Soap is reeom-
mended and used largely in the sick room, owing to its great purifying and sanitary influence.

Business, Robbery, etc.
“Stony”: There are more prams, than people
on the sidewalks of Prahran (Vic.) these summer Satur-
day afternoons. Out there, where a great part of the
country’s backbone is found—the part which sweats for
tbs country’s good—the “ Fambly ” has to be taken out
shopping because it can’t be left at home. Prams, there
are, and feeding bottles, and very fat, purple women with
loud voices, and children with pink sugar-sticks. Also
dogs. The bargains are astonishing. When I start my
suburban villa I am going to do my shopping in Chapel-
street, Pra’n. You can get some very cheap lines in eggs
and macintoshes ; and the men’s trousers at 4s. lid. the
pair are particularly refreshing. But there is something
suspicious about the dining-room suite at £2 10s. I
don’t see how they can do it, particularly when one con-
siders the system of payments at Is. a week. lam sure
it cannot be quite good at the price.
* * * *
“Quan”: When Australia sets out to make
alcoholic fuel it won’t have to rely on that exotic, spiny
curse, prickly pear, for its raw material; the indigenous
grass-tree will supply it. Experiments in Westralia
show that the fibres of the core of the grass-tree yield
from half-a-gallon to a gallon-and-a-quarter of proof
spirit per bushel. Early Swan River convicts knew of
the grass-tree’s capabilities, and used to make a liquor
from it. Had Botany Bay convicts known the same trick,
some of Sydney’s leading families would now be doing
honest toil, instead of riding in carriages, enjoying the
fortunes made for them bv their rum-selling grandfathers.
• • * •
“ A.E.M.” : The cool drink and fruit luncheon
man grew confidential, the other day. “It’s been a
wretched season,” he said. “ What suits me is a good,
steady heat—say, 106' in the shade. When it’s like that
in my shop I can take from £l2 to £l5 a day in cool
drinks, ice-creams, and fruit luncheons and salads. When
the ’mometer drops to 95°, my takings drop, too, to any-
thing from 30s. to £3 a day. Take it all round, lemon-
squash is most popular of all the soft drinks. Squash,
with soda or lemonade, is 4d. ; with water, 3d. With
lemons cheap, I’m right. With lemons at 2d. wholesale,
where’s my profit coming in ? When lemons are about
20. each to me, you get less’n a quarter lemon in your
squash. The flavoring’s lime-juice. Fruit salads are
popular and profitable. You can get rid of your split
peaches and your speckled apples, and your black-skinned
bananas can bury themselves.beneath the cream.”
Writing of Weyerhauser, America’s lumber
king, lately alleged to have been kidnapped, Norcross,
Washington correspondent of London papers, talks of
America’s timber resources. “ Nothing in this country is
growing by leaps and bounds like timber properties. The
pinch is coming. The prodigal waste of years is creating
a paucity of desirable timber tracts. A piece of land in
W. Virginia, covered with spruce and hemlock, and pur-
chased five years ago for 12,000 dols., was recently sold for
500,000 dols." And he gives other instances. Think —
£2400 turned into £lOO,OOO in five years 1 And without
an hour’s labor or a shilling expenditure. And the fool
Governments of Australia are following the same course.
Timber is cut down and rushed away by the square mile,
without the slightest attempt at re-afforestation being
made. Australia is living on its forest capital just as it
has done on its land asset.
• * * *
On March Ist there came into force a law to the
effect that all brandy entering the Commonwealth must
be distilled juice of the grape. Concoctions of spirit
distilled from any old thing, and hitherto masquerading
.as pure brandy, must now drop the word brandy off the
label before they can be passed by the Customs. So
during the last days of February the villainous importer
rushed vast quantities of sham brandy through the
Customs, the amount of duty on this one article collected
in Sydney alone on February 27 being £3lOO. Conse-
quently, until the present stocks are used up, Australia
will still drink spirit made out of pine shavings, and old
boiled-down boots and hats, and all manner of wild sub-
stances, and the importer will not be ashamed.
* • * *
“S.T.”: A large firm, importing periodicals,
which recently closed its doors in Australia, told its
newsagents that competition and Trusts, and other
things, had pushed it over, and incidentally remarked
that, during its life, it had built up Australia’s periodical
and “ comic ” trade to £30,000 per annum. Let us, there-
fore, consider some: We pay a big sum like that for
what? Is it for something that will teach us to believe
in Australia, and work for it, and, if necessary, fight for
it? Not much ! The £30,000 goes yearly to bring us a
brown’s worth of inanity in the shape of “ penny comics”
or a penn’orth of ’Omb Slops, which affords us a full
account of how Lord Toneyville or Milady Middle Broke
“ took the waters.” Personally, I’m not sorry that that
firm shuts up shop.
« • * •
N.S. Wales Deputy-Comptroller of Prisons
M'Cauley, who was largely instrumental in introducing
the finger-print system into Australia, suggests the use
of the method to prevent cheque forgeries. The idea is
that each bank customer should leave his thumb-print
with the teller, and when he draws a cheque he should
put his thumb print on the back of it as evidence of good
faith. There are two difficulties in the way. First, a
teller couldn’t recognise a thumb-print as he does a
signature, and there would be a slow and laborious
process of comparison. Second, a man in a large way of
business, who signed a number of cheques daily, would
have a verv murky and disgraceful-looking thumb.
What’s the "matter with a toe-print? The toe isn’t
conspicuous.
* * * *
“W.H.W.”: “Hermes’” sympathy with the
burglar who ought to succeed, but is pipped by an acci-
dent (B. 14/2/’O7), calls to mind an incident of the fifties
in Ballarat. / Wedged in on the main thoroughfare near
the Montezuma and Charlie Napier Theatres was a gold
buyer, financier, &c., with a good record. Owning a large
fire-proof safe, many diggers stored their gold with him.
Late one night fires broke out simultaneously on either
side of the premises (incendiarism, without a doubt).
Very suddenly an improvised lorry was placed in
front of. “Mo.’s'” premises. The door was burst
open, and willing hands quickly piled the safe on
the lorry, which then went off down Plank Road.
Interested inquirers, on arrival at the raging fire,
were informed that “ Mo.’s ” safe was rescued
and all right. Gradually volunteers retired from push-
ing the lorry, until the “ promoters ” were left in full
charge. Then, turning into the scrub, Eureka way, the
weight of the safe broke the back of the lorry in crossing
a water channel. That was as bad as dropping down a
600 ft. shaft, and so the safe had to be left, and was found
intact early next morning. Years after, meeting one of
the gang in Sydney, I asked him why he kept so close to
the drink. He said he dated his downfall from the time
that wretched gutter got in the way’ on the Plank Road
at Ballarat. ;
That lately-mentioned fraud, the German
razor, with “ Price 10s. 6d.” printed in gold on the case,
has turned up in a Melbourne Police Court. It was
sworn that the fraud can be purchased for 18s. a dozen,
and is dear at the price. The “ Price 10s. 6d.” is ob-
viously put on to help the swindling trader. The Com-
merce Act is, presumably, keeping its best eye on this
remarkable German razor.
“ Mallarapoonie ” tells of the undertakery
business in the back-blocks :—“ Like some recent Bul-
letin contributors, I’ve done a little bit of a lot of things
in my time; sometimes the middle of things ; sometimes
the two ends; but mostly the tail end. The worst failure
of my life was as an undertaker. I only undertook one
funeral—that of a Chow in western N.S.W. The planting
was accomplished successfully enough ; it was the K.i.r.
process that was a fiasco, and any undertaker who can t
guarantee the corpse eternal R.I.P. for at least seven clear
days is a flat, monotonous failure, and a distinct Diignt
on an honorable profession. . .
“|Ah Pong, a scrub-cutter, was suddenly called hence,
and for some religious reason his mates flatly refused to
bury him, or even approach the corpse. They reported
the occurrence to the manager, with gesticulations,
hinting that the defunct was no gentleman, and not a
friend of theirs.
“ As the nearest police were four days journey away,
the boss, as J.P., ordered a burial, and told off Bill, the
carpenter, and self to conduct it with all due respect o
the deceased. We suspected some infectious disease to
be the root of his mates’ objections to bury him, thougn
they assured us not. Anyhow, we took no chances, and
Bill, with a long-handled shovel, and self, with a gum
sapling, handled the remains as gently as might he into
the flimsy coffin that Bill had constructed out of kerosene
cases. He didn’t seem to fit too well, though Bill re-
marked that it fitted him all over and touched him in
only three or four places. However, we packed up the
crevices with items of his property that the other Chows
vehemently insisted upon having buried with him, and
made him look comfortable enough. Then we _ loaded
him into a spring cart and started off to look for soit
ground. Finally we selected a site near a wire fence on
a box flat, but, after sinking 20 inches, we bottomed on
blue clay with a hard, dull thud. The day was awfully
hot. Bill got out of the hole and hooked the sweat off
his brow. He guessed that would do. I guessed so
too : so we settled it at that, and, though the coffin-lid
was only 7in. below the surface, we banked it up well
with loose earth and ashes from a place where a stump
had burnt out. ‘What about a bit o’ fencin ? 1 sug-
gested. ‘ Blow the fencin’,’ quoth Bill, and we went on
our way rejoicing. .... *
“Next day a pedigree bull conducted his harem to
that locality what time an old ‘hatter’ from the herd
happened along outside the fence. Now, anyone knows
that if you produce two bulls, with one barbed-
wire fence between, and a patch of soft earth
on either side, things are liable to happen. What
happened was that Royal Duke XIV. got down on
his marrow bones and inserted a horn into that
new mound with grievous results. Among other things,
besides much dust and bovine cusses, that were hurled
at the intruder across the fence, were one small bag of
rice, one coffin lid, one packet of tea, two bundles of Joss-
sticks, ditto of crackers, one jar of preserved ginger, one
string of Chinese coins, some silk handkerchiefs, assorted
wearing* apparel, a blanket, a battered Mongolian hat
everything, in fact, except the corpse itself, which was
only saved by having its pigtail jammed in a crack of the
coffin floor.”
* * * *
“ The Gunyah ” : Despite Bulletin’s continual
teaching, could not, until a week ago, erase from my
brain-box the idea that Federation was a curse to Aus-
tralia, and N.S. Wales in particular. Being young, and
a daily reader of the N.S.W. Press, thought it was only
established to provide “fat men with fat billets.” But
have just lately had that delusion removed from my
noddle, and the blessing part brought really home. Some
six years ago, I invented, afLer years of constant study and
experiment, an acetylene gas generator. It cost me
close on £l5O to patent in six different States
(got about £5OO out of same). At present time, I
have another idea, but, thanks to Federation, will be
able to patent the same in all the States for £l3,
payable on the instalment system. Enough to make
one give up reading Sydney dailies. By the way,
to show the up-to-dateness of the press: In its “ Will You
Tell Me ” column, the World's Scissors, week after week,
answers “ Constant Reader,” “Pro Bono Publico,” &c.,
re patents, trade marks and copyrights, with the same old
stereotyped par : “ You seem to have a good idea ; write
to Patents Office, Chancery-square, Sydney.” Now, the
N.S.W. Patents Office left C.S. for Phillip-street in ’99.
So that “Will You Tell Me” column is a trifle behind
time—just eight years behind.
THE VISITOR.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
He oame unheralded, alone.
The blinking scars that swam in space
To light the portals of the Throne
Beheld his form and saw his face.
Not I, who, deep in dreams of love,
Did kiss my girl on lips and eyes,
And for her brow gay garlands wove,
The while we roamed in Paradise ;
Not I, indeed 1 Had I but dreamed
Of baser deeds, and seen him there,
While on my face the pale light gleamed,
And cast strange shadows on my hair,
In bitterness and passioned might
From my lone couch I would have sprung
And questioned him, and given fight,
While on the air my voice had rung.
Alas for me ! When I arose
To speak with whirling eloquence,
To feel the pockets of my clothes,
My watch and all my affluence
Had vanished quite. And after him—
When burglar, watch, and coin had flown-
With much ado and visage grim,
The Sergeant went on feet of stone.
TAS.
THE CHOST.
EBENEZER PERKINS.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
When Ebenezer Perkins came along to Tilberoo,
He was taking life proposals for the well-known 1.0. U.
His air of modest dignity,
Combined with mild benignity,
Convinced us that he was a man to whom respect was
due.
From our little township carnivals he strictly held aloof,
For he looked on ; worldly pleasures with a visage of re-
proof.
In words of simple piety,
He showed that inebriety
Would place us, bound and helpless, underneath the
cloven hoof.
We gathered Mr. Perkins to our warm and trusting hearts,
For he seemed a chosen vessel, and appeared a man of
parts.
He gave no weakly latitude,
But took the sternest attitude,
And pierced our sinful failings with religion’s keenest
darts. ,
We must have been exempted in a manner that is rare,
From physical infirmities to which our flesh is heir ;
And though we were not alien
To habits Bacchanalian,
We passed as sound and sober men by Ebenezer’s care.
Then our hearts beat high and happy in that town of
Tilberoo,
When we handed in our premiums to join the 1.0. U.
We quivered with anxiety
To join that great society,
And pinned our faith on Perkins as a man we thought we
knew.
When Ehenezer Perkins wended homeward on his way,
We shed the tear of sentiment, and begged him hard to
stay;
We pointed out that vanity,
The curse of poor humanity,
Would wreck our new-found morals, for we were but
human clay.
His arm was slow uplifted, and it pointed in the air,
As if to tell our citizens to look for comfort there ;
And words, too deep for uttering,
We saw his lips were muttering—
’Twas Ebenezer Perkins wafting up a final prayer.
Since Ebenezer Perkins left the town of Tilberoo
Our trust and moral rectitude have disappeared from
view.
The 1.0. U. Society
And Ebenezer’s piety
Were figments of his fancy which have vanished like the
dew.
Now we carry lethal weapons ever ready to our hand
For the conscientious stranger, with a manner smooth
and bland,
Who reproaches our impiety
And frowns on insobriety.
We will argue with that stranger in a way he’ll under-
stand.
B.A.
DEXTER.


MICK SIMMONS
HaymarKet, Sydney, N.vS.W.

Letters about
Liver Complaint
From Mr. J. Edwards, Davey-street, East Geelong, Vic., 31st August 1905.
“ For about twelve months I had been ailing from aggravated liver complaint, producing indi-
gestion, biliousness, a heavy feeling in the chest and frequent pains in the shoulder blades. I tried
the usual purgative remedies, which had the effect of only temporarily improving my condition. I
could get no permanent relief until I took Warner’s Safe Cure. lam pleased to say that the good
effect of that medicine became apparent almost immediately I commenced to take it, and in a short
time I was thoroughly cured. I have recommended Warner’s Safe Cure to many of my friends, whc
all speak well of it.”
From Mrs. K. Chapman, 150 Paddington-street, Sydney, N.S.W., Bth February, 1906.
“ For a considerable time I was a sufferer from liver complaint accompanied by severe pains in the
back, depression of spirits and loss of appetite. I tried anything I thought would do me good, but
obtained no relief whatever. I happened to read one of your books giving accounts of wonderful
cures Warner’s Safe Cure has effected, and therefore decided to try its effect on myself. The result
was marvellous, as in a short space of time I was completely restored to good health, and, I am glad
to say, I have not been troubled with pains of any description since.”
From Mr. Gladstone Mayne, 22 Parade, Norwood, S.A., 14tli December, 1905.
“For several years past I suffered much pain from symptoms of liver complaint, such as bilious-
ness, indications denoting congestion, heavy pain in the right side, and a dull persistent pain under
the right shoulder blade. I was also affected with dizziness, headaches and loss of sleep. I resorted
for relief to a number of medicines, but did not obtain any good result until I began to take Warner’s
Safe Cure. 1 took only a few bottles of that medicine when all painful symptoms subsided. During
the last twelve months I have not felt anything of the old complaint. Being fully convinced that 1
am permanently cured, I am truly grateful.”
From Miss Annie Leleman, Zabina-street, off Thomas-street, North Broken Hill, N.S.W., 28th
November, 1905.
“ I have much pleasure in testifying as to the efficacy of Warner’s Safe Cure, haying taken that
medicine when suffering from functional derangement of the liver. I was troubled with severe pains
in the back, sides and head, with constant dizziness. Indigestion troubled me a good deal and caused
me many sleepless nights, whilst exercise of a.nv kind brought on fatigue. Warner’s Safe Cure was
brought under my notice. I procured two bottles of the medicine, and after taking the second bottle
I found a great improvement had taken place in my health. All aches and pains had gradually
ceased to trouble me. I could eat without distress, and get refreshing sleep.”
From Mr. A. McEwan, 6 Princes Place, Darlinghurst, N.S.W., 15th September, 1905.
“ I suffered for months from liver complaint, which caused dizziness in my head and a languid
tired feeling. I also had severe pains in my side and back. I tried many medicines to obtain relief,
but without effect. I was advised to try Warner’s Safe Cure by a friend of mine, and it is a good
thing that I took his advice, as, after taking about four bottles of Warner’s Safe Cure, I was
thoroughly cured.”
