Ballad of the Elusive Keyhole.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
THIS is not a pious ballad for the people
who are pallid—
It is not a mournful mumble for the rising
cherubim ;
’Tis a story glad and frisky, made for folk who
browse on whisky—
For the band of keyhole-seekers when the dawn
is breaking dim!
Have you ever chased the keyhole?—ah, that
slick, elusive wee hole —
How it dodges in the darkness; how it skips to
left and right;
How you grind your teeth and grumble, as you
fossick and you fumble—
As you chase the ribald keyhole in the dear,
dead hours of night!
For the keyhole is a caitiff, and the body legisla-
tive
Should prepare a stringent measure to amend
its wicked ways ;
They should pass a Bill instanter, warning key-
holes not to canter,
Nor to gallop, nor to gambol, nor to impudently
gaze!
For I’ve heard the keyhole giggle when, with
quick, avoiding wriggle,
It has left me flabbergasted at the carnal hour
of three !
And upon the mat I’m sitting, whilst the scampish
keyhole, flitting,
Tells its shameful comrade keyholes of the joke
it’s played on me !
Aye, when midnight tolls, the keyhole doth be-
come a cheerful free hole.
And it meets its naughty brethren at the corner
of the street;
There they gossip, talking scandal, while I fumble
at the handle,
And with stick, and fist, and knocker, impo-
tently bang and beat!
I demand the swift suppression of this impish
keyhole-session—
Aye, I make my earnest protest from the vant-
age of the mat;
Here am I, locked out and lonely, with the steps
for refuge only,
And regarded with suspicion by the dim
domestic cat!
There! the key is lost completely—as I fumbled
indiscreetly,
Some infernal vampire snatched it from my
oscillating hand ;
I shall sleep upon the mat now, I shall cease my
rat-tat-tat now—
I shall slumber on the doorstep, and be wrapped
in visions bland !
* * * *
Ah, the dawn is creeping over ; now the sun, like
any lover,
Weaves its couch in halls of darkness, whe.e it
bedded with a star,
Now the bad and wicked keyhole is no more a
vagrant free hole —
Now I’m serious and sober, for the world-door
stands ajar!
I have found my missing key now, and the key-
hole winks at me now,
And pretends it’s been a fixture since the sun
went down last night;
But I know that it is lying by the hours I spent
in trying
To locate the impish fissure as I fumbled left
and right.
Have you ever chased the keyhole ?—ah, that slick,
elusive wee hole—
How it dodges in the darkness, how it skips to
right and left;
How you grind your teeth and grumble, as you
fossick and you fumble—
As the keyhole sprites enmesh you in their
most malicious weft!
But a time is coming, brothers, when this woe
shall join the others—
When no more shall missing keyholes make bon
vivants curse and frown ;
For our proud enlightened nation shall invest in
legislation
That shall make our lazy landlords chain all
frisky keyholes down !
VIC.
GRANT HERVEY.
The Wildernesses of Commerce.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
FATHER was not “ getting on,” save in
years. But he still dreamed dreams, and,
though forced to temporarily abandon his
plan of artificially filling the dry creek, and
thereon sailing pleasure boats at so much per
passenger, in favor of more expeditious schemes
for keeping the creditors at bay, he still nursed
the Great Idea. This, evolved by magic from
the purchase of a few strawberry plants, was to
turn his sterile acres into Recreation Grounds (by
name Gethsemane”). Already his prophetic
eye viewed the moneyed of Quondong thronging
his quaint mia-mia refreshment stalls, the girls,
now fine young women, spryly serving home-
grown strawberries with cream straight from the
“ coo,” and he himself reaping in the shekels.
Mother took a gloomy view of the Idea-she
bad seen the birth and death of so many like it.
.. ifL-f 8 ’ i 6 berr .i es plucked up to date and
stealthily absorbed while still in green iimnaturitv
seemed of a strangely malignant nature, as the
after convulsions of little Lige clearly proved. So
she put her foot down.
Mother was understood, in the family circle, to
have a business head, a reputation acquired bv
her having once detected a railway clerk in the
act of palming off upon her a bad sixpence. Her
talents were thrown away at the paddock. The
world of commerce called her as its own. Fearing
that Gran pa s last cheque would go underground
in the shape of almond trees (a grove of which
'! as t -, embe i t “Gethsemane”) she clutched
it tightly, and leaving Father in charge of the
thp W; ° Cks i ll , 1 a bu y er should come
over the horizon, and brother Pete in pawn at
the store, working off the bill as rouseabout, she
yarded up the rest of us and took train for Mel-
bourne. She had decided to go into business,
heedless of Dad’s call to wait till the strawberries
were grown. In the city sale of these berries he
already saw gigantic profits.
Mose, already a man about town, had been
charged in advance to find a suitable warehouse.
He was given a free hand, Mother only stipulating
that it be “ cheap, high and dry, near town, and
no stinks.” So Mose timorously interviewed the
agents. They brushed aside the stipulations with
a royal air, and, as a great favor, put him in
possession of a stand in which the last tenant had
realised “a little fortune.” It wore an air of
brooding melancholy, and was not blameless in
the matter of smells, and Mose was not favorably
impressed, but the agent was so pleased with
Mose’s luck in securing it that to damp his joy
would have been too cruel.
Competition was rife in the street of brooding
melancholy, but, as Mose explained later to
Mother (with a very fair imitation of the agent’s
large manner), that was no detriment. Rather it
was the life of trade—he had the agent’s word for
the fact.
Arrival at Spencer-street was a perilous busi-
ness. We had been solemnly sworn to cling to-
gether at all costs—to let go even for a moment
would mean kidnapping. Little Lige clung to
Mother tenaciously, and we to him. Strung to-
gether we threaded the maze of traffic, became
entangled in barriers, and were run over by
porters’ trucks. Mose met us, and quickly got us
out of the public gaze and into a cab. Thus we,
for the first time in our history, rode in something
less homely than a spring cart.
Mother bore up heroically against the melan-
choly shop and the street of desolation, but broke
down utterly at sight of the yard—a dismal,
squalid corner, no bigger than the hen-house at
home.
The shop interior, too, was a barrack. We
worked like slaves scrubbing it out, and swashing
its gauntness decently in newspapers laboriously
and fancifully; hacked out with blunt scissors,
quite unaware that paper was purchasable,
brightly tinted and cut by machinery, for almost
nothing per mile. At last Mother declared the
premises wholesome, and she and Joe went to
market for the stock.
Considering the outlay, the show was not great,
and even when every apple, wonderfully polished,
was made to fill the space of three, there were
dreary wastes of window unoccupied. Father
sent us the dairy produce. The butter was very
creditable—hot weather and lack of method being
considered.
Like the Swiss Family Robinson, each did a
share. Mary Ann, aged 14, was deputed to count
the eggs. Grovelling on the floor in the midst of
hen fruit, dazedly staring at grotesque hierogly-
phics on a list, she was not an inspiriting spec-
tacle. She was no arithmetician. Her totals and
Father’s never coincided. At the slightest inter-
ruption, behold Mary Ann abandoning hundreds
and starting again at egg No. 1. Meanwhile Joe,
creaking in blucher boots and sulky with tooth-
ache, was being coached in the art of serving
butter without leaving finger-marks on it, and
pat after pat was sacrificed in the process. The
amount he dropped on the floor was terrible.
Our home-printed cards, inscribed with ‘‘Oh,
look!” and “Only 6£d,” shakily done in red
letters amid a hail of black dots, we considered
marvels of artistic effect. Mose alone was dis-
satisfied. They weren’t ’‘the thing,” he said
gloomily, and “put the whole show away.”
Mother got badly, battered in the first day of busi-
ness in a tussle with shutters that were refractory
from long disuse, and had to take to her bed.
Still we were open long ere the public had
shuffled from its night attire.
Contrary to expectation, we had time for break-
fast. The public was clearly not an early riser.
Later on, school children lined the windows and
rubbed sticky fingers on the glass. They impu-
dently inquired prices, and deluged us with
unasked advice. The more venturesome even
gave Lige a run for his life, and robbed him of his
ginger-stick.
The day wore on, and Mary Ann, poised ready
for action behind the counter, looked pathetically
at the passing herd that always passed. And she
poised similarly the next day and the next, and
not a coin crossed the counter.
Mother began to fret, and Joe got the hump.
We re-polished the apples and kept the butter
with us by strategy, and when at last a buyer did
come, Mary Ann (who had been early taught that
honesty was the best policy) “ could not recom-
mend it; only fit for pastry,” she said, conscien-
tiously. And she smiled a wan smile as the
customer, sniffing fastidiously-, sailed away.
A loud knocking after closing time announced
another customer. He was an imperative person,
but we were very cute—we were not going to be
fined for trading after hours, and we disregarded
his thunderous appeal. We were presently dis-
turbed by loud sobbing in the lane and the appear-
ance of a grimy woe-begon6 figure over the fence.
It was brother Pete, escaped from pawn and
fleeing to the bosom of his family. About this
time Mother took “ the shivers” and had to go off
duty.
As locum tenens muddle-headed Mary Ann ran
the whole gamut of imposition. No confidence
trick was too stale to take her in. She became quite
an adept at exchanging good money for bad. or
absent-mindedly neglecting to deduct the price of
the purchase, and her honesty was worse than ever.
Then the Quondong fowls went on strike. We
bought, but could not sell, other eggs which used
to explode with loud reports, Charging the air
with odors suggestive of gunpowder and bone-
dust. And through force of circumstances, the
produce department gradually died. In fact, it
had never lived.
There was nothing: narrow about Mother. She
decided to add other branches of commerce-
drapery, fancy goods, and what not. And in
these varied lines we soon became fair game for
every tinpot canvasser whose brow was brass and
whose hands were the claws of Beelzebub. Mother
took the lying assurance of one concerning the
public’s screaming necessity for gaudy little
aprons and rubbishy blouses. It was the fag-
end of summer, but she. bought heavily. This
same Ananias it was who persuaded her to take
the roll of serge, reputed to be “ Fox’s.” He got
it under the rose—hence its cheapness. Could
he rely on Mother’s integrity to keep the matter
secret ? No, nothing dishonest about it, he
assured her; all fair and square—merely a good
business deal. The serge cannot be said to have
been an entire swindle, as, warped and spotted,
portions of it, in the shape of half-mast trousers,
cover Father’s legs to this day.
We persevered, but somehow the new “lines”
didn’t take. Certainly one of the gaudy aprons
was bought by a half-witted girl with a stolen
shilling, over which transaction, by some twist of
law. Mother narrowly escaped 14 days.
Then a sneak thief stole the till, and Mother
gave up. Home-sick, forgetting the recurring
worries of the paddock, the heart-breaking chase
after Billy the steer, the everlasting job of fowls’
food, the daily trudge for the cows, and the mad-
dening optimism of Father, Quondong loomed in
the distance like a Paradise. To the sound of
the explosion of the last eggs and the smell of
ancient vegetables she gloomily reviewed the
situation, and turned her back on Commerce.
The fiat went forth amid general rejoicing.
Even little Lige speculated fondly on the fate of
certain yabbies he had left in a jam-tin. Mary
Ann hastened to prepare our last meal in the
city, and went peering in the shop-window for
any remaining edibles, and such was the joy of
Pete that he seized her by the ankles from be-
hind, and turned her into a human wheel-barrow,
her legs being the handles and her arms the
wheels, and guided her skilfully through the re-
maining provisions and hanging papers. Crash-
ing over the cruets and hair-pads, Mary Ann
rioted confusedly in the window, and caused the
general public to take a real interest in the em-
porium for the first time.
In the midst of packing up a shaven stranger
entered. His boots were white with dust, and he
bore a glaring carpet bag. He advanced to
Mother, who seemed turned to stone, and grin-
ning broadly to hide other emotions, he kissed
her. It was Father without his whiskers, in buff-
colored clothes and a boxer. He beamed on all
affectionately till the situation was explained.
Then his jaw dropped.
“Going back!” he roared testily. “Going
back just as I see my way to turn this business
into a gold mine ! Going back ! Why, I sold the
tarnation place a week ago, an’ here’s the pro-
missory note.”

•j
Youth.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
I HAVE been told that I must grow old,
and become as the old men be,
With never a tooth in my gums forsooth,
nor a hair on the head of me !
With a palsied hand as the old man’s brand, and
a watery, red-rimmed eye,
On doddering crutch T shall mumble much of the
good old times gone by.
With shanks shrunk thin and a parchment skin,
and hollowed hand to my ear,
I shall greet the New with a weak “ Pooh, pooh !’
and an impotent, senile sneer.
My thoughts each one (with a staggering run)
shall circle this dreary theme —
The conquests made by the Old Brigade in the
days of the old regime.
With a pulse grown slack and a rounded back,
and blood in my veins grown cold,
I Shall gather the wage that waits on age— !
Ah, never shall I grow old !
For I’m young ! I’m young ! and my hopes are
strung in tune with the infinite.
So a fig for Age with its smirched page—MY
pages are clean and white !
They are clean and white for my hand to write
whatever my heart decree !
I shall be what I will! Let the stars stand still,
but I’ll fashion my destiny !
And fortune shall hold no gift of gold, no prize
of place or power,
But what she shall yield when I take the field in
the pride of my morning hour !
Yes, I have been told that I must grow old—
most surely the seer is wrong !
Grey age is as far as the star points are from me
and my world of song !
M.L.
SING. S. OLARK.
A Tale of the Sea.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
CLUMP POINT had been selected by
the Boss as the site of the next camp, but
the trouble was how to get there. The
roads were in a shocking state, and our three
packhorses were quite inadequate to the contract.
“ There’s nothing for it,’'said the Boss to the
Assistant-Surveyor, “ but for you to take the camp
gear round in the Enterprise. Tom can go with
you. You’ll be there early to-morrow morning,
and as it will be night before I arrive with the
men you’ll have the whole day to fix camp. We’ll
make a start now. S’long.”
Then the packhorses and the men, in the lightest
of light marching order, swung off through the
box trees and disappeared.
* * * *
The s.s. Enterprise was an unwieldy, untidy,
locally-built flat-bottomed craft of about 20 tons
burthen, and 2J indicated horse-power. In some
respects it was an interesting creation—a sort of
relic from the days of the .old merchant adven-
turers who made Old England’s name smell so
highly. The owner was captain, chief engineer,
supercargo and purser. The other fellow was
first mate, quarter-master, deck-hand, assistant
engineer, fireman and cook combined. These two
composed the entire list of officers and crew.
Early in the afternoon the Assistant-Surveyor
and Tom got the camp gear on board the Enter-
prise, after some argument with the purser, who
was very drunk, and an entire absence of any
assistance from the crew, who was far from
sober. The craft was due to sail, or at least to
crawl, but the long afternoon wore away and the
passengers saw no signs of departure. By 5 p.m.
the captain and chief engineer was helpless, and
the crew was stating emphatically that the ship
would not leave till 8 o’clock next morning.
Having made this announcement the crew retired
to the innards of the ship, and the Assistant-Sur-
veyor and Tom took turns at cursing.
The next morning found the captain suffering a
recovery. To steady his nerves he had to
resort to sundry hairs of the dog, and he kept
resorting till the alarming baldness of the dog
must have been noticeable. When sufficiently
braced up to attend to business, he discovered
that the tide was quite unsuitable, and went
ashore to indulge in walking exercise to expedite
his recovery.
When the crew appeared the tide was favorable,
but the captain was missing, and it was not till 6
p.m. that he was discovered and brought on board
in a partially-sober state. The process of weigh-
ing anchor was interrupted by a slight unplea-
santness with the crew, and the captain chased
that large and influential body of men round the
deck with a spanner. While discipline was being
restored the Assistant-Surveyor and Tom hid
behind some deck cargo and awaited develop-
ments. No bloodshed occurred ; the winded
captain gave up the pursuit, and announced his
intention of going ashore to get some whisky to
pull him together after the exertion. This
brought the Assistant-Surveyor from his lair to
remonstrate in vigorous language against any
further delay. The crew chipped in. The cap-
tain offered to fight the A.-S. and crew combined,
and their marked disinclination to meet him
moved him to cheerfully offer to do it with one
hand tied behind his back. Their continued
refusal disgusted him. He found it hard to ex-
press his feelings, and concluded by remarking
that a man should be very careful about the
selection of his ancestors.
Having completed his observations, the captain
struggled on shore. After a while, he returned
with a supply of whisky, and shouted the order
to let go, and fell downstairs, and struck some
wood under the boiler. From his place on the
wood under the boiler he called to the quarter-
master to take charge of the wheel. The A.-S.
lifted up his voice in rejoicing at having at iast
got a start.
The Heads were cleared shortly after dark, and
once the Enterprise was over the bar the captain
Went to sleep hugging the bottle of whisky. He
resolutely refused to be awakened. So the A.-S.
took the wheel while the crew went down and
looked after the engine. Tom mooned round and
expressed doubts about ever seeing the missus
and the kids again.
Even the buoyant A.-S. felt depressed. As the
ttight wore on a cold, dispiriting breeze came
moaning over the sea, and the steamer began to
roll slightly. The A.-S. kept her on what he
believed to be the course, judging by the coast-
line, and wondered if there were any jutting
reefs. The waves moaned louder, and the A.-S.
saw with an uneasy feeling that white manes
began to appear on the top of the water rather
frequently. In the midst of this he suddenly
became aware that the ship was not answering
its helm too well. It also occurred to him that
the thud of the engine was slow and irregular.
.Letting the wheel revolve to a straight rudder
he hurried below. The dull glow from the open
furnace door was the only light in the place—and
it was very dim. The crew lay in a heap in front
of the fire-box, fast asleep. The captain was
there also, and in the same state. The A.-S. felt
vexed, and smote the crew with his foot. The
crew half woke up, but was mutinous, and reso-
lutely declined to put another adjective stick into
the carmine fire. “ Let the lurid something
captain fire the ruddy adjective engine—his
immoral troubles! ”
The A.-S. thought hard for a moment, and then
hurried on deck to offer the billet of chief engi-
neer to Tom. After an anxious search, Tom was
discovered groaning up near the nozzle of the
boat, hopelessly sea-sick. He was too ill to be
interested in anything; all he wanted was to be
left alone to die in peace. He wouldn’t fire any-
thing, and would be delighted if the ship would
sink. Then he grew reckless, and proposed to
scuttle her to escape his misery.
The A.-S. tried to keep a tight grip on himself,
but he felt that if he stayed there he would lose
it. That sea-sickness seemed decidedly catching,
and, holding his waistcoat, the A.-S. fled to the
other end of the deck. As he did, the steamer
got into the trough, and, rolling horribly, took
great green seas aboard, which caused Tom to
howl dismally.
Mad with rage and fear, the A.-S. rushed down
into the stokehold again. Was he to be drowned
like a rat on account of these drunken brutes?
Not much ! Next moment found him kicking
the sleeping captain with all his heart and both
feet. The mariner scrambled to his feet and
used languagethat scorched the sides of the ship.
But the A.-S. was thoroughly roused, and he fell
on the captain with a lump of wood, and jammed
it into the middle of his waistcoat as a terrific
lurch threw him on top of the ancient and dis-
respected commander.
“Will you fire up and navigate the dashed
steamer, damn you ?” he yelled, smiting the cap-
tain across the midriff as the back roll of the ship
helped him to his feet. “Will you?” he roared
louder than ever, bringing the wood down on the
captain’s guard and arresting an uppercut in mid-
air. “Yes, you fool!” shrieked the captain, as
the timber nearly broke the right arm with which
he tried to hook the A.-S. “ Will you ? ” bellowed
the A.-S. suddenly falling on him as the ship
rolled once more, and kicking him savagely with
both feet as they went down together.
The captain dived for some wood to put under
the boiler. The infuriated A.-S. thought he
meant to use it for purposes of offence, and he
brought the timber down once more, this time over
the captain’s neck. The officer howled for mercy,
and the A.-S. desisting, once more scrambled on
deck and took the wheel.
Presently the engine again started, and the
ship’s head came up to the seas. The A.-S. was
beginning to feel safer, when sounds of a savage
altercation between the captain and crew smote
his ears. The crew was requesting the captain to
go to Sheol and not disturb his slumbers, but his
remarks were cut short by a series of heavy thuds,
followed by silence. The A.-S. concluded that the
crew was dead, and remarked that it was a good
thing.
For two hours all was peace, and the A.-S.
steered on, dozing at his post. He was suddenly
awakened by another stoppage of the engine, and
once more he went below. This time he smote
the crew hard with a lump of wood, and, after a
good deal of profanity, he induced it to take the
wheel. The captain declined to budge, so the
A.-S. fired up, and then lay down on the deck for
a brief nap.
* * * *
The A.-S. awoke with a start. It was morning;
clear, fresh, and divinely calm. He staggered to
his feet and stared round. The engines were still
throbbing. The crew was at the wheel, fast
asleep. Then the A.-S. looked across the water,
and found the gallant Enterprise proudly bear-
ing down on a solid rock.
His hair flew upright.
“Hi, there ! Let go yer afterspring—back
water, reverse or what the devil you call it. Oh,
you blanketv fool of a blank. Let go an anchor
or something. Tom, cap’n, steersman! For
Gawd’s sake turn the damn ship ! ”
The crew who was leaning, fast asleep, against
the wheel, heard the frantic yells and instinctively
ported his helm, thus saving the situation. But
it was the closest of close shaves. The crew
glared at the rock, and when he got control of
his voice let fly a magnificent burst of language.
The A.-9. drew a deep breath and raved. Even
the limp and dispirited Tom came from the
nozzle of the ship, where he had fallen asleep when
the calm happened along, and joined in the roar
of vituperation. The mingled cursing and disre-
spectful mention of his name and office roused
the captain, who promptly came on deck to vindi-
cate himself.
But the sight of that frowning rock face struck
him dumb, and, thoroughly seared and awakened,
he went below and stoked up with great vigor,
The crew hoisted a bucket of water over the side,
and, washing his face and hands, set to work to
prepare breakfast, while the A.-S. took charge of
the wheel once more.
* * * ■*
Somewhere about 10 a.m. the Enterprise crept
to the moorings at Clump Point. The captain
and crew had finished the bottle of whisky be-
tween them, and had cjuite recovered their spirits
by the time their destination was in view. The
Boss came on board, and with a cold, sarcastic
voice asked if all hands were alive.
The captain resented his manner, and cheer-
fully offered to fight him or any other chain-
lugger for a fiver. The Boss glared, and told him
he had a good mind to commence an action
against him for delay, loss of time, and depriva-
tion of employees’ services.
The skipper waxed eloquent, and, prancing
round, offered to slay the whole survey camp
with both hands tied. The camp ignored him,
and, starting to unload the gear, threw tents and
swags round him, while the camp oven rolled
over his feet.
Noticing that the crew was rather enjoying his
discomfiture, the skipper struggled across to him,
and invited him on shore for three rounds, just to
decide, first, what he was grinning at, and, second,
who really was the blanky boss.
The argument raged all over the foreshore while
the gear was being taken ashore, and the last
that the camp saw of the Enterprise and its
complement was the captain thumping the ground
with both hands, while the crew lay underneath
holding his officer’s legs with his own two arms,
and thereby keeping out of harm’s way.
FLABBY PAUL.
